The Red Page
Strindberg
From that groat Swedish reservoir, named
August Strindberg, more water continues to
flow into English literature through the
spillway of translation. A collection of 20
stories of married life, titled Married
(Palmer, per Dymock’s Library), would
make for tfye average, and not too squeamish,
reader an admirable introduction to the mes-
sage of this giant. He has the same com-
mand of the short story as he has of the
drama; but the difference between his drama
and the ordinary English published drama,
giving to the English reader a sense of strain,
is lacking in nis short stories. Here he is
at ease in an art with which we are more
familiar. Shaw’s Getting Married was
a brilliant play of ideas, some of which we
suspect were purloined from Strindberg;
Well’s Marriage was a sincere working-out’
of a modern problem, but that problem was
an isolated and an individual one; his char-
acters were not ordinary persons, nor were
they set in an ordinary environment. Hence
his conclusion could not be accepted as a
text for general use. Strindberg is here
concerned with marriage, not in one, but in
2C aspects: he knows the symptoms of
every modern variety of the disease. While
the psychology of Wells hesitates a little in
fear of hurting, and that of Shaw seems but
an excuse for scintillating paradox, the psy-
chology of Strindberg probes unhesitatingly,
even savagely, deep into vitalities. Yet
these brilliant short stories are not, as
might have been expected, mere vents for his
misognism. The writer was not all bitter-
ness and cynicism. An incurable optimism
often asserts itself; his humor is not
always a thing that stabs. Some of these
stories are delightful, full of fun and pleas-
ant raillery. Thus he concludes his story of
a financially unwise marriage with the
words: —
But how people can get married and bring
up a family when they have not enough for
themselves while they are. bachelors, is a
riddle to me. It almost seems as if babies
brought their food with them when they
came into this world ; it reallv does look as
if they did.
Equally charming is the conclusion of the
story about a husband and wife who were
both getting wages. The wife insists on
continuing her employment as a clerk, in
order that she may retain her feeling that
she is not economically dependent upon her
husband. But babies come, and with them
the end of her long struggle against nature.
She resigns her clerkship.
“I am your slave, now,” she groaned,
when she came home with her discharge.
Nevertheless she is the head of the house,
and he gives her every pennv he earns.
When he wants to buy a cigar he makes a
long speech before he ventures to ask for
the money. She never refuses it to him, but
all the same he finds the asking for it un-
pleasant. He is allowed to attend meetings,
but no dinners, and all botanising with girls
is strictly forbidden. He does not miss it
much, for he prefers playing with his chil-
dren. His colleagues call him henpecked;
but he smiles, and tells them he is happy in
spyte of it, because he has in his wife a very
sweet and sensible companion.
She. on her part, obstinately maintains
that she is nothing but his slave, whatever
he might say to the contrary. It is her one
comfort, poor little woman !
Included in this volume is Strindberg’s
famous one-act drama, Creditors, in which
the “Strindberg woman,” half allurement,
half deviltry, plays with the happiness of
two mep, her husband and her divorced hus-
band. It would be-horrible on the stage.
J*
The Inferno (Rider) is undiluted Strind-
berg. It is a personal narrative of that
painful period he went through when his
brain hovered on the edge of madness —-an
experience from which he almost miracu-
lously recovered, to amaze- —with a tremen-
dous outpouring of novels, stories and
dramas —a world that had reckoned him
dead. •
His four autobiographical works, The Son
of a Servant, The Confessions of a Fool
[already noticed on this pageß Inferno and
Legends, are four segments of an immense
curve tracing his progress from the childish
pietism of his early yqars, through a period
of atheism and rebellion, to the sombre faith
in a “God that punishes” of the sexagen-
arian. In his spiritual wanderings he grazed
the edge of madness, and madmen often see
deeper into things than ordinary folk.
The record opens with Strindberg’s part-
ing with his second wife, “my beautiful jail-
keeper, who watched my soul day and night,
guessed my secret thoughts, marked the
(course of my ideas, and was jealous of my
investigations into the unknown.” He parts
from her with a feeling of wild joy, and with
the inner decision never to see her again.
A play of his had just been produced in
Paris, but now Strindberg hated the theatre.
He returned to his poor student’s room in the
Batin Quarter, and proceeded to experiment
in chemistry. He made one discovery—that
sulphur is not an elementary substance, but
contains carbon, and already feels that he
has “won the immortality grudged to mor-
tals.”
Already he was close to madness; and the
remainder of the book contains the prognosis
of his mental disease. He sees signs and
omens everywhere, buys a Catholic prayer-
book, reads Buddhistic books, believes he
can exercise telepathic influence, investi-
gates and, thinks lie practises witchcraft,
reads the Book of Job, and “arrives at an
ever clearer conviction that the Eternal has
handed me over to Satan to be tried,” be-
lieves that he can make gold, "and gradually
becomes possessed of a firm belief that un-
known human or supernatural, are
persecuting bird. This persecution mania
takes ultimately the form of a conviction
that electric currents are being passed
through his' body every nig;ht. preventing
him from sleeping and reducing him to a state
of pitiable terror. Every new room he occupies
he explores for hidden electric, machines,
finds them in the room just above him, and
triumphantly notes trivial coincidences as
proof of his persecution. He is convinced
there exists a plot td kill him. He finds
evidence in the playing of a piano, the find-
ing of a piece of paper with strange writing
on it, the behavior of friends, the manner
of his neighbors, nightmares, an American
bed, “with its four legs topped by four brass
balls, which look like the conductors of an
electric machine. Add to this an ela’stic
mattress with copper springs, resembling
Ruhmkorff induction coils, and one can easily
imagine my rage at this diabolical coinci-
dence.”
In order to assure myself that nothing is
concealed above me, I mount into the loft
overhead. There is only one object there,
but it drives ine almost to desperation. An
enormous wire-net rolled together stands
immediately over my bed. One could not
wish for a better accumulator. If there is
a thunderstorm, the wire network will at-
tract the lightning, and I shall be lying on
the conductor. But I do not venture to say
a word.... Besides, it is impossible to ask
for another bed, as I might be suspected of
being mad... .Granted that I am suffering
from persecution-mania, but what smith
forges the links of these hellish syllogisms ?
Follows naturally the discovery that he is
in Hell. And in Swedenborg he finds a
fellow-soul.
Hell? But I have been brought up in the
profoundest contempt for the doctrine of
hell, as one consigned to the rubbish-heap of
outworn ideas. And yet I cannot deny the
fact that we are already in Hell. Earth,
earth is Hell —the dungeon appointed by a
superior power, in which I cannot move a
step without injuring the happiness of
others, and in which others cannot remain
happy without hurting me.
His tortures continile. Into the most ab-
surd happening he reads a diabolical mean-
ing. His mother-in-law offers him for
dinner calf’s head with brown butter, think-
ing it is his favorite dish. There is nothing
he dislikes more than calf’s head with
brown butter.
It is too much! Formerly I attributed
these annoyances to feminine malice ; now I
acquit everyone and say, “It is the Devil!”
Worse! His little daughter wants to
accompany him on his morning walks, dur-
ing which he was accustomed to plan out
his day’s work. But he has not the heart
to send her back. “What a penance—to
be loved!”. he cries. “Truly the powers are
not so cruel as we are!”
The next chapters are entitled, “Extracts
from the diary of a damned soul,” “The
Eternal has spoken,” “Hell let loose.”
Let us therefore suffer without hoping for
any real joy in life, for, my brothers, we are
in Hell. And do not let us accuse the Lord,
when we see our little, innocent children
suffer. No one knows why, but Divine jus-
tice gives us a ground for surmising that it
is on account of sins committed by them
before their birth. Let us rejoice in our
torments, as though they were the paying
off Of so many debts, and let us count it a
mercy that we do not know the real reason
why we are punished.
And Strindberg is led back towards
Catholicism; but he never actually entered
the Catholic Church. Surely a strange
Catholicism, this of Strindberg’s!
J*
In the serenity of his 59th year Strindberg
composed his last important work, The Blue
Book. He had hesitated to set to work
because of the greatness of the task: he in-
tended to compress in 365 brief essays, one
for each day in the year, his final conclu-
sions on life. But one day he saw a tram-
car with the number 365 on it, and other
equally impressive portents, including the
British flag, a ladV in a red dress, and a
sign in a chemist s shop; and he says: “It
was natural that all this should make an
impression on me.” The higher powers had
told him to proceed with his 365 apothegms!
This emancipated genius could see more in
a tram-car than most people. And The
Blue Book, whose identity is lost under the
title of the translation, Zones of the Spirit
(Allen, pen Dymock’s Library), is as child-
ish, as superstitious, and occasionally as
silly as the impulse that brought it into
being. Here we find, in his dotage, he can-
not shake himself free from his misogynism ;
many of his old hatreds survive; but he had
become a kind of Swedenborgian Christian.
He apologises for much of The Inferno : he
would be at peace with all men—even with
God —before he dies. But Strindberg in his
dotage is still Strindberg.—in patches. He
speaks of the huge humbug which is called
“youth”:
No one has dared to uumask it, for fear
lest the young might pelt him with stones, or
draw caricatures of him on the avails. The
teachers in the schools crouch before them,
flatter them, pretend to envy them. If any-
one comes who does not flatter these shame-
less and conscienceless little bandits, these
lewd apes who live in the age of innocence,
these parent-murderers- —there is always
some old woman there who exclaims, “Ah!
he does not understand the young!” He
understands them very well, for he has been
young himself. But the young do not under-
stand the old, for they have never been
old
But if one does re-attach himself to Chris-
tianity, one should take it without refining
—stock and barrel, dogmas and miracles.
One should swallow it uncritically, naivelv,
in great gulps, then it goes down like castor-
oil in hot coffee. “Open your mouth and
shut your eyes.” That is the only way. .. .
I am a Christian, i.e., I am a nobleman ;
I belong to the upper class; I have been
vaccinated ; I have served my time in the
army; I am a citizen, and of full age; I
am a white man; I have a clean birth-certifi-
cate : 1 am a superman. ...
All children are, in spite of idle talk,
wonder-children—till they have learnt to
talk. They understand all that we say
even when we hide it from them. They
seem to be thought-readers, divine our most
secret purposes, and rebuke us beforehand..
There are illnesseg in which one loses mem-
ory. May death prove to be such an ill-
ness !. . . .
One of my tasks has been to unmask
gynedlatry, the worship of women in his-
tory and life. Woman-worship is the re-
ligion of the heathen, but it is a religion of
fear,' which has npthing to do with Love.
Lust, hate and fear—those are the compo-
nent parts of it.....
There are several reasons why woman is
depicted as a sphinx by men. She
is incomprehensible, because her soul is
rudimentary, and she thinks with her body,
. . . . Why cannot one guess the riddle of this
sphinxV Because there is no riddle there!
.. . . Nevertheless we take her as a charming
actuality, a delightful child who may pull
three hairs out of our beard ; but if it pulls
the fourth, there is an end to the enchant-
ment. . . .
Strindberg could forgive God and man, but
he could not forgive woman.
Man loves and woman hates; man gives
and woman takes; man sacrifices, and *
woman devours....
I have said that the child is a little crim-
inal, incapable, of self-guidance, but I love
children, all the same. I have said that a
woman is—what she i#, but I have always
loved some woman, and been a father. Who-
ever, therefore, calls me a woman-hater is a
blockhead, a liar, or a noodle. Or all three
together.. . .
As soon as I believe in an Almighty God,
who can suspend the few natural laws which
we know, and bring into operation the count-
less host of laws which .we do not know, I
must believe in miracles....
It is among these degenerate brutes (apes)
that man (?) now does his best to seek his
ancestry. For my part I would rather trace
my origin from a noble horse, or a sagacious
and honest elephant, or from a courageous
and thankful eagle. But it is probable that
apes sprang from degenerate men, escaped
criminals and ship-wrecked Robinson Cru-
soes. The hand of the chimpanzee is not a
paw which is being evolved into a hand, but
a human hand which is degenerating into a
paw.
A strange book, compact of mysticism,
superstition, religion, piety, hatred, jealbusy,
cynicism and optimism—the essential Strind-
berg.
Fiction
Gilbert Cannan lias written a novel of
deliberate, but delightful, dulness. Round
the Corner (Bell, per Dymoek’s Library) is
a prolix narrative of the life and death of a
clergyman, and of the lives of his large
family. That is all. All the members of
this large family merely exist; for them
Life is always “round the corner.” The
theme of this story is that life is merely an
untidy mess, and the world the garbage tin
into which it is hastily stuffed. This kindly,
foolish, muddling clergyman begets a large
family of human beings, “produced quite
legitimately after due notice given,” only to
find that one after another the world re-
jected them, or at any rate refused to pro-
vide the males with worthy work or the
females with husbands. Yet most of them
manage to muddle along somehow. Only
one of the sons, himself a failure as an
artist, discovers that: “A man’s life consists
of only two things, work and love,” and asks
humanity to concentrate on the instinct of
hunger and the instinct of reproduction and
organise for the satisfaction of both. The
surprising thing about this dull catalogue
of the fortunes and ill-fortunes of the par-
son’s large family is that it is extremely
interesting; and one feels at the end that
the people are—what so few people ever
are, even in life —real. Round the Corner
is a noteworthy piece of work.

The work of Hugh Walpole shows a steadv
upward trend. His works include three
"Studies in Place,” The Wooden Hone
Maradick at Forty, and Mr. Perrin and Mr
Traill, two “Prologues to The Rising City”'
The Prelude to Adventure and Fortitude
And The Rising City is stated to be in pre-
paration. The Prelude to Adventure (Mills
and Boon, per Dymock’s Library), published
last year, is a remarkable novel. A Cam-
bridge student knocks down a man whom he
hates, and the blow kills him. And in that
moment in the wood, as the murderer stands
above the dead body, he is suddenly con-
vinced that there is a God. Dune, the mur-
derer, is not sorry that he has put out of
the way “a rotter,” but he is seriously
worried about God. He returns to college,
convinced that he will be arrested; but no
evidence is found to connect him with the
crime. Yet the strain of keeping his secret
grows almost past bearing ; again and again
the temptation comes to him to ease his soul
by confession. Slowly evidence "gainst him
accumulates in the hands of the brother of
the girl whom Dune loves. And God keeps
on nagging at him. Ultimately he makes
his confession to a foolish fellow-student,
and this, in turn, leads to his confession to
the brother and the girl he loves. And Dune
goes off to make good his position with God'
by service to the world, before he dare re-
turn tb claim the waiting girl. Thus out-
lined, the novel seems but a slice of sensa-
tional fiction ; but the plot is but a frame
for a subtle and deeply probing psychological
study. The tragic key of the book is mar-
vellously sustained, the characterisation is
brilliant, the style sombrely glowing. There
is one chapter, in which the tortured mur-
derer plays a football match —with an almost
visible God for referee—that is an achieve-
ment in literature.
Hugh Walpole’s Fortitude (Bell, per
Dymock’s Library) is a less brilliant but a
more solid piece of work. “ ’Tisn’t life that
matters. ’Tis the courage you bring to it.”
On this text Walpole preaches a finely con-
vincing sermon. Peter Destcott is a Corn-
ish youth who becomes in time a novelist of
one book. Beaten by a brutal father, and
sent to a dreadful boys’ school, Peter early
learns that the way to get through life with
the least harm is to get your back against a
wall and hit out. The chapters dealing
with the boy’s home and school life are ad-
mirably realistic—Walpole knows the school-
boy and the university man. Peter escapes
from Cornwall to bury himself for years in
a second-hand bookshop in London; and in
a Bloomsbury boarding-house he writes his
successful first novel. He marries the wrong
woman ; his work deteriorates ; his married
life comes to wreck ; his novels fail to sell;
and then the call of Cornwall comes and he
returns to his awful father to carry on the
Westcott tradition of brutality and vice.
He is saved by the right woman, but finds
her dying, and he returns to London to fight
his way back to life and art. Not to happi-
ness : he has learnt that happiness will
wreck his art.
As in his other story the style has a dig-
nity, and the structure of the plot is the'
structure of a living organism,. There are
chapters here that rival in poignancy that
of the remarkable football match, where the
ball was a murderer’s soul and the referee
Was God. These two “preludes” assuredly
herald a great achievement in the forth-
coming The Rising City. But already Hugh
Walpole has secured a definite place in the
small compay of the coming younger novel-
ists.

The first book-shop confined solely to the
selling of poetry has been opened in London.
The poetry shop is situated in Bloomsbury.

ss. for a set of verses. The conditions, advertised
on the “Red Page’’ of March 15, insist that tne
anthem shall consist of three six-line verses and a
four-line chorus (no line to contain more than eign
syllables). Anthems must be delivered by June 19.

The Inky Way.
Again the persistence of myth. The Sydney
Worker, “an Australian paper for Australian people,”
is apparently with the “great” dailies in the belief
that an 18th century English navigator was the first
European to sight “New Holland.” Anyhow, it prints
a par. beginning “when Mr. Cook claims to be, neces-
sarily, an Australian, because another Cook discov-
ered this continent,” and says nothing to question
Joe’s alleged historical geography.
* * * *
The dreadful superiority of these English! Lon-
don Observer has been talking of the “obvious re-
dundancy” of place-names in general and of Townsville
(Q.) in particular. With a patronising assumption
of superior knowledge, it implored its readers “not
to be hard upon the plucky pioneers, stronger in
brawn and brain than in book-learning, who invented
Townsville.” The prig hadn’t, of course, heard of
Captain Robert Towns, the founder of the place.
Still there are lots of things the Non-Observer
doesn’t know. In the same issue it had a par.
headed “The Australian Premier,” and beginning—-
“Mr. John Scaddan, Premier of Australia.” [Just
here it may be mentioned that a member of The
Bulletin staff once, on the trip from Launceston
to Melbourne, met an English teetotaller. It wasn’t
his fault; if he had known the teetotaller was on
board he might have waited for the next boat. And
the teetotaller lamented that the beautiful strait
should be named after a well-known brand of that
depraved brown fluid —beer. He had never heard of
Bass, the explorer.]
“C. L. Morgan”: “Jack Hardgraft ” i„
Bulletin was wrong in incluZ! v a rec, ’" t
the politicians who had graduated f J e ?J? er , amoil B
lie Sun. Vesper founded and edti th , e Kal Boor

after his death; but he was n,! Sun P*>ple
the latter paper himself. r COnnecte( ' with

poet, I cannot allow “O.K ’s” claim th°? n dea<i

“Digamma”: Herewith, as something of a literarv
curiosity, is William Cullen Bryant’s “Index Ki
Purfatonus,” referred to by “Quarter Single” (B
17/4/ 13). According to tl? N. Y. World Almanac’
when Bryant was editor vi the New York Evenikg
Post, the following list of words, which writers and
reporters on the paper were forbidden to use, was
posted in the editorial room. His son-in-law who
was afterward editor, Parke Godwin, in a letter to
the editor of The World Almanac, several years
ago, said: “Mr. Bryant’s Index grew up gradually
out of the bad habits of reporters, who in their
haste were apt to make use of words and phrases
that were offensive to a nicer taste. He did not
exclude forms of expression that were incorrect or
improper only, but others, that had become almost
nauseating as commonplaces or as slang. lie did
not like to see the vulgarisms of the street intro-
duced into the news:taper, lest they might make their
way into literature” :
Aspirant, authoress, “being” done, built, etc.,
bogus, bagging (for “capturing”), balance (for “re-
mainder”), collided, commenced (for “begun”),
considerable, couple (for “two”), debut, donate and
donation, employe, “Esq..” funeral obsequies (for
“obsequies”), gents (/for “gentlemen”), “Hon.,”
humbug, ignore, ilk, illy, inaugurated (for “begun”),
indorse (for “approve”), initiated (for “begun’ ’), in
our midst, jeopardize, jubilant (for “rejoicing”),
juvenile (for “boy”), lady (for “wife”), lengthy,
loafer, loan or loaned (for “lend” or “lent”), located,
measurably (for “in a measure”), minister (for
“preacher”), obituary (for “death”), ovation, pants
(for pantaloons”), parties (for “persons”), poetess,
portion (for “part”), posted (for “informed”),
predicate, progressing, quite (prefixed to “good,”
“large,” etc.), realized (for “obtained"), reliable
(for “trustworthy”), repudiate (for “reject” or “dis-
own”), retire (for “withdraw”), rOle (for “part”),
roughs, rowdies, states (for “says”), taboo, talented,
tapis, to progress, transpire (for “occur”), the de-
ceased, vicinity (for “neighborhood”), Wall-street
slang generally (“bulls,” “bears,” “long.” “short,”
etc.), would seem (for “is” or “appears.”)
Several of the expressions debarred are now accept-
able newspaper English, but “donate,” “transpire”
and “inaugurated” are still evil things which a gen-
eration of blue-pencilling has failed to remove.
• * * •
Next September another daily newspaper will
blossom in Christchurch (M.L.). That will provide
five dailies for a population of 80,000. They are
whales for newspapers in Maoriland. In a total
white populatioh of a million there are 238 on the
recister. Sixty-five are published daily, 34 tri-
weekly, 26 bi-weekly, 71 weekly, four fortnightly,
one three-weekly, and 37 monthly. Of the cities,
Auckland supports 24, Wellington 22, Christchurch
21 and Dunedin 11.
THE PRUDENT PRESSMAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Pressmen in Peril.—The other day, at the terri-
torial camp, in Otago (M.L.), a pressman, who had
excited resentment by his reports, was ducked. A
Wellington reporter at the Oringi camp published
details of a disturbance among the men, a large num-
ber of whom subsequently organised a hunt for him,
threatening more serious punishment than a ducking.
—News item.
The pressman bold went forth to see
The tame town-soldier shoot;
He sallied forth right fearlessly,
A cannon in each boot.
And, as he went his peaceful way
To battle, I believe,
lie carried, both by night and day,
A slingshot up his sleeve.
The pen is mightier than the gun,
As every pressman knows;
And so he took, for every one
Of these, a brace of those.
The sort of underwear he wore
Is seldom seen on sale
In any haberdasher’s store —
It was a shirt of mail.
He passed along, I understand,
Half-way his fate to meet,
With knuckledusters on each hand,
And hobnails on each feet.
Since he had just insured his life
He hummed a tune, though grim,
Wild scenes of stress and bitter strife
Were then awaiting him.
Armed to the teeth, he went to do
His daily work.... You see,
He recognised his duty to
His wife and family.
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Ballad of D.D.E.
Strength, and the beauty shown in strength, these
are the keys to our temple. . . .Characteristics purely
Australian will be developed by us naturally. Energy
is the capacity for labor. It is consistent with all
the robust v.rtues. It is the measure of the fulness
of life: the more energy, the more abundance of it;
no energy at all is death. Energy is the most im-
portant quality for which we must provide—energy,
the basis of living action. —“National Notes,” p. 77.
The world goes by with a valiant tread in
the forthright hours of morn,
And an anthem swings from the railroad-
bed —’tis a blast of primal scorn !
Each wheel-beat sings in a major tone of the
pulsing Years To Be,
When we raise on a Hod Australian throne
our God of Energy !
The swift express with her rolling cars is a
vibrant harp of steel,
That flings its psalm to the paling stars from
shaft and crank and wheel;
Let weaklings prate of an Eden lost —we
are marching thunder-shod.
And the blast of the engine’s fierce exhaust
goes up to the Throne of God ;
The D.D.E. is a Thing alive —a quivering
soul of fire
That speeds at dawn through this human
hive with a chaunt of strong desire!
We “gave her the lot,” and she laughed
again—her throttle was open wide:
“Yea, strength is the key to Australia’s
fane,” she sang in her mighty stride !
More strength ! More strength I —and the
beauty drawn from the effort that
reaches far”—
Her cylinders hymned at the gleaming dawn
as she mocked at the Things That Are !
“ Energy! Energy!—strength sublime!” she
crooned on the topmast grade ;
And the earth-pulse beats to that action-
rhyme as the sunrise splendors fade!
The fireman’s shovel swings swift once
more, and she rolls “all out.” Her tread
Is the tread of a fighting dinosaur with a
vast Cyclopean head !
The road-bed quivers beneath her thrust —
lo! fast in the forward notch
There’s a lever bites through the fine, black
dust to the oily ouadrant-croteh!
Her steam-gauge flutter's from pound to
pound, as she beats with an iron flail —
All the earth is filled with the rhythmic
sound that purrs from the steel-bright
rail!
Who hears the song that her pistons lift? —
wi,th the world and his sleeping wife
In the cars behind, she demands this gift:
Full measure of Fight and Life!
Full measure of strength! Is an engine’s
prayer not heard by the Glassy Sea? —
I can see God stand by His round-house
stair as He listens for D.D.E.!
The cripples have called—they call in vain !
’Tis Energy counts with Him,
When His Deoot receives each human train
by the heavenlv coal piles grim!
It isn’t the cut of your dungarees that mat
ters, but Did You Fight?—•
If you did, all’s well with the D.D.E.’s, and
all of the Lights are white !
Whilst weaklings quaver and failures quit.
God collars the section-staff;
And each fighting soul goes over the Pit
with an engine’s valve-deep laugh!
Does the world go by with a forthright tread
in your valiant hours of morn?
What anthem, swings from your railroad-
bed- —a whine or a blast of scorn?
Whose soul-beat sings in a major tone of the
pulsing Years To Be? — ,
Do you praise on a glad Australian throne
this God of Energy?
For the swift express with her rolling cars
is a vibrant harp of steel —
Have you flung your psalm to the paling
stars as she flings from each crank and
W fl (*,(>], f
Yea the D.D.E.'s send up to God the blast
of each fierce exhaust;
Let weaklings prate !—are you thunder-
shod? Is your Strength Retrieved or
Lost?
VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.
A Dream of Life.
A white dream came to me
Ont of the olden sea
When, veiled as a bride.
Young Death walked at my side
Sweet were his lips and fair.
As virgin gold his hair,
His crystal sandals clear
Left no black print of fear.
Through the still night he went,
The winds about him snent
Such fragrance as men knew
Whose first feet walked in dew.
I said : “Oh, balmy Death,
I reck not of scant breath,
Oh, take me. set me free
From Life’s lush mystery.
“Walk with me, let me wear
Thy garments of full air,
Blown on the days along
Like some clear, flowing song.
“The dead, the dead are wise,
They look with wider eyes ;
Untrammeled as the night,
They fear not God’s delight.
“Their larger dreams are spun
Of system, star and sun ;
Above earth’s voices rude
They walk in quietude.”
I went with Death a while ;
And. oh, his still, white smile
Was wonderful to me
Beside the olden sea.
But never word he said ,
A glory roupd his head
Shone as when youngest morn
Vanquished the dark forlorn.
And somewhere far and high
I heard a great voice cry:
“Roll ye the stone away ;
God liveth, it is day!”
N.S.W.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to Methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin/’’ is
the proper superscription for “copy." Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there ; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence" col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded with-
out stamps for return it will be understood that the
copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Personal
applications for return of MS. cannot receive atten-
tion. The Bulletin desires, further, those sending
MS. to distinctly understand that it is not respon-
sible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash, of
sufficient merit and interest and adapted for direct
reproduction, or any interesting photograph illus-
trative of a current event, will be paid for same on
acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea, but
unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MAY 22, 1913.
THE BLESSINGS OF MANUFACTURES
AND HIGH PROTECTION.
Two matters about which both Federal
political parties try to say as little as pos-
sible are the Northern Territory railway,
which is a massive item in the question of
Defence, and the need for a new tariff,
which is the biggest possible feature in the
problem of industrial expansion and the
growth of population. Both matters are
of greater urgency than even the Referenda,
but it happens that each party is a hash of
Protectionists and Freetraders, and each
party is liable to lose either in the
East or in the Centre and West, whichever
way the -Territory railway goes. So there
is a tendency to stick hard and fast to safe
subjects —matters regarding which one
crowd is solid on one side, and the other
gang is unanimous on the other side.^
The tariff question, however, is so buoy-
ant that all the dead weight of the party
system fails to keep it submerged. Lor
about a dozen years Australia has enjoyed
a certain measure of Protection —scant and
inefficient certainly, but still something to
go on with. The results have been so re-
markable that it is no longer possible to
doubt that manufactures are worth en-
couraging. Thus between 1901 and 1910
the value of the work done in the various
industries by which Australia makes its
bread and cheese is estimated as having
grown in this fashion: —
Value of Australian Production.
1901. 1911. Increase.
Manufacturing ..£27,191,000 £50,931,000 £23,740,000.
Pastoral .. .. 27,150,000 50,725,000 23,575,000
Agricultural .. 23,835,000 38,774,000 14,939,000
dairying (Cow, etc.) 9,740,000 19,107,000 9,307,000
Sundries .. .. 2,772,000 5,728,000 2,956,000
Mining .. .. 22,016,000 23,480,000 1,464,000
It is a good showing for manufactures,
and reveals the possibilities of the business
when it gets only a fraction of the
encouragement that is lavished on other in-
dustries. For the manufacturer, though he
receives some few scraps of aid, has always
been scurvily treated as compared with
many other “captains of industry.” He
pays far higher railway rates on his goocks
than the agriculturist, or the mine-owner,
or even the pastoralist. No Government
land is given to him below cost, with, per-
haps, 30 years in which to pay off. the bill.
The Government doesn’t supply him with
money far below the market rate per
medium of an Agricultural Bank or a State
Land Bank. He is charged much higher
local rates than the bucolic or bullock-rear-
ing person, for all the State grants for the
support of roads go to the country. He is
expected to pay higher wages for shorter
hours than the dairy farmer or wheat
raiser. He has no inexpensive child labor
like the cow cocky. Lie gets no Govern-
ment aid in drought time. And all the
while he is fighting against a foreign com-
petition to which other industrial bosses
are strangers. The wheat-grower, the wool-
raiser, the meat-producer, the gold-miner,
the timber-getter have little real competi-
tion to fear. They are producing things—
in many cases they are not really produc-
ing things but just tearing off the face or
out of the innards of the earth the irre-
placeable capital provided by Nature —
which are in short supply. They have not
to do serious battle with the importer, while
the manufacturer has to fight him all the
time. Incidentally it may be mentioned
that the figures quoted above, good as they
are, fail to do justice to the manufacturer.
The statistics credit him with the value of
his output less the cost of raw material.
The farmer is credited with the value of his
entire output, and the value of his raw
material —seed, manure, etc. —isn’t de-
ducted. The miner is also credited with the
value of his entire output. Yet his raw
material consists of whole towns and cities
—Kalgoorlie, Broken Hill, Charters
Towers, Bendigo, Ballarat, and places of
the sort. Wnen he has used them up great
will be the fall thereof. But even with the
dice loaded against him in these various
ways the manufacturer stands to-day for
the most progressive branch of Australian
industry.
Again, it is worth while to remember that
the manufacturer stands for the only con-
tinuously progressive branch of Australian
industry. In this country’s variable climate
other businesses have dizzy booms and
disastrous falls. But the maker of finished
materials, when he is given anything like a
show against cheap foreign competition,
plods upwards all the time. Thus the value
of the agricultural output of the Common-
wealth reached the top in 1909
(£41,056,000). By 1911, despite all
the struggle to promote land settlement, it
was down to £38,774,000. The value of
the pastoral output of Australia reached
the maximum of £56,993,000 in 1910. In
1911 it was down to £50,725,000. The value
of all mining products fell from’£2B,3ol,ooo
in 1907 to £23,450,000 in 1911. All this
time the value of manufactures kept rising.
Between 1910 and 1911 the product of the
combined “primary industries” of the Com-
monwealth dropped from £142,136,000 to
£137,814,000. a fall of £4,322,000. But
manufactures increased by £5,333,000, and
this saved the situation.
Though the figures show that Australian
manufactures, when they get a very mod-
erate amount of encouragement, increase
faster than any other branch of industry,
and that they grow steadily even while the
much-boomed but exceedingly variable
“primary industries” are in*a state of
slump, the need for higher ITotection is
obvious. The “primary industries” acquired
such a start in the days when people held
that it was Australia’s mission to be a
hewer of corn and a drawer of wool and
bullock-meat for more advanced nations,
that the output of wheat and wool and
butter and cheese and beef and mutton and
things of that sort is vastly ahead of the
local demand, and the producer has to de-
pend largely on the foreign market to keep
him afloat. On the other hand the local
production of clothes, boots, hats, ships,
machinery, paper, ink, furniture, locomo-
tives, steel rails, crockery, glassware, and a
thousand other items is still much behind
the demand, and the community has to de-
pend largely on imports. The business of
paying for these imports is a serious drain
on the “great natural resources” of the
country, when the “great natural’ resources”
strike a bad year. And when a strenuous
war comes, and the command of the sea is
temporarily or permanently lost- —which is
a disaster that happens to every country
one day or another —the shortage of local
manufactures will be just as bad a disaster
as the superabundance of raw products. It
will be impossible to send away the surplus
of raw material for foreign sale. So the
surplus will rot and become a bad debt.
Incidentally, it will give forth a bad smell.
It will be impossible to import boots and
hats and paper and ink and steel rails and
locomotives and crockery and glassware and
ships and sundries to make good the local
shortage, and even if they could be im-
ported it would be impossible to pay for
them through inability to turn into money
the surplus raw products that are giving
off the smell already mentioned. As a nat-
ural result, the country will relapse into
savagery. It is an unpleasant fact that
Australia is even now so far behind in
manufactures that, six weeks or so after
the war starts and the command of the sea
is lost, it will be necessary to treat wounded
soldiers without chloroform, and to saw off
broken limbs with crude carpenters’ instru-
ments. And even the wives and daughters
of the rich will have to be operated on for
appendicitis and such-like complaints with-
out anaesthetics, and probably with carving
knives and other dinner cutlery. The
country that is really strong for defence is
the one that doesn’t owe money abroad, and
that can supply its own requirements at
home when foreign supplies are cut off.
Australia isn’t that kind of country by a
long way. Furthermore, it is never likely to
be that kind of country while it sinks the
fiscal issue, and regards the growing of wool
for sale in Germany as of more importance
than the making, by local industry, of the
essentials of local civilisation. It is on these
grounds that The Bulletin regards an im-
proved tariff as one of the great questions
of the hour. Even the Referenda don’t be-
gin to compare with it.
[James Edmond, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
THE “REASONABLE AMENDMENTS”
THAT ARE NEVER DEFINED.
From time to time, of late —due possibly to
a carking suspicion that the electors are be-
ginning to acquire something like a correct
estimate of the Referenda proposals—Mr.
Joseph Cook and other Liberal leaders have
gone so far as to mention that the Austra-
lian Constitution may not be faultless after
all; and that they are not opposed to what
they call "reasonable amendments” of it.
A Sydney Sun writer, at the finish of a
series of anti-Keferenda articles, which
called upon the populace to turn down every
one of the Government’s proposals, went even
further. Said he, with a very taking air of
super-factional impartiality:—
If these propositions are rejected it will
become immediately the business of both parties
to agree upon amendments giving to the Federal
Parliament such added powers as it may reasonably
ask for.
If the Liberal party should be in a majority in
the next Parliament it will fall into utter national
contempt unless it formulates a scheme of Federal
expansion in the direction just indicated.
This is an eminently discreet attitude in
several ways. Racecourse frequenters
term it “laying off” or “hedging.” Assum-
ing that the Liberals get a working majority
in the next Parliament, they will be com-
pelled (bar accidents) either to remove the
Constitutional anomalies which are blocking
so much urgently-needed legislation at
present, or to face a record spell in
Opposition. And they know it. They
are in much the same disastrous posi-
tion on the Referenda question to-day
as they were on the Defence question in
1909. The present Liberal leader and his
associates somersaulted briskly on their im-
memorial policy of gift Dreadnoughts and
an increased subsidy when faced with the
responsibilities of office in 1910. Obviously
they are now getting the padded carpet and
so forth ready for another gyration of the
sort, on the off-chance that they may regain
the Treasury benches in 1913. The tactical
wisdom of this precaution is unassailable.
There is not a constitutional lawyer outside
party politics who disputes that, in order to
give the Australian Parliament any addi-
tional powers of the least real value, all the
Government proposals, with the possible ex-
ception of the last, will need to be carried.
One of the most obvious proofs of the cor-
rectness of this view is to be found in the
fact that though, during the last couple of
weeks, most of the leading Liberal speakers
and many of the Liberal papers, have
blithered repeatedly about “reasonable
amendments” and the desirability of the same,
not one of them has tabled an alternative
set of questions, or for that matter a single
alternative question, to those submitted by
the Government. It is unlikely that this
arises from a sporting desire to give the
other side a show. Such things do not
happen in politics. The stupidest of Conser-
vatives understands that nothing would
spike the Government’s guns more effectually
than the presentation, by Liberalism, of a
set of proposals for constitutional amend-
ments which would go just far enough to
satisfy nationalists (as distinguished from
Laborites) without in any way perturbing
the State Righters. Proposals of the sort
are not forthcoming for the simple reason
that Liberalism is incapable of performing
impossibilities.
Take the very first of the suggested
amendments ; namely, that which deals with
trade and commerce. Liberalism has gib-
bered more distractedly over this than over
all the rest put together. Joseph Cook and
others have pointed out that, since it will
give the Australian Parliament unrestricted
power over the Commonwealth’s trade and
commerce, the most ruinous and exasperating
things are bound to happen to business men
in the event of its being carried. It has been
prophesied variously that the tiniest details
of the management of the smallest and most
remote shops will be dictated from Melbourne
or Canberra; that every shopkeeper in every
State will need to keep a tame lawyer on the
premises to advise him when he is. or is
not, encroaching on purely Federal
powers; that no one will be able to sell
a bag of fruit or a chop or a pair of boots,
without consulting a herd of Commonwealth
officials; and that, owing to this insane
situation, trade will stagnate and chaos will
supervene. As Carruthers put it in Syd-
ney Telegraph (13/5/’l3) :
The Federal Parliament wants to control your
shoes and stores and interfere with your buying and
selling and trading. The Federal Parliament wants
to take sole charge of every company, large or small,
and regulate its business. Tell them you say NO to
this. Etc., etc.
No Fusion orator has forecasted, so far, that
if the Trade and Commerce Amendment is
carried, every business man will be ordered
by the Federal Parliament to cover his pre-
mises with red and white stripes, and decor-
ate the tame lawyer, aforesaid, with leg-
irons, and perform his daily toil in a full
suit of chain armor. A matter of inadver-
tence, doubtless. These untruths are more
picturesoue and novel than the stock ones
about Parliament interfering fractiously
with inoffensive little shops' and stores, and
they are no less flamingly idiotic.
-»* •*’*
Above everything these arguments about
the likelihood of the Commonwealth Gov-
ernment making a laughing-stock and a
political pariah of itself by meddling un-
necessarily with the details of small busi-
nesses are dishonest. Most of the Liberals
who are now yapping about the need for
“reasonable amendments” know perfectly
well that if their party is ever given a chance
to alter the Constitution in any direction it
will have to start on the Trade and Com-
merce clause. And they know, furthermore,
that it is impossible to frame an effective
amendment in this respect which will be any
less drastic than the one that is proposed by
the present Government. The deletion of the
three words "among the States” will give the
Federal Parliament, so to say, everythin?
So long as these words remain it has leeis
latively speaking, nothing or next to no-
thing. So long as the Australian l’arlia
ment is empowered only to legislate as re-
gards trade and commerce among the States
or, in other words, so long as it is debarred
from legislating within the borders of indi-
vidual States, it will never be able to pass
effective anti-Trust legislation. Here, as in
the United States, the Beef Trust, the Oil
Trust, or any other old Trust will be in v
position to register in those States which are
prepared to house them, and from behind
their borders hurl defiance at the Common-
wealth —just as the Tattersall’s Sweep
people are doing at the present moment. So
long as, to quote VY. H. Irvine, K.C., these
•‘extremely artificial restrictions upon the
trade and commerce power” remain, the Na-
tional Parliament will be unable to pass an
Invalidity and Unemployment Act of the
sort that is now operating in the
United Kingdom; or a satisfactory
Patents Act; or an Act regulating insurance
companies (other than inter-State ones) ; or
an effective Trade-marks Act, or a Seaman’s
Compensation Act that will benefit more than
a small proportion of sailors; and so on.
In fact, so long as the words “among the
States” remain in their present place in the
Constitution, the Commonwealth will con-
tinue to be comparable to a liner whose com-
mander is forbidden to control things aft of
the smoking room; or to a fleet, with an
Admiral-in-Chief who is permitted to give
orders in respect of only those ships which
have three or more funnels. With the re-
moval of the words of limitation, on the
other hand, all the barriers go down. The
Commonwealth Government will have the
same complete sway over the trade and com-
merce of Australia as the N. S. Wales
Government has over the trade and com-
merce of N. S. Wales; or as the British
Government has over the trade and com-
merce of the United Kingdom. How does
Liberalism propose to improve on that posi-
tion? By what means does it intend to give
the Australian Parliament any increased
powers over trade and commerce, without at
the same time handing over to it all those
trade powers which 95 per cent, of Liberals,
for the last three years, have been de-
nouncing as insanelv excessive?
The palpable fact is, that Liberalism has
no alternative amendments to offer. It
couldn’t frame them if it would, and in any
case it has no such desire. In this, as in so
many other campaigns, it is concerned merely
to obstruct; to play the part of the inebriate
who is propelled along from behind with his
heels in the earth, or who, that method of
resistance failing, lies down and blasphemes.
By wild talk about the degradation of con-
scription, the wicked disloyalty of those who
advocated a local fleet; and the foolishness
of defending Australia anywhere but in the
North Sea, this party blocked the Defence
movement for about six years. It will be
satisfied if, by means of similar tactics —that
is tb gay, by prophecies of assaults upon
“State Rights,” and by threats of
senseless interference with well-conducted
businesses on the part of the proprie-
tors’. own Federal representatives—it
can hold up for six, or even three
years, the industrial and social defence
inovement which is involved in the Referenda
proposals. At the last possible moment,
when the people have grown tired of its
perile gags about the horrors of Socialism,
and the miseries of being wronged by Mel-
bourne or Canberra, Liberalism may be re-
lied on to adopt the identical amendments
which are being advocated by most democrats
to-day, and put them .to the country as its
own.' But by may be too late for
the comfort and happiness of many thou-
sands of Australians. Nobody claims that
these amendments represent the last word
in Constitutional reform. This paper,, tor
example, as a paper, aspires after - a
change whereby the States shall hand over
all powers of every description to the Com-
monwealth, and receive back such powers as
the Commonwealth thinks advisable. How-
ever, reforms of that size take time; so,
meanwhile, it is advocating yvhat it regards
as the next best thing. Liberalism admits
the urgencv of “reasonable amendments m
the future.' Tiie Bulletin contends that it
“reasonable amendments” are desirable at
any time, then the amendments proposed
the Australian Government are desirable
now —since at least five-sixths of them re-
present the irreducible minimum in the way
of effective Constitutional alterations. _ «
Parliament can be trusted to admimst
rationally so much as a Dog Act it cap
trusted not to waste its time nationalist g
fruit-barrows and ruining suburban S r( ?cer -
stores. In a manner of speaking, the i cl
issue before the electors on May 31 will •
Can Parliament be trusted to admimste
Dog Act? ,
[John Bede Dalley, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.!
OUR COMPULSORY ARMY.
Great and bitter is the clamor of the _ us
tralian artti-Defence party these cold ay s
and wet nights of early winter. It is <?on
ing a sort of forlorn campaign on its little
own, with a view to the election of a new
Parliament which will disband the in an
army of Australia, and decide either to e
this country slide, or let it be a beggar a
Mother’s gate, clamoring for cheap de en
and eleemosynary protection. It ca *™;® p
its campaign mostly in the correspo ,i ie
columns of the daily press, and v,
fluff of battle has cleared away, and *
situation becomes fairly visible, its p P
arguments appear to be these:U) t . .
is ruinous, disastrous and inhuman,
is solid truth ; and if the other fellow couia
be persuaded of this fact, it would be ‘
good argument and worthy of imc
plause. (2) That the boy soldier is iw
to fall amid bad associations throug t 0
ing with other youths while R |ueii-
“slope arms” and “form fours fj be
like. And if the ultra-refined boy cojjj of
prevented from associating with . n g
his species at school and college, bear i n g
them on trains and steamboats, a an d
them occasionally cuss in the football-
being mixed up with t he “ pf a f t f h 0 e ideal
cricket and other games in f act > , w jth a
boy could be headed up in a- - j n a
Scripture for company, and kep g
cool atmosphere till he . ls , g r° W f n ma ke sure
policeman on perpetual duty to maKe


that no one swore through the bung-hole and
thus corrupted his morals —if all this could
be done there would be some reason in the
complaint that the good boy of the family
shouldn’t be compelled to slope arms in com-
pany with the bad boy of the highways.
And (3) it is alleged that “compulsion” is
tyrannical, shameful, and an outrage on the
inalienable right of every inhabitant of the
British Empire to be “free.” Yet the Brit-
ish citizen isn’t so madly free as a lot of
people profess to believe. He is compelled
to pay rates and taxes. The only country
in Europe that ever had voluntary taxes in
modern days was Poland, and it died soon
after it achieved complete freedom. The
Englishman is forced to hand out his money
for w T ar whether he likes war or not. He
is required to send his son to. school to learn
the rudiments of civil education, which isn’t
widely different from being compelled to
learn the rudiments of military education.
He is called upon to keep his back yard
clean, not to rear or kill pigs inside certain
areas, to wrnlk on the right side of the pave-
ment, and to drive on the left side of the
road. He is arrested if he appears in the
busiest street of a large city in a bathing
costume. And when he is a young citizen
at school he is forced to spend about
two of the best years of his life in
learning that almost every letter of
the insane British alphabet is pro-
nounced in so many different ways that it
would be almost as cheap to have no alpha-
bet at all. No person who is driven at the
whack of the cane to believe, contrary to
reason and conscience, that “c” sounds the
same as “k,” and that “col" in “colonel”
sounds as “kur” (which it really doesn’t,
because the “u” sound in “colonel” doesn’t
appear in the alphabet at all) has a right
to call himself free. He is much more like a
slave. It is a thousand times easier to be-
lieve in transubstantiation than to put
implicit faith in the pronunciation of the
English language. Yet the miserable serf-
kid is driven to school and spanked if he
hasn’t belief in these and a hundred equally
foolish propositions.
The Bulletin seriously and in all ear-
nestness propounds the statement that the
one word “which” is a greater outrage on
the freedom of the subject than anything
that Australia has yet devised or suggested
in the way of compulsory military training.
The small Australian boy is taught the
alphabet. He is instructed that certain let-
ters represent certain sounds. Then he is
confronted with such a word as “which,’
and it doesn’t contain a single sound which
is to be found in the alphabet. It is a com-
pound of three phonetic letters, well known
in Pitman’s shorthand system, but not to
be discovered in any ordinary school book.
A crowd of peace faddists which can put
up with a compulsory school system is
based on lies, beginning with the lie that “c”
is mostly “k,” shouldn’t make too great a
parade of its conscience over such a small
matter as compulsory drill.
In any case the alleged un-Britisli charac-
ter of compulsory military service is a
strange delusion, or a purposeful falsehood.
Most probably it is a purposeful falsehood.
England had, to our knowledge, a policy-
shambling and ill-organised at times, but still
a defined policy—of compulsory military and
naval service for some 2000 years, and pos-
sibly for much longer. It lasted down to the
time of Wellington and Nelson. I lie
kidnapping of men on land for naval pur-
poses was common enough during the
Napoleonic wars. The kidnapping of men
out of ships bailed up at sea was equally
common. When other means failed theie
was a handy device by which the sheriff: or
other official was advised that his county
was expected to provide a certain quota ot
men for military or naval _ business,
and the Government “saved its face
and avoided the outward appearance
of compulsion by not inquiring how the
official found the quota. The leader of the
famous mutiny at the Nore was a compul-
sory defender of his country who, so far as
can be discovered, had been stolen for naval
purposes out of the debtors’ prison m an in-
land Scotch town, where he had been confined
over-a matter of twopence or more. In Ireland
much recruiting was done under a simple
enactment which set forth that any able-
bodied male who was accused of an offence
might be sent to serve the King instead of
being brought to trial. Therefore any J.I.
who wanted his neighbor’s wife or daugh-
ter or best girl, and who found difficulties
in the way, or who wanted to cancel a debt
or get rid of an awkward witness, had the
said neighbor arrested secretly after dark
on a charge of high treason or intoxication,
and the neighbor was far away before morn-
ing on the road to join his blessed Majesty s
forces. » * *
Britain, since it really attempted to adopt
the voluntary service system, has fought a
few border wars, lately by the aid of colored
troops, and it has been involved in the
Crimean scrap and the South African un-
pleasantness. The latter may be summed
up in a few sentences. The two Boer na-
tions didn’t equal the population of Sydney.
They fought the voluntary army of the
proudest nation in Europe for over two
years. They made that proud nation feel
glad to accept all the assistance it could get
from Canada. Australia and Maonland.
And in the end the nation with the voluntary
army paid its microscopic enemy several mil-
lions to make a nominal surrender, and faxed
up terms by which the enemy’s General is now
Prime Minister, not onlyof the
States, but of the old British States as
well But the Crimean war was the first
real test of Britain’s voluntary system, and
the result was poor enough to make any
patriotic Australian desire to put this coun-
try’s anti-Defence faddists in gaol. When
the four allied States got together the odds
against Russia in population were about
three to two; in money probably 12 to one,
and in sea-power almost anything that any-
body liked to mention. In bookmaking
phrase, you might write your own ticket as
regards 'the sea-power. Britain s voluntary
army went to the front in great sty 1 .
had enjoyed many vears of peace. In time
of quietude the loafing barrack life appealed
to many, and enough recruits were available
to Ktp the fore? together,. Tbtoto .was
sent to the Crimea, leaving tibe British
islands nearly destitute of troops. It
fought at Alma and Balaclava and Inker-
mann and very soon after that there was
practically no army left. _ A time. came
when Britain had only 12,000 soldiers in the
field, many of them crippled, and only the
solid masses of its allies prevented the beg-
gared handful from being eaten up. The
new recruits, so badly wanted, refused to
transpire. The voluntary system burst at
the first time of asking. Britain came down
to the idea of inviting its old discharged
veterans to come out from the workhouses
and the slums, and form a few grey-bearded,
bald-headed brigades. It seriously attempted
to hire soldiers in Germany. It had no
share in the last battle of the Crimean war,
and the handful of men whom it contributed
to the last storming expedition was beaten
down by sheer force of numbers. But for
the fact that France possessed a real army
—one large enough to burst through the line
of Russian forts —it seems just possible that
the allies would be outside Sebastopol to this
day.
There is nothing in British experience to
persuade Australia that any voluntary army
it could possibly hope to raise or pay would
make the slightest show in the defence of
this great thinly-peopled country. And yet
the country must be defended, and very much
better-defended than by its present compul-
sory boy army, if the Australian wants to
feel sure that he will be something better in
the future than wood-and-water joey to an
Asiatic invader. The assurance of the anti-
Defence crowd that war is a curse is abso-
lutely solid. Unfortunately it is impossible
to get any adequate assurance that the other
fellow will give up the curse.
fJames Edmond, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Pure Matter of Law.
A Sydney Telegraph writer gave to the
world, the other day, one of those stunning
discoveries which wreck Ministries and be-
smear unscrupulous politicians with the mud
of ignominy and the dust of ridicule. In the
cold, judicial language of the immemorial
lawyer, he pointed out —taking little more
than a column and a quarter of his journal
to do it—that the Chief Electoral Officer of
the Commonwealth is liable to be fined
£234,162,000. His offence consists in pub-
lishing the Referenda pamphlet, at the dic-
tation of W. M. Hughes, without the signa-
tures and addresses of the authors. The
Telegraph man, having tried and sum-
marily convicted the wretched official, con-
tinued remorselessly, as follows :
The inexhaustible resourcefulness of the Attorney-
General, however, will doubtlessly prove equal to
providing some dodge by which the Chief Electoral
Officer may be spared from having to offer himself
up as a vicarious sacrifice for the expiation of other
people’s misdeeds. Unless Mr. Hughes has lost all
his old agility, he will be able to find some way of
dodging the recoil of his illegal dodger. Etc., etc.
Now the writer is in a position to state that
the above surmise is all too true. The un-
speakable Hughes proposes, in the first
place, to take the entire responsibility for
the crime, or whatever it is, on his own
shoulders. This he is enabled to do by a
statute of Carolus I. (see Rex v. Croydon
—Churchwardens of —S. T.R. 713). He will
then proceed to prosecute himself in the
nearest police court; this being essentially
a case where there is sufficient seisin to
maintain quare impedit. He intends to
plead, as a sort of preliminary shuffle, that
while the writs were not issued till April 24
the incriminating pamphlet has been avail-
able to the public since February 12. Is a
newspaper proprietor liable, he will ask,
because unsigned electioneering matter
which he published on April 23 is
sold in Port Darwin on May 1? Having
been bumped out contemptuously on this
point, he will proceed to unloose his second
string. Parliament, he will argue, with that
low cunning for which he is notorious, in
authorising the publication of the cases for
and against the Constitutional changes that
are sought by the Commonwealth, inferen-
tially discharged the printers, and so on, of
the Government pamphlet from the obliga-
tions of the Electoral Act. _ Assuming that
the magistrate is fit for his post, he will
immediately dismiss, with scorn, all thought
of what the late Lord Macnaghten has
pithily termed “fortuitous occurrence from
without” (Fenton v. Thorley). and impose
on Hughes a penalty which, with costs
added, will amount to £234,162,400 10s. 6d.
Hughes will now be seen with his back to
the wall, haggard, desperate —a pitiable
object even to right-thinkers. It is as easy
to foretell the rest as it is to prophesy the
miseries that must follow upon an affirma-
tive answer to the Referenda questions.
His first move will be, in his capacity
of culprit, to hand a cheque for the
amount involved to the representative
of infuriated Law and Order. It
will then be his duty under the Impound-
ing Act (29 Vic.) immediately to demand
back the cheque as the only member of the
Government present ('vide Lacave v. Credit
Lyonnais. 1 Q.B. 148) and pay it into Ins
account 'as Attorney-General. The next
scene in this squalid drama is the dishonor-
ing of the cheque, by Hughes’s bank, on
grounds that are plausibly set out by
Jevons, in his “Money and the Mechanism
of Exchange,” Bagehot (“Lombard-street )
and other authorities. We now have the
enemy in a fair way to being the most stupen-
dous insolvent that the world has ever
known. As Attorney-General he simultane-
ously files a petition in bankruptcy against
himself, and begins a criminal action against
himself for giving worthless cheques. He
also writs himself in the District Court;
gets judgment for the* full amount; garni-
shees his banking account; and puts a
bailiff into his mansion Gore Hill. In
vain. He is still £234.161,000 Is. 6d. to the
bad. As culprit he remains sullenly recalci-
trant. As Attorney-General he begins to
get extremely annoyed with himself. He
asks himself indignantly, once and for all,
is the balance of the fine going to be* paid?
The answer is a distracted “No. 'Momen-
tarily losing his temper, he threatens him-
self with physical violence. Being now on
the verge of a nervous breakdown in both his
'capacities, as a result of worry, fie insti-
tutes suits against himself for barratry,
chamoert.v and maintenance, and multiple-
poinding : and has fiimSelf heavily fined for
loitering, depositing debris in a public place,
and driving a taxi-cab without a license.
Meanwhile Costs are steadily mounting up.
As culprit he has nothing to offer but more
cheques, and these, as Attorney-General, he
firmly refuses to accept—on the excellent au-
thority of Kutner’s “Gerichtliclie Medicin.”
He issues a c a sa order against himself, and
is committed to prison till such time as he
shall have earned enough, in his capacity of
daily journalist, to satisfy the debt. A
cheerless ending to a brilliant career; but
such disasters almost invariably overtake
those who hurry about trying to amend
Constitutions, and in other respects inter-
fere with the established order of things.
[John Bede Dalley, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
The Fall of Wentworth.
Joseph Carruthers, the political parish-
ioner who dropped out of the N. S. Wales
Premiership so suddenly a few years ago,
has been pumping his bitterness against
Australia into the columns of the Sunday
Times (Sydney). One slop:—
Wentworth, Parkes, Dalley, Martin, Robert-
son, Lang, E. S. Hall. .. .won for us [State Frights]
not only our elective Pari aments, but generally
laid down the basis upon which has been reared the
progress and prosperity of our State. .. .there stands
on old Grose Farm our noble University founded by
Wentworth.
Does the education there inculcated teach us that
any people in all the eras of history have ever been
made happier or more prosperous by sacrificing that
form of government under which peace and plenty,
comfort and contentment has been the result, for a
mere shadowy pretence of government, which placed
the governing authority further away from the
hearts and the homes of the people?
The gall of Jokeruthers in leading for-
ward the ghost of Wentworth to back the
anti-Australian fight is wonderful. It was
Wentworth? who, while in London, formed
the “General Association for the Australian
Colonies,” and, on March 3L, 1857, took the
chair at a meeting of the association, at
which a memorial was adopted emphasising
the need of a Federal Assembly, and setting
out that —
It was not to be wondered at that a strong feeling
of d'scontent should be growing up among the in-
habitants of these colonies from their being com-
pelled to resort to such indirect, tedious and illegal
expedients in substitution of that Federal authority
without which their several Constitutions must con-
tinue incomplete as regards all measures and un-
dertakings which require the joint action and co-
operation of any two or more of them.
Wentworth’s hope was that this Assembly
would have power to deal with Australia s
“tariffs, lighthouses, gauges of connecting
railways, navigation of connecting rivers,
telegraphs and postage, the upset or mini-
mum price of land, management of the gold-
fields, coinage, weights and measures, de-
fence, a court of appeal, penal settlements
and any other matter which might he sub-
mitted to it” His expressed prayer was
for “the rapid expansion of Australia into
a nation which should rule supreme in the
Southern world.” William Charles
Wentworth was a Federalist born ahead
of his time, and it is hard metal that, by
the gang that hates Australia and the
national aspirations, he should now be ex-
hibited along with the straw Sosli tiger, the
Frights and other wild myths—especially
when the exhibitor is a parishioner like J.
Carruthers.
[John Barr, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
The Blessings of Peace.
The Anglo-American Peace Centenary cele-
bration, which was widely advertised last
week, is impressive, yet less impressive than
it might be. It suggests the reformation of
the old sinner who has lost his punch, or
the resolution in favor of the simple life
which is passed by the gorged alderman at
the end of the banquet. The British Empire
covers nearly 12,000,000 square miles of the
earth’s surface, and Britain has temporarily
owned and lost a vast amount more. In
olden days, when the Ma country hadn’t
acquired its place in the sun, it fought like
a demon on the smallest provocation against
Frenchmen, Spaniards, Dutchmen, Portu-
guese, Boers, Hindus and miscellaneous
niggers who had desirable allotments that
might be seized.' Now it has got so much
land that it sags at the knees under the
burden of its responsibilities, and it says:
“Let us cease from wars of conquest. Let
there be a profound and beastly calm.” The
United States began by wresting its original
13 States from Britain. It bought up a
great area from France and another from
Russia when these countries were in a tight
place. It bullied the war-worn, distracted
republic of Mexico out of most of its terri-
tory. It beguiled the Texan republic into a
union. It knocked a few islands out of
Spain when Spain was on its uppers, and it
snatched the Kanaka empire of Hawaii
when the fighting race of Kamehameha had
died out, and a silly, fat, brown woman sat
on the throne at Honolulu. Now the
United States, with nearly 4,000,000 square
miles of territory, believes in peace—un-
limited peace —with the proviso that if
there are any robberies to be done in Latin
America it shall be sole robber. Russia,
with nearly 0,000,000 square miles of coun-
try, mostly gained by conquest, reckons that
as soon as it gets a few ports which will be
free of ice all the year round it will also
howl for perpetual tranquillity. In fact, all
the huge States which have robbed whole-
sale believe in brotherhood and arbitration
and so on, in order to prevent any
ambitious new States depriving them
of the plunder. It is a beautiful
arrangement, especially in the case of
the United States, which reserves to itself,
under the Monroe Doctrine, the sole right
of freebooting the lands of Latin America.
And all the small States of the globe applaud
wildly, and wave their tattered hats in the
air, and say that as soon as they have had
their turn at the plunder, and have robbed
their neighbors to their complete satisfac-
tion, and have acquired so much property
that they would be liable to blow up if they
attempted to eat any more, peace wilj be
their chief aspiration also. Just now Turkey
is being carved up, and every little. State
thereabouts reckons that it will be quite sat-
isfied if it can only get as much of Turkey
as it wants, and will abhor strife thereafter.
And Britain, which has already robbed the
Sublime Porte of Egypt and the Soudan and
Cyprus, urges these small hungry nations
to be calm and moderate.

The alleged campaign of the Cabbage Gar-
den Ministry against the Referendum pro-
posals was a humorous circumstance. .The
only energetic speaker in the Cabinet,
Watt, had dodged the extra toil by getting
away to the Cold Land. McBride
there also, as Agent-General. Lands McKen-
zie developed congestion of the lungs, and
had to drop the job. Then John Weary
discovered he was much run down in health
(he hasn’t been getting enough sleep, poor
man), and must recuperate at Warrnambool.
Peacock fluttered into the breach —and con-
tracted a chill, which necessitated the can-
cellation of all engagements.. Railways Bill-
son next offered to sacrifice himself ; he wasn’t
much good; still, he was something. But
alas! he had only got as far as Beechworth
when illness overtook him too. There only
remained Graham, who wasn’t taking any
State Frights ; Edgar, who had a tough elec-
tion of his own on his hands; Thomson,
and Fat Landlords Hageltiiorn and J. Dry
Brown, none of whom could draw an audi-
ence of two men and a boy. It looked as if
the negative side in the Referendum scuffle
in the Cabbage Garden would be left to the
Women's National League and a few elocu-
tionists who were doing the cause more harm
than good; so the Age spoke firmly to the
associated Wearies and Invalids, and with
one consent they recovered a.nd got busy on
the job of explaining to Victoria that it
would really be a disaster to shift any power
from such a vigorous Ministry and pass it
on to a poor crowd so lacking in energy as
Fisher and Hughes and Pearce, not to
mention John Forrest and Willy Kelly.
[F. C. Jones, Elgar-road, Burwood, Victoria.]
The incapacity of the present N.S.W. State
Government in the essential matter of finance
is becoming a public disgrace as well as a
public misery. So far as its ability to look
after the cash of the community is concerned
it seems to be partly an infant and partly a
lunatic and partly a nigger. It doesn’t seem
to know any more about how to keep sol-
vent than a mulatto treasurer in Hayti.
During the first 10 months of the present
financial year it had £114,920 more to spend
than it had in the corresponding period of
last year, but unfortunately it rushed up
the expenditure by (£1,701,918. Already,
though the Government had the bivgest in-
come that any N.S.W. Ministry ever saw r or
dreamt of, the outgoings are £1,969,519 ahead
of the receipts. The loan expenditure for
10 months is over £6,600,000. And this is a
Government which is pledged, on the
security of its political conscience—if it
possesses one, which is doubtful —to practise
economy, to reduce loan expenditure, and to
spend no loan money except on works which
will pay full interest on the money bor-
rowed for their construction, and. something
towards a sinking fund. Never in all Aus-
tralian history has there been a Cabinet
which turned dog on its promises more
shamelessly, which got votes under false
pretences more recklessly, and which showed
► a more complete incapacity to realise that it
is a blot on the political landscape.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Voice: Give us your policy.
Mr. Cook: If I gave you my policy you wouldn't
understand it. (Laughter.)
—Report of Joe Cook’s Bendigo Meeting.
Wiiat! Would you ask for the Liberal
policy ?
When did I ever such ignorant folly see?
’Tis inconceivable !
Hardly believable!
What could you know of that mystical thing?
Nay, ’tis enshrouded in sacred obscurity ;
’Twill be revealed in some distant futurity ;
Wait a few years for it;
Then you’ll raise cheers for it,
And all the land with Hosannahs shall ring.
Hist! Lest the populace glean the least
word of it!
Tell them our policy ! Who ever heard of it ?
People, be serious!
Something mysterious
Lurks in the dark at the back of the scenes.
Nothing in modern nor yet ancient history—
Delphian oracle, Asian mystery —
E’er was so mystical,
Signs cabalistical
Have to be learned ere ye know what it
means.
Hush! Let the ignorant never get breath
of it;
One little word would encompass the death
of it.
Guard it religiously !
It is prodigiously
Secret and sacred. Ah, cherish it well!
Let not the tiniest rumor auricular
Get to the crowd on the smallest particular.
Argue persuasively,
Answer evasively,
But our Great Secret we never must tell.
Chut! Have a care! E’en our minions be
mutable!
Give them no hint of our secret inscrutable.
Talk like an oracle;
Words metaphorical
Pour in the ears of the credulous crowd.
Nay, keep it dark, if existence political
Ever you valued —the moment is critical!
Close as a cloister
Be, dumb as an oyster.
The Caucus to baulk us would howl it aloud.
Ssh! Only Joe and a few hold the key to it;
Trust them implicitly; safely they’ll see to
it.
Fondly they’re holding it,
Shaping and moulding it,
Oh, ’twill be marvellous when they are
through !
They will reveal it when Labor is perishing ;
Till then our Holy of Holies they’re cherish-
ing.
Cryptic and wonderful!
Tut! Let no blunderful
Liberal speak, or the day he will rue!
Hist! Not a word! Lest ■ our chrysalis
beautiful
Should be disturbed by a whisper undutiful.
Hush ! Not a syllable !
For it is killable.
Once to reveal it were fatal for sure.
Though it is now in a state somewhat sta-
tical,
Wholly mysterious, quite enigmatical,
In some futurity
Out of obscurity,
Lo, ’twill emerge to us perfect and pure.

Toolangi, Victoria.]

( harles Gregory Wade begins to see ghosts
everywhere. <)n Monday he announced a
tresh batch in a half-column shriek to the
electors, aimed at the Referenda and the
senate. Here are some items :
It means minority rule. New South Wales will
only have six members when we ought to have 14
in the Senate. The three smaller States, with half
our population, will have three times our votiner-
power.
No, it doesn’t mean minority rule exactly,
lhe small State Senators can do more than
their fair share in blocking “hasty legisla-
tion which the Representatives have
passed, but they can’t pass legislation that
the Reps, disapprove of. And Wade is a
whale for blocking “hasty legislation.” Any-
how, of all politicians he surely can’t object
it Queensland and Tasmania, with sensible,
reliable Liberal” Governments, are given a
certain special advantage in Federal legisla-
tion as against Labor-ridden N.S.W. Again :
The Commonwealth will have a controlling influ-
railways, which we have paid for, and
which are the security we offer for loans for develop-
ment purposes.
It will lead to complete control of the railways,
and the Federal Parliament will then regulate the
policy of railway construction in this State.
Now, when did N.S.W. pay for its rail-
ways? Doesn’t it simply owe for them?
And considering the hash N.S.W. made of
things when it broke the uniform gauge
agreement and started all the diverse
gauges which have been a misery to this
country for 50 years or so, isn’t it time
that some central authority came fflong to
straighten the tangle—likewise to pay off
the railway liabilities which N.S.W. seems
incapable of shifting?
[J. Reid, 179 Bourke-street, Darlinghurst. ]
