Since the Walls of Derry and the Flames
ot Smitlifield and the spectre of old Dad
Hamlet on the battlements of Elsinore,
there have been no more remarkable spectres
than those recently dug up for Federal elec-
tion purposes by Cabruthebs, ex-Premier
of N.S.W. Hear them gibber a little:—
Just as surely as six Britishers were punished for
coming here to engage in lawful occupations as
hatters, under the “immigration” powers vested with
the federal Parliament.
Just as surely as a shipwrecked crew were gaoled
under the “aliens” powers vested in the Federal
Parliament.
Just as surely as a woman, a British subject, and
her children born in Australia are being sent out of
Australia under the “aliens” laws of the Common-
wealth.
So surely you may expect that the Federal Parlia-
ment will have its full ounce of flesh under the new
powers which it seeks.
Now, in the first place, no six “Britishers”
were ever “punished” for coming here “to
engage in lawful occupations as hatters.” In
the second place no “shipwrecked crew were
gaoled.” In the third place, no female
British subject and her children born in
Australia” were ever expelled. The Six
Hatters were neither fined, imprisoned,
flogged nor outlawed; in fact, when last
heard of, some of them were canvassing
strenuously in the Labor interest. The
wrecked Petriana niggers were carefully
sent home instead of being gaoled, which is
hardly the sort of treatment a white sailor
gets in many nigger countries. Mrs. Poon
( I9OEY wasn’t a British subject, and her
children, “born in Australia.” weren’t “sent
away. ’ Mrs. Poon voluntarily took the chil-
dren away. These may be small discre-
pancies;—just like the small discrepancy of
a certain French editor, who announced "that
a dragoon had been executed at Paris, and
had to withdraw the charge next day, and
state that it was really a female cook who
had been arrested for theft at Valence—but
it is well to be accurate when the writer
can do it without undue exertion.
[James Edmond. The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
From a London sampler of printed ware :
A book on The Married Life of Queen Victoria ”
by Mrs. Jerrold, just published in London, contains
a lot of new material, and throws fresh light upon
the Prince Consort. He died at a little over 40
and if he had lived longer Mrs. Jerrold believes
™ ould have been a revolution in England
His life was a long attempt to put back the hands
ot the clock, she says. “ There would have been
a struggle between people and the Crown, which
would have ended in the abolition of Monarchy.”
the training of the numerous Royal children is
condemned as “ too priggish and severe.” Hence
... the ready way in which the eldest son (the late
Edward) took \enus as his guide, philosopher
and friend by the time he was 20.” According to
Mrs. Jerrold it was while on a visit to Cambridge
to get the young Prince of Wales out of a scrape
with a girl that the Prince Consort caught the cold
that brought about his death.

On Friday evening Oscar Asche and Lily
Brayton will be carried on to the stage of
Sydney Royal for the last time by a number
of perspiring colored gentlemen. On Satur-
day evening “Kismet’'’ supplants the great
Shakesbacon play of “Antony and Cleo-
patra,” and, unless the managerial adver-
tisement lies, it will run on its own bare
teet for one week only. The playchaser will
please note that he is expected to be on the
mark promptly, unless he wants to be left
lamenting at the box office.
* * * Sis
This paper has little to add to the re-
marks which it made last month about “The
Count of Luxembourg.” The music is
charming, but there is not enough of it; the
humor is thin, and there is too much of it.
Why the chief comedian has been suffered
to interpolate _ a couple of flamingly vaude-
villian items into so gracious a setting is
not apparent. They have about as much re-
lation to their environment, artistically
speaking, as a pair of second-hand goloshes
bears to a Worth frock. The topical song
is not merely dull; it is pathetic. However,
m justice to the business capacity of the
people who are responsible for the produc-
tion, it may be mentioned that on Monday
night, when the writer attended at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney, the bulk of the audience
was convulsed with amusement as often as
convulsions were expected of it. Ready, re-
peated and strong convulsions on the part
of an audience are the surest tests of effi-
cient stage-management, after all. “The
Count of Luxembourg” differs chiefly from
its predecessors of the past 10 or 15 years
inasmuch as the hero and heroine (1) ‘waltz
“THE COUNT OF LUXEMBOURG," AT
HER MAJESTY'S,
SYDNEY.
The latest lunacy—waltzing downstairs.
up and down stairs, and (2) are married
with a large picture between them, so ar-
ranged that they cannot see one another’s
taces. In other respects there is a certain
sameness or familiarity about the little mis-
understandings which make up the plot.
The ridiculous old Grand Duke thinks that
the distracting Angele loves and will marry
him. The good and cheery, but deplorably
plain Brissard fears that Juliette does not
care for him. He goes to the Grand Duke’s
reception without an invitation; assumes
various disguises; and is in a chronic state
of nervousness lest he may be flung out as
an impostor. Angele does not know that
the man she loves is the man she has mar-
ried ; the Count is ignorant of the fact that
Angele, whom he worships, is his wife. They
all recover their intellects with a rush in
Act 11., and the show ends. The essential
feature of it is the waltz number which we
have come to associate with Franz Lehar.
Love, can it be Love?” is its name, and it
is as tuneful and sensuous and dreamy and
emotional as anything that has come out of
Vienna. How many hearts have beaten
wildly to that air! How many little hands
have been squeezed ! How manv evening
and other slippers have been stood on with
rurtive affection, for hours at a time, and
mayhap, irretrievably ruined! Millions
probably. But this is no place for
statistics.
Miss Florence Young (Angele) sings as
accurately and as tunefully as of yore, and
the same commendation applies to W Tal-
o-vr,A4ndre\vs; who plays the Count. Miss
oibyi Arundale is her customary entrancing
self, as Juliette. Phil Smith, the J.C W
co. s new comedian, has distinct threaten-
mgs of humor, but he toils too hard, or tries
to do too much, or something. Just as one
can have too much Johnson, so one can have
too much Smith. His Brissard habitually
works overtime, a revolting spectacle to a
community which is seriously thinking of
evolving a Strikers’ Union whose members
will support themselves entirely by drawing
strike pay and giving evidence before Wages
Boards. W. S. Percy, Victor Prince and
Edmund Sherras are their old competent
selves. Louis Victor and Miss Flossie Dick-
enson do « dance in Act I. which should be
popular in chaoel-going circles during the
coming winter; and Miss Vivien Talleur and
Charles Albert do likewise in Act 11.

Already the Criterion, where “The For-
tune Hunter” gets the girl as well as the
fortune, has beaten the record rainfall of
Genesis. It has reigned there for 'over 40
nights, and there seems no sign of the
weather clearing.

The headliners at the National Amphi-
theatre this week are Henchey, Vincetti
and Bush, comedy acrobats. Anyhow, they
are billed as comedians, but that is a mild
way of putting it. What the National
wants is a good, strong, boiler-plated puff
writer. Presumably, the National manage-
ment, being on a sound wicket and fearing
no investigation of its show by the general
public, has no need for. a chilled-steel puff
agent. After seeing the programme, this
writer agrees with the management.
[Elizabeth Prig, c/o Mrs. S. Gamp, High Hol-
born, London.]
* * * *
At West’s Sydney house, Manager Geach
is unwinding this week an exclusive film
entitled “Red and White Roses.” It gives
the inside running of a prominent American
politician’s double life, or a life distributed
into two halves, or apportioned as fths and
ith, one portion (probably the |th) being
thrown to his wife and the other portion
being devoted to a beautiful lover. West’s
advertisement writer describes this as “the
sweetest story ever told.” Maybe, but it can’t
last. Leastways, it didn’t last on a cele-
brated occasion in the writer’s experience,
and probably a week of it will be enough
at West’s. But, it’s a good story in the
meantime.
[Adam, Garden of Eden, Sydney. Enter without
knocking and keep off the grass.]

The revival of the old minstrel “first
part” at the Alhambra (Sydney) music-hall
has drawn increased business to that ex-
cino theatre. Fresh vaudeville turns, rang-
ing from ragtime warbling to wire walking
and from juggling to joy-dancing have im-
proved the programme this week. There is
plenty of variety and all the talent isn’t in
the up-town halls.

Miss Mary Butler, of M.L., gave a
dramatic recital at St. James’s Hall (Syd-
ney) on Saturday night. She is a highly
capable young woman, skilled in the tech-
nique of her art, and with a wide range of
achievement. In fact, as a ranger she is
almost unique. She ranged last week from
Queen Katherine’s Defence” to “How Girls
I lay Bridge (a monologue), and “Jemima’s
Courtship. She also ranged from “If I
Were King —the romantic play which Syd-
neyites associate hauntingly with the vast-
footed Cuyler Hastings—to “Yes, Papa,” a
mere lightsome sketch. In the former she

“Within the Law,” introduced to Aus-
tralia, at Melbourne Royal, as aforesung, is
a kinematographic kind of erimodrama of
WITHIN THE LAW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Amurkan erimodrama, so the management affirms,
To be properly translated needs a key to “crooky”
terms ;
Then here are some selections that enable one to
draw
True criminal conclusions from the play “Within
the Law.”
If a burglar, or a 'bezzler, or a crook of common
clay
Does a guy (which means to clear out) —well, he
“makes a get away.”
But if the traps arrest him, and to quod, or prison
fetch
The fugitive from justice, he is sentenced to a
“stretch.”
A “dip” is a pickpocket; should he chance to pick
a purse
He is said to “lift a leather,” and he mutters “Good-
night, nurse,”
When he’s taken to a “cooler,” there in solitude to
dwell,
For a “cooler,” in the lingo, means a little dungeon
cell.
A “bull” is a policeman, and a giddy girl’s a
“frail” ;
If she shakes his “piece of change,” the lady “cops
the copper’s kale” ;
And if the cop intimidates the frail he wants to
snare,
She doesn’t say he frightened her, she says he
“threw a scare.”
A damsel young and innocent, not yet upon the joh,
Has “nothing on” her character—in fact, she is a
“squab” ;
Whereas a “stiff’s” a fathead, and a “pigeon on a
stool”
Is a fizgig by whose “frame-up” pals are put away
to cool.
To “blow the joint” means vanish from the shady
haunts of crime;
A whisky mixed with soda is a “highball” all the
time. *
And if a boob when inky talks a lot of silly guff,
You tap him on the gab, and bid the josser “can the
stuff.”
If a footpad wants your ticker, as he does without
a doubt,
When he orders you to “shoot it” you had better
fork it out.
“Cracked ice” is slang for diamonds, with citizens
who thieve;
And wisdom cries “fall off it” if a fiction you
believe.
After sitting on the story of the play “Within the
Law”
(Which, staged at Melbourne Royal, seemeth pretty
sure to draw
The many-headed public who can easily believe),
The Bard has “fallen off it,” and is laughing up
his sleeve.

’Phone No. 236 Central. SYDNEY H. CARY,
the same moral tone and color as its com-
patriots “Alias Jimmy Valentine” and “Get-
Rich-Quick Wallingford.” Such dramas are
a natural product in the paradise of Trusts
and Combines. The programme puts the
story in a glazed nutshell: —
Mary Turner, an innocent shopgirl, is convicted of
stealing. She is “sent up” for three years as an
example to the other girls. She lays the responsi-
bility for the stealing at the door of the proprietor
of the Emporium, who pays starvation wages. The
law can be moulded to suit the wishes of the rich,
6he finds, and once out of gaol she lays her plans
accordingly. Surrounded by a band of clever crooks,
she, soon becomes rich. The system which she pur-
sues in her depredations is to keep within the law
without breaking its actual letter, though constantly
defeating its spirit. Meanwhile she is steadily pur-
suing her plans for revenge, for the debt which
society owes her she is determined to collect. How,
in carrying this out, she marries her old employer’s
son, and then crushes him with the announcement
that she is a gaol-bird; how the police “stool-
pigeon,” or decoy, is shot with a silent revolver, and
Mary and her husband are dragged into the net of
Police-Inspector Burke, are incidents in the pro-
gress of the play.
“ LIFE’S SHOP WINDOW," AT MEL-
BOURNE KING’S.
Max Clifton as the
Colonel.
Fred Kehoe , a blood-
thirsty cowpuncher.
The glaringly theatrical finish of the first
act is, after all, the strongest and least
false situation in the drama. Mary, hand-
cuffed to a policeman, is allowed • to come
and talk to the Emporium boss in his private
office, telling him that his shop-girls can’t be
expected to remain honest on a wage of six
dollars a week. She threatens to be re-
venged when she has done her time, and the
young woman’s absurdly improbable accom-
plishment of her purpose provides lurid cur-
tains for the next two acts. The curtain
to the final act is feeble, for the ungentle
Mary, after weeping for her pal, forger Joe,
who is about to be convicted of murder, falls
weeping again, on the chest of the young
man she married for revenge’s sake.
* * * *
The performance is remarkable for a
strange medley of accents. Some of the
characters talk American, but most of them
seem to forget they are living in Noo Yark.
Miss Muriel Starr, as the unadmirable hero-
ine, is very effective at the outset. . She
puts force into her harangue without raising
her voice, and she bursts into tears without
perceptible effort. But in the later scenes,
where she figures as a commanding adven-
turess in fine raiment, without being able to
show definite cause or reason for her extra-
ordinary transformation, Miss Starr ceases
to be convincing. Other new arrivals are
E. W. Morrison (the Gecko of “Trilby” long
ago), who plays forger Joe in a painfully
subdued manner; Lincoln Plumer. who im-
personates a bullying police inspector in the
stout, aggressive way of a typical commer-
cial drummer; and Miss Mary Worth, well
chosen to represent a Yankee tyne of
“crook.” In her amusing impersonation of
low cunning and vain-glorious criminality
the play utterly condemns itself, for Agnes
Lynch, the “crook” in question, is the hero-
ine’s faithful friend and co-worker. What
a heroine for a popular success ! Eric Maxon
is passable as the soft-headed young waster
son of the Emporium, who becomes impos-
sibly keen-witted and unselfish as soon as
he learns that he has married a spieleress
who has been in gaol. For the rest, there are
Robertshaw, Harry Sw-eeney, Wykeham, and
manv people disguised as crooks and police-
men. Also, bull’s-eye lights, a revolver, and
some excellent stage management. A large
nortion of the audience apueared to be deeply
impressed on Saturday night.

One of these weeks the Burlesque Co., at
the Bijou, will have to move to Sydney, or

On Tuesday last the Le Bruns were due
at the Opera House, but a turn that starts
on a Tuesday is as far distant from a first
notice in this journal as circumstances per-
mit. The latest turns which came within
range of its present issue were the Flying
Banvards and the Jackson Family. The
Banvards are a graceful sextette of gymnasts
who bounce rather than fly, whilst the Jack-
sons are a double quartette of cyclists, who
include one individual performance of note
amidst a lot of combined evolutions that
don’t matter. It is all very clever and
rather wearisome. Trick cyclists these times
are merely sad reminders of the last lot that
came along. Cummin and Seaham fall over
one another as before, also Reynard, the
baritone, refers to his harp-accompanist in a
weird assortment of broken Ingleesh, and
Madge Temple continues to sing sundry dit-
ties with dainty art.

The revival of “A Village Priest” at the
Princess’s on Saturnight provoked vague
regrets in the bosom of an elderly playgoer,
although newercomers, whose critical sense
\vas not lumbered up with fond recollec-
tions, seemed to be highly gratified with the
results. Age and custom do not wither
and stale the excellent stage story which
Grundy borrowed over the back fence, along
with the gridiron, from the French lady next
door; and there is no noteworthy diminution
of George Titheradge’s powers, as the senti-
mental grey clergyman who wears his black
empire gown hitched in the middle, and has
benevolent intentions towards all created
things, _ with the solitary exception of the
blight insects on his rhododendrons (if they
are not tulips or lungwort; this critic is
no specialist in vegetables). In fact, Tith’s
Abb€ grows mellower with successive years,
like old Rhine wine and someone’s cele-
brated soap,' and a mote suave and benig-
nant object never beamed under a white
umbrella. Harry Plimmer’s Armand is
saddled with obesity and years, and is not
the exuberant young lawyer and lover we
have every reason to expect. It is a good
performance, but does not produce quite the
right results.: Miss Day handles the blind
mother gently and graciously, and Mrs.
Brough rep’eats her study of the Comtesse
who slipped—in fact; slid—with her
familiar earnestness. ' 11. R. Roberts’s
Torauenie is unlike Brough’s convict, in
being . too obviously acted. The old play-
goer is not as sorry for the present perse-
cuted person as he was for the former-
harried inqocent. The Jeanne of Lizette
Parkes is a-nice performance, a trifle marred
by a voice that wants modulation and
Lizette’s inability to be convincingly tear-
ful. Madeline and Nanette are received
with chastened gratification at the hands of
Misses Towers and Collins. The season
positively terminates after six nights of “A
Message from Mars,” which gets into opera-
tion on Saturday night.
* * * *
Clara Butt, modified in width, took a
Brunhilde stand on the new platform of the
Auditorium on Saturday night, and showed
Melbourne what she had done to the voice
since six years agone. It seems to have
shed some of its big, sonorous boominess,
but gained, in exchange, some clear sweet-
ness. The ripple is like thin honey, so to
speak, instead of treacle, though there are
occasional burrs in the honey. After two
dirgey Handel numbers, the Star turned the
rest of her programme into “little” songs.
“Early Morning,” by Graham Peel, and
Leoni’s “Leaves and the Wind” were the
type. Debussy’s “Mandoline” seemed to hit
popular taste harder than any of them.
Kennerley Rumford has let high living, or
strenuous paternal duties, or something,
creep into his voice and insidiously sap
some of the bite —that bite of words (with
good white teeth accompaniment) which
was once his most endearing attribute.
Perhaps he nearly got back to his old form
in this direction by driving forth “King
Charles, King Charles” as an encore.
Pianist Murdoch, who suffers rather from
being a pianist in his own country, is other-
wise a host in himself on the platform. He
played a Rachmaninow Prelude in A Flat
and a Chopin Prelude in A Flat. In the
Chopin prelude the oianist came down on
the repeated A Flat in a thunderous, boom-
ing-cannon way that made critical ears
prick up and take, notice, the sound pro-
perties of the new Auditorium being a
marked improvement on what Melbourne is
used to in concert halls. Frank Mowat
Carter, violinist, is another Australian. He
nlayed well he left this country, and
has not apparently learnt much abrawd.
ta if if if
Fried and roast Christian have been on
the menu at West’s, Melbourne, ever since
Nero came in to declaim bad verses to an
enthusiastic populace. Nero and the Chris-
tians are the principal attractions in the film
of “Quo Yadis,” the longest adaptation of
a popular yarn that has ever arrived to
preach piety and humility to West’s congre-
gations. The production takes two hours or
more to unfold. It embodies episodes of
history and imagination, and is crowded
with snapshots of banquets, loose women, and
flowing wine. It presents Nero adding the
insult of his poetry to the injury of the
Homan conflagration, and it provides an
entertainment good enough for any person
to gaze at.

The sheet at Melbourne Majestic last
Saturday was crowded with tragic happen-
ings. “Gold from the Gutter” revealed a
female, torn by conflicting emotions, stalk-
ing a husband with pots of coin. “The
Romance of the Utah Pioneers” took the
spectators to the Amurkan continent, and
dumped them down amongst violence and
bitterness, apparently caused by the white
man’s bad habit of gazing fondly on the
squaw when she is red and charming. This
week the Majestic is screening 2600 ft. of
“The Hidden Message,” a sensational story
of Love laughing at Locksmiths, not to men-
tion motor-cars and balloons.

“The Sunshine Girl,” now at Melbourne
Majesty’s, is probably the weakest show of
its kind that has yet endeavored to develop
public pessimism regarding the future of
comic-opera in the Commonwealth. The
music, at its best, is only moderately melo-
dious,_ and contains nothing sufficiently
haunting to be whistled at a push soiree.
All that one learns from the plot is the
sad case of Vernon Blundell (Frank
Greene), ordered by an old man’s will to
abstain from matrimony for a period on
pain of losing an inheritance of soap works,
soap, and sundries. Vernon, however, de-
feats the will and gets hold of the property
by acts of impersonation, which lead to the
introduction of Lord Bicester (Frank Hol-
land), Floot (Jack Cannot), and one or
two others who, in a series of rather poor
songs and interpolated stunts, do their best
to revive the flagging spirit of the piece.
The whole production howls for assistance
from a capable company, but the present
combination is not strong enough in voice or
dramatic attainments to render first aid
to the afflicted. Jack Cannot, of course,
does lion’s work, but then Jack is a joy, a
shriek and a laugh for ever. His fooling
throughout is a comfort to those of the
audience who are looking for some excuse
for applause. Leslie Holland as Bicester,
the errant lord with a swag of debts, is as
delightful as his somewhat foggy voice will

Blundell. Greene has one popular duet, a

Phil Smith, the little comedian who struck an
Australian audience for the first time in “The
Count of Luxembourg,” at Her Majesty’s (Melb.),
is credited with a disposition to stay here as long
as he can. If he gets a release from a Dublin panto,
engagement for next Christmas he will paint his nose
red and be the dame in the next Williamson panto.
Some actors from Britain are always suggesting that
deathless fame and long, luxurious engagements await
them on their return, but Smith reckons he will be
better off in every way if he can get a steady job
in this country, where life is easier and the climate
more genial.
“Harrison O.”: The Butts are already intruding
their family upon the attention of an enraptured
populace. The music warehouses and illustrated
weeklies in Melbourne are featuring numerous photo-
graphs of the Amazonian contralto and her other
half (or less), surrounded by their joint progeny.
Joy is shown eating an apple at her ma’s feet.
Coy is held by puppa. San Toy perches on mumma’s
ample shoulder. Boy is by her side; Roy on the
other side; Mark Foy on some more side, and every-
thing in the garden is lovely, from the box office
point of view.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Actor James Atholwood (he has prepared a
new and neatly-dovetailed arrangement of the ortho-
dox Shakspearian “Hamlet” for the press, and
deserves to get his improvements tested on the
stage next time a revival comes along) shows solid
ground for his contention that the Prince of Den-
mark was not a bit mad or melancholy, but
thoroughly stage-struck. Atholwood’s case turns
mainly on the question whether an ancient German
play entitled “Fratricide Punished or Prinz Hamlet
of Denmark” was published before or after Shak.
got to work on the historic theme. In “ Fratricide
Punished,” which contains no long soliloquies, nor any
poetic thought, and has strange incidents of its own,
the precious character of Hamlet is stripped of all
the mystery which Shakspeare maniacs nave been
discussing for ages past. Therefore “Jimmy’s”
theory cannot be taken seriously by the learned com-
mentators. If accepted, it would blow all their
controversial pamphlets and papers to the winds and
leave them no more Hamlet matter for discussion.
In “ Fratricide Punished ” the Prince has no sooner
heard the Ghost’s story—a much more feasible story
than appears in Shak.’s version of it —than he
decides to play at being mad in order that he may
do the detective without exciting suspicion. He
loves acting, and has apparently hobnobbed with
all the mummers who ever came his way. Every-
thing is plain and reasonable in this crude German
play, which may have been written before Divine
William invented his wordy hero. But the Shak-
speare Societies are not going to have their beloved
Hamlet made plain and reasonable if they can stop
it.
The infallible correctness of anything in the
theatrical way that comes to Australia stamped with
the London hall-mark is vouched for by a puff para-
graph in a Melbourne Royal programme. Thus:
“It has been said in some quarters that the dressing
of the two early periods (1860 and 1885) in “Mile-
stones” is exaggerated for stage purposes, and does
not faithfully reflect the fashions of the time. Mr.
Julius Knight points out that this could not be so,
for the dressing is an exact replica in every detail
of that of the original production in London, for
which designs were made exactly according to sketches
made of the costunjes worn at the periods men-
tioned.” This is lovely. No doubt was cast on the
correctness of the “Milestones” costumes for the 1860
period, as far as The Bulletin knows. They might
have been designed from London Punch or Family
Herald illustrations of 53 years ago. But a critic
in this paper did protest that Lord Monkhurst’s
short, single-breasted frock coat, worn in the 1885
period, was fully 25 years out of fashion. The
absurdity of it was apparent to any middle-aged
person who remembers the tailoring of 27 years ago,
or can refresh his memory from an old photo, album.
Something very like Monkhurst’s abbreviated garment
can be seen in the caricatures of 1865 or thereabouts.
As for the 1895 outfit of Nancy—-a quick-witted typ-
iste who had married the boss of a London commercial
firm —it must have been designed from a caricature
of an outrageous rural ’Arriet of the period. Nothing
so hideous is discoverable in Punch for 1880-85, and
no existing mother of a grown-up family can produce
a portrait of herself as a young woman looking like
Nancy in the play.
The opening of the Auditorium Concert Hall in
Melbourne came coincidentally with another out-
break of comment on the obstructive fussiness of
Clayton, the metropolitan Town Clerk, under whose
management the confused methods of Melbourne’s
municipal council become worse confounded. Of
Clayton it is now said again, more loudly than
ever, that his policy in town-clerking is to make
himself indispensable to the muddled council by
keeping control of all the detail work in municipal
business. The obstruetiveness of his system was
glaringly revealed when he went away for a few
months’ holiday. During the autocrat’s absence his
office work was more expeditiously performed by
the assistant-clerk. On his return he grabbed the
whole command of work which had for a time been
distributed among minor officials, and the old state
of congestion was restored. These are the allegations
against Mr. Clayton as general mismanager of muni-
cipal affairs for his old conservative firm—the
council. The recently opened Auditorium is the
admitted outcome of his tactics in the letting of the
big hall of his stronghold to concert managers. His
renting system was practically a demand for a
“cut” out of the speculator’s receipts when the
maximum price charged for admission exceeded ss.
He taxed a Melba or a Clara Butt concert because
the tariff of admission was higher than for ordinary
concerts. The Saturday rent charge for any sort of
Town Hall entertainment was higher than the Mon-
day charge. The possibilities of the exactions of the
Town Hall landlord were unlimited as long as Clay-
ton had the agency, and the inevitable result was
the Auditorium, built by J. and N. Tait. Though
it will not necessarily prove a profitable spec, for the
builders as the ground floor of their e : ght-storey
building in Collins-street it will certainly mean a
considerable loss of revenue to the council.
MEN AND RELIGION FORWARD MOVEMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Special Women’s meeting on Wednesday, Melb.
Town Hall, 2.30. Fred B. Smith will speak. The
International Male Quartette will sing.—Age advt. of
the latest Yankee Revivalist—{with collection).
Forward, upward, onward
To the women’s matinge,
And the voice shall lead them manward,
And the baritone shall pray.
Capture the masculine sinner!
With prayers from lips of rose.
Lift his thoughts above mere dinner—
With a girl in a minster close.
Exalt him above the sordid
Old world, with a brown-eyed girl,
With the grace by kisses afforded,
And save his soul with a curl.
But if you’d convert the ladies
Sing hymns that crave and entreat,
Of “Oh to be nothing! nothing!”
And “Only to lie at his feet.”
There’s a special women’s meeting
Where Smith makes the welkin ring
And the choir gives masculine greeting
And the Male Quartette will sing.
N.s.w.

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
Melbourne got a good look, the other day,
at the ‘conscientious objectors’’ who are
dodging drill; and it is likely that fewer tears
will be spilled over them in future. One of
them, who claimed exemption on the ground
of physical unfitness, turned out to be a
prominent League footballer, temporarily
under a cloud for obstreperousness. The
Court laughed, and sent him to the compul-
sory camp at Langwarrin. Following him
on the list of shirkers came an undersized
degenerate who excused himself from attend-
ance beoause he had been convicted of
robbery-in-company. There was nothing for
it, when he raised that plea, but to discharge
him, yet, as. a matter of .cold fact, he seems
to be more in need of a course of military
training and discipline than most young
men.
The English suffragettes, having failed to
secure their object by breaking windows,
biting policemen, assailing Cabinet Minis-
ters with sausages and pigs’ feet, tearing up
golf links, burning down houses, and explod-
ing bombs, have started a destructive cam-
paign among pet dogs. With a pinch of
aconite they slew Miss Cross’s Pekingese
spaniel, alleged to be worth £2OOO, and thev
have since announced their intention of
spreading death and devastation among
cherished curs, the pride of our best families
Herein the suffragettes show a devilish cun-
ning and a profound knowledge of human
nature in high places. There are in London
troops of fat dowagers and long, lank spin-
sters to whom the only thing on earth that
really matters is a finicking tan spaniel
about the size of a French roll, or a wad-
dling poodle—a fatty degenerate with a nink
silk saddle-cloth and ruby earrings. These
pups appear in the parks in droves, walked
out by hired handmaidens, and the energetic
suffragette who got among them with a club
would excite a passion of grief in aristocra-
tic families that no national calamity like
the explosion of Lloyd George could provoke.
The suffragettes will be serving a worthy
double purpose in going to the dogs. While
boosting their cause, they will aim a blow
at the dog-devotion of English high society
—a deplorable craze for curs that is rapidly
deposing the British lion and giving his job
to a tripe-hound.
“Rob” pictures a no-license M.L. town -
In Invercargill the Wowser ranteth and the coun-
tenance of Bung is not. For three general elections
Invercargill has hugged to itself the delusion of No-
license ; while on each occasion the electorate of
Awarua, which lies hard up to the town boundary,
has declared for the twin indiscretions of More Beer
and lou Ward. To the north of Invercargill, 50yds.
on the Awarua side of the boundary, is a series of
healthy-looking brewery depots, where kegs of beer
may be purchased. The industries of supplying In-
vercargill's demand for beer have their headquarters
in Bluff, Wallacetown, Dunedin and the brewery de-
pots referred to, and it is a notorious fact that
several fortunes have already been made out of the
business. Figures show that Invercargill puts as
much cur-r-sed drink out of sight now as it did in
the bad old days when liquor was sold over the bars
—probably more, because the average Invercargill
“nut” loathes the local brand of Wowser, and will
often buy a keg and cherish it lovingly just to
show that he is a gay young dog with opinions of
his own.
The blight of Wowserdom being upon the town,
it is not considered respectable to be seen in posses-
sion of a keg. If a man walks along the street
carrying even a handbag the shopkeeping Wowsers
breathe heavily into their whiskers, and wrestle
within themselves for that man’s soul. To such
lengths is Wowserism carried that the Women’s
Christian Temperance Union is boycotting the newly-
installed tram service because there is a whisky
advt. on the tickets. The members of the Union
decline to take the tickets from the conductors, and
the ticket inspectors eject them with enthusiasm.
The various Wowser organisations are making a fuss
about this, and it looks as if they will win out in
the end.
The numerous part of Invercargill which tries to
reconcile a thirst for beer with a desire to dwell in
an aroma of respectability is driven to quaint de-
vices. A carpenter cycling in from the north with
his kit thrown over his shoulder offers nothing new
in the way of a sensation; but the initiated Inver-
cargill person at once smells surreptitious beer. A
carter who lives near the middle of the town does a
big business bringing in loaded beer-kegs from the
depots; but he is not permitted to deliver them to
any of his customers. I stood near his house one
Sunday, and watched the said timid souls coming
round for their liquor. It was a sunny morning, *
but a surprising number of persons who drifted
casually up the carter’s right-of-way wore overcoats.
Two or three men came along with a halter in one
hand and a feed-bag in the other, and dropped in to
see their friend, the carter. When they departed, it
invariably appeared as if the feed-bags were more
difficult to carry. A couple strolled along in an
anxious kind of way, and ducked up the right-of-
way when the street was comparatively empty. They
were wheeling a go-cart, and when in a few minutes
they sneaked off again with their Sunday keg covered
up among baby’s bedding, they looked no more than
they professed, to be —a couple of eminently respect-
able counterjumpers taking the youngest infant out
for an airing.
To such hypocrisy is a community reduced when
the Wowser gets the upper snuffle!
THE LIMIT IN
ECCENTRICITY.
SHE: "Clara is now
wearing a watch on her shoe."
HE: “I wonder will she ever be capable of
wearing her heart on her sleeve?"
‘Croson : I paused the other day to ad-
mire the foresight and sagacity of a cycliste.
hhe looked between 20 and 30, was nicely
dressed, and was propelling her machine
along a fairly-busy _ Melbourne street at a
smart pace. Seated in a basket-like contrap-
tion on the handle bars in front of her, and
strapped thereto, was a small infant of about
two. I don’t know whether it was her own
child or a hired one, neither does it matter;
but the arrangement struck me as most pru-
dent. In the event of a collision with a tram-
car, motor-car, elephant, fat landlord or
other massive obstacle, the infant would act
as a buffer and save the rider’s nicely adorned
countenance from damage. Also should the
bike collapse, skid or otherwise come to grief
the child would most likely be underneath and
the lady s beautiful clothes would be kept un-
muddied to a large extent. I don’t know
what the infant thought of the matter- it
wasn’t old enough to tell.

“0.K.”: A parson wrote to the Presby-
terian Assembly, in Melbourne, last week :
I am convinced, from personal observation, that
the church-going habits of many church people on
holiday are deplorable, but most likely they are
equally unsatisfactory in their attendance at their
own church at home.
“Deplorable” seems a harsh term to apply
to church people’s habits of church-going at
holiday times. Having no business to attend
to and being bored to death, perhaps, by
their co-boarders at the. particular holiday
resort where they are getting sunburnt, they
naturally go to church if no congenial picnic
intervenes. Thus they avoid the necessity
for talking when spoken to. Only one per-
son speaks in church, and he doesn’t expect
to be answered back. For people on holiday
the little tabernacle is often the only place
within easy distance of the boarding-house
where they can sleep without risk of being
disturbed for an hour or two. Why, then,
refer to their church-going habits as “deplor-
able,” and suggest that “their attendance at
church at home is equally unsatisfactory”?
Church-going in town is unpardonable as
compared with the same indiscretion at a
holiday place. Personally, I never do it ex-
cept when I am away from my little sphere
of labor, and I resent the insinuation that I
am equally unsatisfactory at other times. If
the Presbyterian shepherd meant to insult
my sort of sheep, I am somewhat insulted.
I say “ba-a” to him. But perhaps I have
taken his meaning too grammatically.
* * * *
From information received, as the police
say, the writer is inclined to think that
there is somewhere a leakage of newchum
Chows into the Commonwealth. The lady
who purchases the vegetables for the house-
hold has no White Australia prejudices, but
she is seriously thinking of turning down
her yellow cabbage merchant and telling him
never to darken the back door again. It is
all on account of a baffling and incoherent
assistant whom lie has lately acquired. The
new sub-John doesn’t know one vegetable
from another in plain or fancy English,
and he juggles in a sad, crazed fashion with
small change, and shakes his head, and sighs
unprofitably in Cantonese, till the busy lady
of the house is almost hysterical with impati-
ence. His bland owner—or trainer —in re-
sponse to angry protests, begs consideration
tor the cheerless wretch on these totally un-
sufficient grounds : “Him newchum, Missus;
him _learn soon, you see.” Now, has that
coadjutor-John been preserving his massive
ignorance for years amongst the Australian
cabbage beds, or has he brought it fresh and
untarnished, from Cathay?
[Ernest O’Ferrall, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.)
From a Melbourne man lately back after
two years in London :
If Melbourne thinks it will shift a big load of in-
convenience by making itself a subway under Flin-
ders-street for the converging railway mob, Mel-
bourne is mistaken. Experience in the Fog and else-
where convinces that subways, while useful for a
timid few, are no good to the average hurrying,
reckless citizen. There is no necessity for subways
anywhere in this city. What it should do is to call
upon its railway gods with a loud voice, to do their
duty. Melbourne’s is about the only great terminus
in the world with two narrow openings (the one
facing Elizabeth-street is merely a slit) for its cus-
tomers to come and go by. It is about the only
terminus I know, except a couple hidden in a valley
in Edinburgh, where trains are not brought practi-
cally level with the streets. Also, it is the only
terminus in my good and wide acquaintance of rail-

LOST SOULS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Victorian Presbyterian General Assembly, sit-
ting in Melbourne, expatiated on the particular
wickedness of boarding-house life in big cities. The
peculiar iniquities of boarders were not recited, but
ministers were exhorted to keep a sharp look-out
upon “the increasing peril of boarding-house life.”
The pastors still are wondering
Wherefor the boarder’s blood is rash;
What is the lodger’s special sin ;
Why evil-doing thrives on hash ;
Why homes for unattached should be
Such hot-beds of iniquity ;
Although inclined to take the view,
It’s partly owing to the stew.
The boarder is a wretch profane:
He really never goes to church;
He “hops the window” oft for gain,
And leaves the Gorgon in the lurch.
When angered by a leaking roof
His oaths are fifty over-proof.
Twould clot your blood to hear him whoop
When he gets cold and wat’ry soup.
He lacks a mother’s gentle sway,
A sister’s sympathetic tone,
So fiercer grows, as day by day,
He fights the keeper for his bone.
She’s tough, and sour, and sly, and grim,
And ever has her knife in him.
In all the doorways, lurking see
The perils of propinquity.
That early breakfast, and the “girl”
Slip-slopping like a sodden ghost,
The butter with its hair in curl,
The coffee made of ruined toast.
The frosted chop, the wiry steak,
The egg you're terrified to break,
The milk where sotted flies transgress
These things make not for holiness.
Then luncheon brings not peace, but war.
A brutal charge with bayonets
The dinner is. A room-mate’s snore
Makes night a round of dull regrets.
No wonder, then, as saints attest,
That sinners wild and free infest
The boarding-house; for, truth to tell,
A boarding-house is just like Hell.
VICTORIA.

The preachment of "10-2-4” :
For weeks and weeks I have been reading with a
sort of pleasurable horror about the Sydney crime
wave. In a deadly dull life, from which all the glow
and color seem to be swiftly fading, two things have
still retained the power to thrill me —strange dogs
and crime waves. Nothing makes me realise that
there are points about living after all, so much as
a sudden encounter with a large, leaping, tenor-
voiced dog. If I am alone in the dark with an
animal of this sort, and I have no gun, and there
is no fence, or tree, or tower, or coal hole, or trap-
door handy, the desire to live becomes an absolute
ecstasy.
Of late I have been getting a lot of cheap excite-
ment out of the crime wave. For example, I spent
a recent week-end with some friends at Wahroonga.
After dinner we gathered round the fire in the hall,
and while the rain fell in buckets and the wind
blithered miserably outside, we discoursed on Crime.
We spoke of Peace and Deeming and Wainwright
and Crippen and other classic malefactors. We de-
scribed typical burglars that we had known (in
illustrations) —immense, ape-like ruffians with prog-
nathous jaws, jemmies, eyes lijce jet beads, and fur
caps, revolvers, etc. Then came the locking-up of
the house-—a glorious moment. Perhaps the fortifi-
cation of the house would be a better way to describe
it. Door after door was laboriously secured, not only
by means of turned keys, but with all sorts of
ingenious bolts and bars. The various electrical appli-
ances for ringing bells, firing rockets and guns,
and, in other respects, giving interlopers the sur-
prise of their lives were adjusted. Revolvers were
handed round by our General—l mean our host. My
room looked over the front drive. I undertook to
destroy, or attempt to destroy, with my “gun” any
burglar whom I noticed either entering or leaving the
citadel by that route. Quaking with delightful terror,
we went to bed.
I fear now that this joy has gone out of my life
for good. A detective of my acquaintance put me
in the way of viewing a couple of our desperadoes
last week. They represented, I learned, the class
that has been “terrorising the community” (Sydney
Telegraph). They were samples of “the skilled
and daring criminals who have deliberately decided
to pit their nerve and brains against the organised
forces of society” (S. M. Herald). I was presented,
first, to a boss of the Sikes profession.
He was palpably half-witted. A shambling, anaemic
lad, with shifty eyes and spots on his chin. If I
found him burgling any house that I was interested
in my first impulse would be to offer him a few
chocolates or a glass of lemonade, to go away. It
would be cruel to kick him. and to do worse would
be like ill-treating a consumptive. As a result of
playing a leading part in the crime wave for some
months _he is about 7d. ahead of the game. A sorry
record for any boss. I was taken next to a
murder-suspect. In the matter of intelligence I
would put him on a par with the other. As re-
gards physique, any lightweight could tear him to
pieces. Of courage and resource, judging by his past
performances, he is destitute. Such a man as this
lets off a gun more from nervousness than anything
else. (Indeed, detectives have told me that the
efficient burglar hardly ever uses a pistol, or even
carries one. In the more or less recent case of
Digby Grand and Jones, where two men were
hanged forXthe shooting of one constable, the police
based their opinion that the deed had been done by
Jones, on the grounds that Jones was a highly-strung
character! and Grand wasn’t.)
And so we arrive at the disillusioning conclusion
that the.’leading characters in our Crime Wave are
neither Professor Moriarty’s nor Bill Sikes’s. They
are mental and physical weaklings who have onlv
taken to lawlessness because they lack the strength
to wield a pick or the brains to sell a pair of socks.
Their desires, as regards loot, are modest for the most
part—a few pence or an old suit of clothes. Let
the next householder who hears one of them falling
over the doormat, or overturning the furniture,
in quest of booty, observe his visitor narrowly
through a keyhole, and then go back to bed. The
police will secure his goods in the morning, together
r with the thief. And there will be no uproar, no
shooting, no unpleasantness of any sort. It is a
depressing thing to have to say after a study of the
daily papers has filled one with so many splendid
visions of bellow-dramatic robbers ; but there is little
doubt that the Sydney malefactor is quite harmless
provided he is not frightened, and he is so traeri-
callv stunid that he is abound to be arrested the
moment the police start after him in a serious spirit.

wealth offices in London will be laid next

The discriminating thief who carefully
marks down some suitable booty and goes
after it with steeled nerves is a rare animal.
Most thieves are indiscriminating, jumpy
persons who suffer sudden lusts for propertj
whenever they see unguarded articles lying
around. Every day or so, there is a hue and
cry raised after some flying fool who has
lifted some inappropriate trifle. A hot pie
merchant, who had sold out all his meat
bombs, recently went into a Sydney pub for
a drink, and left his pie urn in the parlor.
The usual acquisitive ass was hovering
round, and the pub was presently shaken to
its cellar by angry howls. The ass had fled
with the pie machine —a cumbersome pro-
fessional apparatus quite useless to any lay
feeder —and the enraged craftsman went
bounding on his trail, hitting the pavement
on an average only three times in every
hundred yards. When the pursuer was close
at hand, the fugitive dropped his accursed
burden; but the pieman fled on after him,
knocked him down, and sat on him panting
till the police bundled along to collect their
prisoner. Even supposing the theft had
been successful, the urn would not have
pawned for much; and' the thief could hardly
hope to start in or carry on the hot-pie busi-
ness locally without fear of detection, the
hot-pie profession filing a select late calling
which has to be carried on when policemen
are most watchful and numerous. Unless
he had some idea of starting in Europe, or
selling his pies anonymously through the
post, he might as well have fled with a
policeman’s helmet, or dashed off with the
wardrobe of a barrel-organist’s monkey.

The recent lowering of the Melbourne-
to-Sydney motor record was written up
everywhere in heroic fashion. Two men and
a moving gas engine had fled from one ab-
surdly large city in the ghost-walking hours,
and had whirred and bounded over 572 miles
of “bush road” in 10 hours and two minutes.
At the very end of their perilous rush up
and down hill, round hair-raising turns, past
and over real and imaginary obstacles, they
skimmed, on scarred tyres and mud-caked
wheels, into —the next bloated metropolis!
It is all very splendid in a muddy, reckless
fashion, and it suggests the dauntless oily
rush of Capital from one lonely frontier post
to another, with the tiger of Sosh leaping
and clawing at the back seat. But, when all
the mud has been scraped off and the tyre
bill paid, it is merely a reminder of thre'e
dreary facts, i.e., that it is a long way be-
tween cities in Australia, that said cities
are absurdly bloated and expensive when
you get to them, and that the “bush” roads
between are d —d bad.
IN THE LIGHT OF RECENT E VENTS.
Sleuth: "Excuse me , but you didn't 'appen

laig and a rumberella , didyer?”
For years past every State of Australia
has stuck out its chest at a tremendous rate
and boasted about the diminution of its gaol
population. This sort of diminution can be
brought about in either of two ways—by hav-
ing fewer or by having a police
force that can’t catch criminals. Recent
developments in Sydney and Melbourne sug-
gest that the reduction of the gaol popula-
tion has been overdone. What these two
cities want just now is a tremendous increase
of their gaol population.

It is an offence, punishable by fine, to be
a corpse in Victoria nowadays. At Ballarat
the other day one Ward was charged £2 tor
non-attendance as a juror. Subsequently
it was pointed out that as \1 ard resides in
Heaven, it was hardly a fair thing to expect
him to come to Victoria for a change. In
Heaven the streets are gold, while Ballarat
as a goldfield is about as dead as the boots
of Moses. Judge Box appeared to think
there was something in this argument, and
since the law’s chance of collecting the £2
was like the loan of a Central American
republic the impost was remitted. If the
offence is repeated a less lenient view will,
however, be taken.

An’ no offence to you, ole cock, I’m grateful all the
same !
I think I’ll take a trip downstairs while things are
all sublime,
An’ overhaul yer sideboard —if you’ll only gim’me
time!
But if yer wake afore I go, I’ll fill yer up wiv lead!
Or bash yer silly brains out wiv a bag of sand in-
stead !
I plavs me part—l takes me risk—an’ so, of course,
I’ll fight!
And by yer laws of “precerdent” ain’t I a pufflek
right ?
I knows a dozen cobbers in th’ shootin’-iron brigade
Who’ve dealt out stoush an’ saved their necks, an’ so
I ain’t afraid!
There ain’t no word for murder now in this ’ere
Southern clime ,
’Cept mentally deficient! — an’ they’ll only gim’me
time ! !
N.s.w.

A DORNCE IN RABBITLAND.
M.C.: “ After the next dornce there'll be a interval of twenty
minutes till the blokes of the committee 'as a look round
ther rabbit traps."
“Xox”: Flying Fox Island, in Broadsound
(Q.), the place of mighty tides, is infested with the
stinking beast that gives it its name. At night the
foxes leave their mangrove fastness and spread them-
selves over the contiguous mainland, devouring and
ruining the fruit on , the few farms that exist in
this well-nigh man-forsaken spot. Not long ago the
sons of St. Lawrence armed themselves with shot-
guns, and set out to blow the stigma from the earth.
Arrived at the island, they left the boats and ad-
vanced on the foxerr'. Before them hung hideous
hordes, resting from the nocturnal activities of the
night before. The firing party crept as close as their
protesting nostrils would take the risk, and fired a
volley into the suspended rottenness. At the report,
thousands of foxes rose chattering and gibbering in
all directions, depositing dreadful mementos of the
occasion upon the representatives of St. Lawrence.
Nauseated, the men rushed into the water (which
was curdling under the stench), and, flinging them-
selves into the boats, seized an oar with one hand
and a nose with the other and fled. Since then, the
abomination of Flying Fox Island has been left to
blush unseen upon the desert air, or words to that
effect.
“Polstan” : The reply of “Unumgar” (B.
17/4/T3) to “D.R.Y.’s” question as to why bees
leave a stored hive, satisfies me that he is the local
bottle-o. He says that the bees swarm out, leaving
only the young bees that are still in the cell to carry
on, or words to that effect. Thej r don’t. If a hive is
left to itself and allowed to swarm it will generally
throw off three swarms in a season, the old queen
accompanying the first and biggest flight. The other
swarms may follow in the course of a week, but this
depends a good deal upon the strength of the mother
colony, supply of young queens or queen cells, and the
honey or pollen supply. In healthy conditions, a
good working colony, with a full supply of stores
and larvae, a laying-queen, young or “nurse” bees
and field bees, remains to continue business at the
old address after the departure of the swarms. With
a good honey flow the numbers soon increase enorm-
ously, the queen laying from two to three thousand
eggs daily, if she is of a good breed. Beekeepers,
though, prevent swarming as much as possible, and
there are strains that have had the inclination to
burst up the happy ’ome bred out of them. Such bees
do not create new queens yearly, but build queen
cells only when the old lady has, through age, become
incapable of providing sufficient eggs to keep up the
hive numbers. They then construct the cells afore-
mentioned, which the old queen tears down as rap-
idly as they are made if not prevented, knowing that
if she permits a young queen to come into being the
latter will search for her as soon as she emerges, a
royal princess, from her egg-prison, and kill her.
But sometimes if the old queen persists in destroy-
ing the efforts of the colony to supersede her, the
bees will “ball” and smother her. Then they will
draw out a few ordinary cells, into which the poor
victim has just deposited eggs, and in a few weeks
there is a coronation. If possible, though, bees avoid
following this course, for it means that they lose
enormously in numbers, owing to no eggs being laid
in the hive from the day of the dethronement till the
new queen is mated and laying. It is generally recog-
nised that “superseding” queens are positively the
best, providing the old one will permit the young
queen to be properly produced at the convenience
of the workers ; but when she won’t play the game,
and compels them to smother her, they make the new
ruler from the oldest larvae that will answer the pur-
pose in order to hurry the business up, and the result-
ing queen is often no good at all. Black bees never
supersede, and among them a queen has a good
chance of dying of old age. If she does the trick in
the middle of winter, when queen-raising is an im-
possibility, the colony dies out as soon at it begins
to fly in the spring. It may leave good stores be-
hind, too, and it is possible that this was the fate
of the occupants of the provisioned hive that
“D.R.Y.” found.
“Kikern” : The natives of the Pnrari are,
to my mind, the most besotted and hopeless in the
whole of known Papua. They are first cousins to
the people of Goaribari, who slaughtered Tamati (Dr.
Chalmers) and Rev. Tomkins, of the London Mission-
ary Society. Jet black, dwarfish, always filthy, with
deep-set eyes, fingers bony and long-nailed (retnind-
ful of the talons of the vulture), enormous mouths
and feet. They are, of course, inveterate betel-nut
chewers. The scarlet saliva from this nut (they
chew lime with it) slobbers down over them. Their
mangy bleary-eyed dogs lap at the bowls of food set
down to the men, who may or may not bother to
“shoo” the curs away. Nauseous, decaying pieces of
pig pelt or dog hide are worn on the breasts. Newly-
“married” girls wear nothing but a coat of mud,
daubed over them from head to foot. I’ve seen some
revolting sights in these parts in the way of disease.
I think a squad of medical men (or executioners)
should be continually travelling through the villages
between Daru and Oricolo. This is principally
swamp country. The people pervade themselves in
filthy straw hovels, built on piles over the ooze,
where their pigs can be heard wallowing beneath
their huts. Whenever they kill anything for food
they employ the methods calculated to be most
painful. They revel and jump about in frenzy at the
screeching of a tortured pig or whatever it may be.
These are the characters referred to by some mem-
bers of the Commonwealth Parliament as “poor, un-
sophisticated children of nature.” So they are —I
don’t think.

“Eddyson”: He was called Abbott the
Charmer, but he didn’t look it. He was a fat man,
coated with ancient grease and ornamented at one
extremity with massed whiskers of the kind which,
when lain on, remained bent all day. Pat Abbott’s
powers as a charmer were not directed for the edifi-
cation of his kind, but to beguile snakes from their
lurking places about homes and houses. Pat’s beat
was over a large snake-infested tract in Gippsland.
The charmer would drift to a farm-house, and lurk
till the housewife came in sight. Housefathers were
less apprehensive and touchy about the ancient enemy
of our race. “Any snakes to-day, missus?” was the
Charmer’s invariable appeal, and in nine cases out
of 10 Pat was put on to try his wiles. His method
was simple. He walked round the house on a voyage
of investigation, then planked down, drew out an old
flute, and piped “The Wearin’ of the Green” untir-
ingly. Sooner or later he turned up with his snake
(sometimes two) in a bag, and took his fee and
passed on. There was no deception about Abbott
the Charmer. Scores of people had carefully in-
spected while he charmed the reptiles, and had seen,
with their own eyes, the snake (sometimes two)
come wriggling from under the house, hypnotised
by the melody, and drift straight into Pat’s bag.
These were always eager to testify on Abbott’s be-
half, and his reputation was high in the land. The
Charmer never played anything but “The Wearin’ of
the Green.” He had no faith in the efficacy of any
other air. “ 'Twas Sint Pathrick himself give it the
right twisht,” said he. Sometimes, where the living
was good and the bunks comfortable, it would take
Abbott the Charmer three or four days to get his
snake, but he never failed. It was understood that
he was immune to snake venom ; but the day he was
put on to charm black snakes off the premises at
Binyip this delusion was dispelled. Pat had been
doing the Pied Piper act for about an hour, when
suddenly the fluting eased, displaced by the roarings
of a strong man in his agony. Abbott the Charmer
was mad with terror. He had been bitten, he said.
He showed his marks, two distinct points in his left
HIS MOSCOW.
forearm. White to the lips, and howling dreadfully,
Pat demanded drastic treatment. He got it. It
was shocking maltreatment, all present being ignor-
ant of the exact rules for the salvation of the snake-
bitten. However, after two weeks in hospital Abbott
was out and about again. He had had a narrow
escape, and h : s occupation was gone. For the first
time he had lured a strange snake out of its hiding
place in mistake for one or both of his own trained
and docile vipers.
“Leo Lear”: I was once under the im-
pression that the small fresh-water yabbie that bur-
rows through irrigation banks, and tunnels down ;n
flat, damp ground and swamps, was the champion
deep miner in Nature, and that he bored to the
summer level of underground water 30ft. below.
But he does nothing of the kind. Lately I was put-
ting down a post in low-lying ground, and had just
reached the 3ft. mark, when the crowbar poked the
bottom out of the hole and went into a foot of
nothing. I thought I’d tapped Hell, and stood
ready to hit his Nibs on the horns and tail with
the shovel. But only water came up out of a kind
of well with yabbie holes leading into it. As the
surrounding earth was dry, and I knew there was no
water within 20ft. of the surface, I made inquiries,
and learned some facts about the yabbie. He can’t
live without drink, and, of course, flourishes when
the liquid element covers the land in the wet and
windy winter. As summer approaches, and before
the water subsides, the yabbies prepare to meet the
coming drought. A colony of 50 or 60 will work
on the co-operative principle, and make a. clay-lined
water-holding cavity, about a yard underground.
Every surface hole in the locality will connect with
one of these wells, .and here the inhabitants live
throughout the hot, dry summer months until the
winter rains bring a fresh supply of moisture. If
you happen to tap one of these reservoirs •in the dry
season, all the inmates retreat up the tunnels, and
all you see of them is a muffled laugh as the back
door slams in the distance.
“Mimosa” : The best horsemen I know are
the Boers. They get every ounce out of their horses
without unduly knocking them about. Whoever saw
a Boer, after riding his horse to a “sweat,” turn it
out in the cold to cool itself? - Upon unsaddling he
will lead it until dry, even if it takes an hour.
Whereas the Australian, as a rule, rips the saddle
off, slips the bridle, and with, a swirl brings it over
the horse’s rump, as an
intimation that it is
knock-off time. No Boer
would think of riding a
gelding hard with o u t
pulling up and dismount-
ing every two hours for
a few minutes. A stal-
lion or mare can go five
hours, but that is not the
case with a gelding. I'd
like the address of 1000
Australian horsemen who
understand that. At pre-
sent I know the addresses
of 11.
“A. Borig” : I opine this will be new r s to
a herd of bushmen. With an old hand I was over-
taken by a thunderstorm with severe lightning ac-
companiments. Old Bill looked round, and then
made for a tree leaning at a sharp angle. I made
tracks for the open, and yelled to him to come out
if he had any further use for his misspent life.
“That’s where you make the m'stake, sonny,” he
yelled back. “I’ve been in hundreds of (blanky)
lightnin’ storms in the (same again) bush, and I've
never seen a (ditto) leanin’ tree struck yet.” He
camped under that one safely, anyhow.
MACPHERSON SADDLES MIDNIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Macpherson leads the muster
Any crock or colt will do
To canter up the river flats
Or thread the mulga through ;
But when the mob is rounded,
And the fats are to divide,
Macpherson saddles Midnight
As the only horse to ride.
He slips into the saddle
Like a girl into a cha'r,
And horse and man are mingled
In a moment when he’s there 1
They move among the cattle
And they edge a beast away—
Macpherson’s up on Midnight,
And the band begins to play !
The victim’s looking nasty,
And he drops a sideway horn ;
But the steer to bluff Macpherson
And his black horse isn’t born !
The fourteen-footer whistles
As the old horse shoots aside;
And Midnight, when lie’s wheeling,
Takes a man like Mac to ride!
The bullock bellows at the thong
That lashes him like wire,
And launches out for liberty
With every nerve on fire;
But though he had the West wind’s pace,
Or flew as black-duck fly,
When Mac is riding Midnight
They can beat him and go by!
The bullock knows his master,
And, see! he stops to wheel!
But Midnight, as a shadow turns,
Turns with him heel to heel!
A man might measure half a rod
Along that double slide
Where Midnight, on his shoeless hoofs
Has checked his racing stride.
A wild scrub steer has little sense,
But Well and he knows
The horse that has the foot of him,
The whip that finals him blows!
And if you want to see him beat
In proper, stockman style.
Just watch old Mac on Midnight,
For it’s worth a fellow’s while !
SCOTLAND.

“0.C.C.”: The carter was very indignant.
“Blime,” he said, “it gives me the fair ’ump! ’Ere
1 am, sent to the Dotty ’Ouse at Gladesville to cart
bricks for old Gravel’s job on the new laundry. Fust
load—a big ’un —I gets into the yard an’ looks round
for someone to show where it’s to go. There ain’t
a soul about, ’cos it was early mornin’, I s’pose;
but I roots about an’ finds a cove with a quiet way,
and an air of some authoritee. ‘Ah,’ ’e says, ’me
man, you’ll be bringin’ these bricks for the noo
biler-’ouse ?’ ‘Yes,’ I says, ‘I s’pose so.’ ‘Well, just
stack ’em ’ere,’ ’e says, pointin’ to a place. Well,
wouldjer b’leeve me, ’ardly ’ad I got ’em out ’an
stacked neatly against the fence, Nve’n up bustles a
important-lookin’ bloke, an’ asks, ‘Wot ’ave we
’ere?’ ‘Bricks,’ I says, ‘for Gravel’s job’, short like,
an’ I gets out me docket for the fust bloke to sign.
I seen ’im sniggerin’, an’ the second bloke ’e gives
’im a push like, an’ says to ’im to get out. Then ’e
turns to me an’ takes me docket. ‘lt’s all right,
me boy,’ ‘e says, ‘only you’ve bin an’ put ’em in the
wrong yard.’ ‘Wrong yard, be !’ I says, gettin’
wet. ‘This yere cove giv me direckshuns’ ‘Don’t
take any notice of ’im!’ says the chap, ’e’s only a
patient. ’E’s ’umbuggin’ you!’ Well, strike me,
Id been ’ad. So I puts the bricks back in me cart,
and me an’ the ole ’orse follers the bloke carryin’
me book, an’ ’e waves me right across to another
place an’ says, important-like, ‘Put ’em there,
please!’ So I puts ’em there and gets me docket
signed ; an’ I was just turnin’ away w’en up comes
a bloke in a ’urry and a uniform. ‘Now, then, you
two,’ ’e says, ‘wot dy’er mean by running in ’ere
Git back to your ward, at once! And ’oo are yout
’e asts me. W’en I tells ’im ’e laughs fit to split,
an’ me if those loonies didn’t join in!
Id been ’ad again!”
“Frae”: “Mallarapoonie” (B. 24/4/’l3)
serves up a lot of misinformation, mixed with techni-
cal terminology. Allow me to inform him that gun-
powder is not a compound at all, much less a potas-
sium compound. To make gunpowder, you take some
charcoal, some sulphur, and some potassium nitrate,
reduce each to a powder, and simply mix them to-
gether with your finger or your wooden leg, or any-
thing else handy. Fixed proportions of each must
be used to get the best results. This gives a dusty
fine-grained powder that is converted into a coarse
grained powder, by various treatments that cause
the fine particles to stick together, in larger or smal-
ler grains. But the result is a mixture, and not a
compound. In the explosion, the charcoal or the
sulphur particle has to go to the nitrate for its
oxygen, and the nitrate particle, no matter how inti-
mately the powder is mixed, is, chemically speaking,
miles away, so the explosion is comparatively slow.
You can adulterate gunpowder by adding charcoal,
so that it burns so slowly as to merely sizzle. Nor
are potassium compounds slow, sb’nt few of these are
used, and the presence of potassium chlorate is for-
bidden unless it is kept separate, and used as soon
as prepared, as in rack-a-roelt. “Mall,” etc., men-
tioned guncotton as being slower than the nitrate
compounds. Gun-cotton is a nitrate. Again it is a
question of adulteration. Nitro-glycerine is a liquid,
dangerous, and hard to handle, but, adulterated with
a particular kind of earth, it is tamed down very
considerably, and used as dynamite. Guncotton,
which is a nitrate of cotton wool, is mixed with
nitro-glycerine to make a compound known as blast-
ing gelatine. Also, gun-cotton mixed with nitro-
glycerine in a special fashion, makes a jelly, which
SUCCEEDED.
WIFE: “You were drunk last night and you
forgot yourself. Why did you get drunk? "
UBBY: “To forget myself."
is squeezed through a perforated plate into long
threads which go by the name of cordite. The
ability of most of these to get off the mark depends
on the quantity of nitro-glycerine present, or how it
is present. Nitro-glycerine spread over a large sur-
face—say, smeared over a sheet of paper—will burn
like kerosene, but the same drop in a thimble is
concentrated sheol. All these explosives are made
up with a view to the special use to which they are
to be put. As for fulminate of mercury, nobody
but a fool would dig a nail or file handle into a per-
cussion cap or any other suspicious-looking copper
tube. If any word at all is written about an ex-
plosive, it should be strictly accurate, and perhaps
the above knowledge of what is in some of these
universally used compounds and mixtures will cause
more careful handling.

“Pick”: Brolga’s rough-haired tyke was
boss dog among the mongrel nondescripts about tne
sawmill until the day he undertook to chew up Wil-
loughby’s new bullock-heeler. Then, after a long
and frothy battle, he threw up the championship.
Brolga, dispirited by the loss of fame, made excuse
for his dog’s defeat. “I bin think it,” he said, “my
plurry dog would won that fight all right, only”—
and Brolga rubbed his head to find a way out—“only
for that other plurry dog.”
“S. S. Severn”: Off Ela Beach (Papua),
at low tide, the reef stands high and dry tor almost
a mile. Upon it great branches of gorgeous-hued
corals, rose-scarlet, purple, saffron and jade, glimmer
in the sun’s rays like Orient gems. Shells,
most beautiful and rare, glow softly.
Lovely anemones blossom in blood-crim-
son, green and ultra-marine; weird bSche-
de-mer, black, grey and brown-streaked,
lies in shallow pools; and star-fish, 2ft.
in diameter, and hued bright-blue and
every blend of puce, decorate the cran-
nies of the rock. Further out gape white
giant clams, 12ft. and more across. Du-
gongs sport with their young and take
the air of moonlight nights, and nibble
soft, sea grass that grows in patches.
Turtles laze on the wave tops, and strange
fish, with curious markings, come up
and stare with great goggling eyes. Sea
snakes, striped and spotted, curl in the
trough of the waves, and great crocodiles
and sharks lie in wait for business. Also
there are octopuses, stinging rays and
squids. I saw one squid off Douka Island
which, above the surface of the water,
was as big as a billiard table. The boat
boys said it was the Devil, and I put the
rifle down. If it had been a missionary
I’d have fired every cartridge in the boat
at him.
“Leo Lear” : Re “Arm” and other scribes
with their various, descriptions of Binghi’s fish-
slaying capacity. Let me give them part of the fish-
gospel as practised by that numerous order—the
Australian nipper. When I ran wild with that clan
around the rough cliffs of Southern Vic., we caught
crayfish by the ton, by hand. Certain pools, access-
ible only in calm weather and at low tide, were
meeting places for all the red-plated crays in the
locality. Our first procedure was to secure a parrot-
fish that had attained a correct degree of unpleasant-
ness, and tie the animal on the end of a rope, haw-
ser or string. After scrambling down the cliffs, one
of the party would approach a pool stealthily on
hands and knees; lie flat out, and dangle the mal-
odorous parrot down among the caves and kelp. A
party of crays would immediately appear to see
wil at part of Footscray had wandered into their
kitchen. Then one would take a holt on the bait, and
the youth above would gently draw the hawser up.
Directly the long claws of the cray came above
water, a waiting hand closed on them, and with a
smart lift the drunk’s pal and provender would be
thrown backwards, to where the rest of the party
were waiting to grapple with and bag him. The
parrot-fish would again descend into the deep, and
the order be repeated until the remaining crays
became canny. Then we moved to the next pool.
There was excitement in it, too, as the octopus had
a fancy for the deep, rocky holes. Also, blind
rolleis frequently washed the captive era vs back to
the briny: what time drenched vouths should have
been thanking Providence that they also were not
bound oceanwards.
‘‘Sydney Sailor” writes from an Egyptian
port: Met “Vagabond” in The Bulletin the other
night at Safaja Bay, and he desired a remedy for
the cockroach curse aboard ship. Powdered borax
is the article. Two years ago my ship was literally
alive with the crawlers, and I could find no remedy
until a pilot on the Hugli River (India) cursed
the swarming soup in 10 dialects and seven forgotten
languages. When he stopped for breath we had
tied up at Calcutta, and there we bought borax.
The powder was freely laid about the runs and
haunts of the vermin, and within a week we couldn’t
have swapped a roach for a dancing-girl. Haven’t
seen one since (I mean a cockroach, not a dancing-
girl).

fingers into the marine wolf's
eyes, which caused the ani

for help, and the black sud-
denly remembered an engage-
ment he had on shore. The
pictures show that the shark
had inflicted terrible wounds
on the boy’s throat, and the
flesh on the face and shoul-
ders. It looks as if the lower
jaw of the shark had caught
the throat almost from ear to
ear, while the upper jaw had
closed on the boy’s head.

“Gouger” : lie was the most playful cuss
of an alligator of my acquaintance. Often when the
Bloomfield flooded and washed down huge logs, he’d
swim from the bank with incredible speed, catch ’a log
in mid-stream, roll it over and over as a cat does a
mouse, then, with a playful swish of his tail, swim for
the shore. One morning he was leisurely walking among
the mangroves on the opposite bank, so we arranged
a pleasant surprise for him. My mate hid himself
while I walked aimlessly about the water’s edge.
He saw me and stopped, while I stood still with the
water at my feet. Stealthily he turned his huge 12ft.
length, took my bearings carefully, and without a
sound, came back on his tracks. He entered the
water opposite me without making a ripple. I
guessed it was about time to be going, and scrambled
up the bank to where my mate had the rifles. Half
a minute after, and the tip of a dirty brown snout
was out of the water not 4ft. from where I had been
standing. The rest of the body was under water, and
motionless, his nibs evidently wondering where the
blazes the banquet had got to. He found out.
“Crack 1” A great semicircle of surprised 'gator
jumped clear 3ft. into the air. A mighty splash,
a swish of the powerful tail, a few waves, and the
’gator had retired. We could see big bubbles rising
from the water, as they always do when a ’gator is
hit hard. But all the same, we’ve seen the cow
since. He takes his siesta on a big flat rock that
commands the approach of anyone sneaking to within
rifle range. A crow is an easy thing to shoot com-
pared with a gator whose faith in human nature
has been misplaced.
WRITING TO MEASURE.
(Scene: Editorial apartment of the DOUBIEBUCK
BANNER.)

“Hee Ivee”: I dunno ; but it seems to me
since I have been reading the reiterated remarks by
The Bulletin contributors that Australians can’t
ride, that I must have been drunk or ratty all these
years. I was under the impression they could ride
like blazes. The ordinary horse on the Kimberley
stations I have been on is run in as required, and
in many cases has only seen a white man once before
in his natural; that was the day he was branded.
The method of breaking is to get on and hang on,
and the station-hand regards the subsequent convul-
sions as commonplace. Horsemanship of this order
does not apply to the Australian of the cities, but
one doesn’t look for such a thing in a town. Still.
I would stake my accumulated debts that I could
produce 500 men in the small city of Perth who
would hold their own—and a bit more —with a like
number from any of the cities of Fogland.

THE GREATEST LAWSUIT ON EARTH.
It is alleged by the Tory press that the election pamphlet issued by the Australian Ministry should have carried the writer’s signature, and that, because
of the avsence thereof, the Federal Treasury is liable for a fine of about £30,000 (there being nothing mean about the artist he has made the cheque for
£500,000,000), payable to itself. As the Government has both to pay and receive the fine, Attorney-General Hughes appears on both sides.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
“Rangan’s” farewell ode :
Gone over, Gustav de Laval, a Swede, whose name
any cocky will find on his cream separator if he
looks at it in the daylight. Writer remembers how,
while turning the mach ne, 15 hours a week, before
breakfast, with his belt in the last hole, and drops of
sweat as big as a shilling (some as big as Is. 2d)
rolling on the floor, he spelled the name frontwards,
backwards, and sideways, and wondered whether De
Lav. ever tried the job of working his devil on an
empty stomach. Like many others whose brain has
gone to universal profit, this inventor received a poor
education. He b'gan life as a clerk. But his scien-
tific tendencies soon drew him out of that. He
experimented in chemistry and physics, dabbled in
steam, and finally entered the Kloster ironworks,
from which place he gave many lesser inventions
to the world. Accidentally and incidentally he
invented the De Laval steam turbine. It was not
till the ’7o’s that he got on to the separator idea.
Though ridiculed, he borrowed money, ran his idea
through to success, and so revolutionised the great
industry of Cow Fat.
THE FACES AT THE WINDOW
OF THE SEA.
Chinese coolie labor is being imported into the
plantations of Samoa by the German Government.
The island of Hawaii contains over 61,000 Japanese,
mostly ex-soldiers while Fiji has a far greater num-
ber of Indian coolies. Even in New Caledonia there
are thousands of Japanese ex-soldiers.

[FRED BROW, KOGARAH, N.S.W.]
Died at St. Kilda (Vic.), J. G. Coles,
director and promoter of the Lyric picture
hails at 1 itzroy, Brunswick and Prahran.
Coles, who had been a sick man for some
time past, was formerly a grazier, and the
? 011 ) that he thus garnered was employed in
lavishing pictorial instruction on the “work-
ing classes.’’ His P’ran show was the hrst of
its kind in Melbourne suburbs. Unfortu-
nately the stack of money he won from the
enterprise couldn’t buy him a clean bill of
health, and he passed at the unripe age of
46.
“Vic.”: The Thomas Mitchell Memorial,
on Mount Aripiles, was dedicated on April
30. There is no cairn built; indeed, that
would be superfluous. Detached from the
mountain, itself a huge cairn, there is one
lofty, outstanding peak, never climbed, shoot-
ing up nearly 800 ft., red, green and grey with
lichens. A copper tablet, inscribed to Mit-
chell, is fixed to this. Deflecting from the
Murray, he saw from Mount Hope a mag-
nificent prospect south and east, and the
name of Australia Felix sprang from his
imagination. The names remain which he
gave to streams, ranges and landmarks.
Yet Queensland’s Mitchell River is the only
notable geographical feature with his name.
It is said that the Natimuk school-children
began the move for the Aripiles memorial.
Mitchell give the name after the Aripiles
Mountains in Spain, at Salamanca, where
he fought under Wellington against Mar-
mont. He climbed the Victorian Mount
Aripiles on July 23, 1836. Horsham is near
by. It sneered at the Natimuk idea at first,
but ultimately went in with enthusiasm.
Three thousand people gathered for the cere-
mony ; the school-children came in five four-
horse waggons. For the edifying of this con-
gregation, Walker, head teacher of the Nati-
muk school, coupled Flinders and Mitchell
happily in a Tennyson quote : “Mighty sailor,
this is he, was great by land as thou by sea.”
THE WHOLE STOP YIN A NUTSHELL.
It is the duty of a Ministry to declare its policy,
not of an Opposition. The primary duty of an
Opposition is to examine a Government’s policy,
and, where that policy is, in their opinion, unwise "to
criticise it vigorously. —Sydney Telegraph.
