Bill Dyson wanted to insure his house at
Chelsea (London) a short time back, but
when it was known that he had caricatured
the suffragettes the insurance co. promptly
jumped up the premium 100 per cent.
According to London Daily Herald, the
recent strike of doughies in the Dense Fog
brought to light the fact that the King’s
baker was an anarchist. The specialist en-
gaged fabricating loaves of
peculiar twists and excep-
tional merit for the royal
table proved to be the usual
foreigner, and a strong be-
liever in the policy of elim-
inating kings and knocking
out law. Fortunately he was
a passive anarchist, who
only blew up in a verbal
sense, otherwise the tempta-
tion to introduce an ingeni-
ous little bomb into the
royal loaf might have been too strong for
him.
From “Rosna” in London :
The High Panjandrums of these isles who won’t
permit the frilly sex to enter the legal profession, are
down on their hands and knees before a mere woman,
who has shown them what the sex can do when it
puts its mind and its tongue to it. She is Miss
Lind-af-Hageby, an Anglicised Swede, hon. sec. of
the Anti-Vivisection Society and editor of its re-
views, and she is conducting her own case in a libel
action against the Pall Mall Gazette. Her open-
ing speech occupied nine and a half hours, and the
Judge and his minions, and just about the entire
British press has been assuring her that it was a
brilliant, concise, lucid and entirely professional ad-
dress, and one that any he-barrister might be proud
of. The lady, who appeared in court in a long, red
Portia-y coat and a little, close-fitting hat, later
spent four hours under cross-examination, with
equally good results. The same day Mrs. Pankhurst
was also conducting her own case on a charge of con-
spiring to outrage, and the contrast was rather un-
fortunate for the Lady of the Hammer and the Bomb.
Mrs. Pankhurst is what male novelists call the eter-
nal feminine. She is a frail, rather pathetic-looking
dame, who always dresses in the height of fashion
and stands in the dock with vice-regal weariness,
clasping a bouquet. She can also talk for days
on end, but she hasn’t the legal mind of daughter
Christabel, and dispenses irrelevant home-truths
chaotically. That curious beastie. the British anti-
suffragist, says that if women had votes Mrs. Pank-
hurst would want to enter Parliament. Well?
Among maritime men there is general sur-
prise over a notification that Commander
Brewis will not be the Commonwealth’s
Chief Lighthouse Inspector after all. The
salary is f 800; Brewis wanted more, and
the Government couldn’t see its way to pay
it. So Brewis took the back track and
withdrew. He was safe to get the job if
he wanted it; wherefore, although Tudor
formally invited applications, few took the
trouble to apply. Consequently. Tudor goes
through the whole business again.
ANOTHER STRANDED CIRCUS
TROUPE.
John James Matters, who died lately in
Sydney, aged 75, came to Australia in the
early ’6o’s under engagement to Pete and
Betts and Co. to supervise the building of
Queensland’s first railway-—or one of its
first. Later, he was boss of the Ramornie
Meat Works, gold-miner at Solferino, and
builder at Armidale (N.S.W.). In 1882 he
became an inspector in the N.S.W. Railway
Construction Department, and laid tram-
ways all over the place, from Broken Hill
to Rushcutter’s Bay. When he wasn’t at
work he toiled for Australia’s defence in
various volunteer regiments, and dropped
out with warrant-officer’s rank no longer
ago than 1910.
There is one good citizen of Melbourne
who objects, on personal grounds, to the
signing of daily newspaper articles. The
popular William P. Hurst, of the firm of
Robert Hurst and Co., is that man. The
Hursts run a boot factory as well as a num-
ber of shops. Every day the iniquities of
the Labor party and the deplorable slavish-
ness of union factory workers is denounced
in the Akgus over the name of William
Peter Hurst. The W.P.11. of Propputy’s
pet journal lashes Labor with knotted head-
lines, such as “Mob Rule,” “The Despotism
of Unionism,” and so forth ; consequently
W.P.H., of the factory, begins to find that
his character is under suspicion. His second
name is Patrick, not Peter, but seeing that
he uses only the initial in his autograph it
is embarrassing for him when a fellow tra-
veller in a tram-car or railway carriage
shakes an Argtjs at him and says: “Peter,
I never would have thought it of you !”
“Mr. Ocky”: A last word re Poon
Gooey, over whom this country’s leg was so
disgracefully pulled by the meddling Holy
Joes and piebald Australians. I notice that
the Sydney pressmen learnt from him that
he had attended the Methodist Church in
Geelong. As one who knew Gooey, let me
say that the soulful stories about his
“Christianity” were piffle. The Superin-
tendent Minister of the Methody Church in
the Geelong district says: “The gentleman
(Gooey) had no connection whatever with
the Methodist Church in Geelong. . . I
do not know. what Church he belonged to,
if any.” Neither do Geelong people gener-
ally. For the rest, Poon Gooey has been
represented as a very successful business
man, who should not be compelled to give
up his large interests, though no one was
asking Poon himself to leave Australia. As
a matter of fact, Poon had the sort of busi-
ness that might be expected of any China-
man—just a medley of fruit, vegetables,
empty cases, sacks, and so on, dumped on
the bare floor of Geelong’s shabby market-
place.

The will of the late Mrs. “Jim” Toohev
has been proved at £21,439. Mrs. Toohev
was a daughter of Mr. Ferris, of the old
Royal Oak, at Parramatta. She split ud
her estate amongst her relatives and her ser-
vants, and did well with her little lot The
family, of course, still holds a big handful of
the Standard Brewery stock. The two
Toohey families turned the concern over to
a public company 12 years ago, and when
things didn’t turn out as well as had been
expected they voluntarily handed bark
50,000 of their £1 shares and had them can-
celled—an action that will always stand to
the credit of the late Mrs. Toohey and the
Toohey executors.
NEWLY-ELECTED SPEAKER EVANS ,
OF TASMANIAN ASSEMBLY.
“Wealsman” : This yarn ought to be true.
A Wowserish friend of the late Jonathan
Best, M.H.A. (Tas.), met Norman Cameron
at or after Best’s funeral, and said: “Well,
Mr. Cameron, your old opponent has gone
and left you a clear field in Wilmot at last.
It’s strange that the Lord should take an in-
offensive man like him and leave you free to
rampage up and down the earth.” “Ah!
replied Cameron, “probably God knows
Best.”
Jonathan Best, of Deloraine (Tas.),
crossed the Jordan last week into the
tattered shadows of the Great Dunno.
His years were 73. Jonathan was an
agreeable old farmer, was- a politician off
and on for 19 years, and during a long and
plodding career on the Deloraine Road
Trust, he helped to keep the local roads in a
state of repair that would madden a Sydney-
ite with envy. Writer has fled over these
roads in various vehicles, and he tesbnes
that Jonathan Best, though neither brilliant
nor eloquent in politics, didn’t live in vain.
When a wet night is coming on, and there
are still 20 miles to do, and the petrol sup-
ply is running low, and the sparking plug is
working none too well —at such a time a
good road bangs all the orations of Demos-
thenes.
The just-dead Mrs. D’Oylev Carte, widow
of the Savoy Theatre owner who produced
Gilbert and Sullivan’s operas in London,
had two brothers in Adelaide, one an arcin-
tect and the other a journalist who also did
a bit of farming. J. M. Black, the journal-
ist brother, has the reputation of being api
to take a shorthand report of a speaker wit
equal facility in English, French or ye '
man, and he has dabbled in several other ia
guages. He has reported Parliament
S.A. so often that his pencil knows J
what each politician intends to say direct,
he rises to speak. Black has scien i;
tastes, and has written a book on Austral
wild flowers; he is a leading light am
the field naturalists, and knows a heap a
indigenous orchids and such abstruse *>
tables. He has retired from strenuous w
but his skill as a note-taker remains.
THE PARTING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hop Poon Gooey not unnaturally resents the harsh-
ness of the law that compels his wife, and indirectly
himself, to leave Australia. “It is finished; I go,”
he told a reporter. Having left no stone unturned
to secure a relaxation of the cast-iron regulations
that govern such matters, he accepted the inevitable.
“ If I stay always in China, I would not go
back to Confucianism,” said Mr. Poon Gooey. “I
will meet Christians in Canton, and as we belong
to the Church of Christ, and have no denomination,
we can go to the church that is nearest. In Geelong
we used to go to the Methodist church.”
Both the children were born in Geelong, the elder
being 22 months and the younger 5 months old.
“This one was christened Queenie; that one Lena,”
said the father. — Mournful Slabs from interview in
Sydney Moaning Herald.
Weep for the Poon ! Mourn for the Gooey !
A hard-hearted Government compels to hump his
bluey.
Everything is finished. Turned are all the stones.
Hop Poon Gooey’s got to go— (Loud groans).
Hard is his lot—dreary is the parting—
Woe to the tyranny that brought about his starting!
Heaven help the Government! How the Herald
scowls—•
Hop Poon Gooey’s left Geelong— (Dry howls).
Twain are the kids. One is rather teeny.
Lena is its Clistian name—the other’s name is
Queenie;
Gloomy are their faces—neither of them speaks—
Hop Poon Gooey’s babes are off— (Fierce shrieks).
Here is the Missus—sorry to have known us!
Ready, ay! and able, too, to earn the Baby Bonus !
How the D.T. blushes while her tale it tells—
Missus Gooey’s had her last one here— (Strong
yells).
Ah, what a scene! Oh, what a picture!
Worthy of (and sure to get) the Clistian’s scathing
stricture!
“ Yellow’s not so very dark—mixed with White, it
pales—
Baptised Christians—Methodists—like us”— (Sad
wails).
Shame on Australia! Hark, the Herald’s weeping!
Ere surrendering the Hops to kindly China’s keeping;
Tender-hearted grandma! Full of “buts” and
“ ifs ” —
Goon Pooey—Going Pooey— Gone!—(Hard sniffs).

The capital, to begin with in 1872, was £6250. It
is now £90,000 ; and most, if not all, the extra
£83,750 has come out of profits, no less than
£65,000 of it in the past 13 years. There are few
more profitable things than a well-managed fire-
insurance office when once it has got through its
teething. The trouble with a lot of the infants is
that they haven’t the strength to pull through that
stage, whereupon some kind lady in the same line of
business takes them over and eats them. The Aus-
tralian Mutual got in early, and made a name for
itself in Sydney when competing Australian offices
were few and not very progressive. In a manner this
wasn’t a very progressive office either. But it had a
policy, and stuck to it —it knew that it took a
whole lot of premiums to pay a £lOOO claim, and it
laid itself out to get the business that looked safest.
That, of course, meant slow progress, so far as aggre-
gate figures were concerned ; but it meant unusually
good profits compared with the co.’s very moderate
capital. Of late, recognising perhaps that its re-
sources are now bigger, it has moved more briskly,
ar.d has even launched out into branches in Mel-
bourne and Brisbane, bes-des jumping into the acci-
dsnt and employers’ liability business. The result is
rfiown in pretty large changes for the past year:—

Both loss and expense rates are higher; but, after
all, a co. that shows a loss rate of 40 p.c. on a pre-
mium income of £lOO,OOO has £60,000 left for ex-
penses and profits, whereas if it selected and took
only £50,000 of the risks, and thereby got its losses
down to 25 p.e., it would have only £37,500 avail-
able for expenses and profits. The expense rate for
the past year is probably abnormal: a co. can’t
establish itself in other States without laying out a
bit and getting very little back for a while. As for
policy-holders, the position of the eo. needn’t give
them a moment’s unrest. It is shown above that the
premiums were £53,260 for the year. Capital and
reserves are nearly 4} times more than that, whereas
in the average British office the co.’s funds are equal
to only about If times the annual premiums. From
this important point of view the position of the co.
is extraordinarily strong. Certainly nothing much
can be said about the securities into which this
money has been put, because the co. merely says that
of its investments £164,627 is in city freehold pro-
perty and mortgages and £69,445 is on deposit;
but, so far as one can learn, the first cons deration
with regard to the co.’s investments is safety. The
interest earnings last year were £10,252, or at the
rate of £4 7s. 6d., which is just about the return
one would expect if the investments are what they
are supposed to be. The fixed div. is at the rate of
8 p.c. ; after that the co allows its policy-holders
the usual rebate of 20 p.e., and divides an equal
amount among its shareholders.

The Westralian thirst is still vigorous, and the past
half-year’s profit is the largest ever shown. The rate
of div. on the ordinaries certainly isn’t what it used
to be, but this is explained by the fact that 18
months ago the shares were doubled out of reserves.
The business has been an exceedingly profitable one;
notwithstanding the big dividends paid, the profits
left in the concern amount to approximately £2
10s. for every £1 of capital put up by shareholders.
And vet there was a time, shortly after the Mel-
bourne boom broke, when a lot of the shares were
forfeited for non-payment of calls. The assets now
amount to £513,098, though‘on the other side there
are £53,287 of liabilities, mostly mortgages on the
hotel properties into which the co. has put most of
its savings. Ten years ago these properties stood at
£107,165 (cost price) ; now they have been in-
creased to £231,562. Rents brought in £9065 in
the past six months. This hotel asset is not being
written down. A few .years ago a deprec’ation fund
was started, but it was presently transferred to re-
serves on the ground that there had been a vast ap-
preciation in the value of the properties, as a whole.
All the same, a depreciation or replacement fund is
a handy thing to have on the premises. Pub build-
ings won’t last for ever, and the bricklayer will want
his wages even if the land he is working on is worth
10 times as much as the owner paid for it. Other
assets of the co. include freehold brewery premises
(also at cost, amt alleged to have iaigelv appreciated),
£84,475j plant. £71,574 t:t actually cost
£139.235) ; book debts. £40,142 : and mortgages
and other loans, £45.576. The amount written off
plant is not included in the profit shown in the
table. Altogether the co. has been a sweet proposi-
tion, and looks just as sweet as ever. A had slump
in hotel properties would no doubt hit it hard; hut,
though nobody can tell what Cold Tea may do in
time, at present there is no apparent reason for this
co. to feel nervous.

This was the gaudiest of the three big Yankee offices
which asked Australia to help them get big figures.
They were b g-figure mad ; the cost of the business
was quite a minor consideration. Certainly, there was
some method in their madness; for the bigger the
funds the bigger the pull the bosses of the concerns
had in America’s “high-finance” circles. That pull
expla ns why not so very long ago Pierpont Morgan
paid some millions of dollars for a controlling share
interest in the Equitable, though the entire share
capital is only about 100,000 dollars ( £20,550) and
the dividend is limited to 7 p.c. But the Legislature
has put a stop to the big-figure madness by limiting
expenses and the volume of new business ; hence, new
business-getting by any foreign office being costly in
Australia, the co. withdrew its canvass, and David
Fell and Co. were appointed attorneys to accept pre-
miums and pay claims.
Under American law there must be a valuation of
assets every year, and the shrinkage or expansion of
values must be shown in the Revenue Account. This
explains the erratic conduct of the Income column
in the table on top. A better view can be got from
the following:—

This shows that the people have got well over the
scare that followed the clean-up previously men-
t'oned, and there is a healthy upward movement
again. Unquestionably policyholders are all the bet-
ter off for the stopper which the Legislature put on
the wild rush for business. The expenses, though
still large by comparison with the best Australian
results, are now down to £l3 17s. p.c. of the total
income—they used to be well over £2O. The reduc-
tion means better bonuses. As for security, there
was never any reason to doubt it. The American
offices publish detailed l'sts of their assets, and the
fact that their business is growing is proof of a sort
that those assets are regarded as satisfactory in the
place where their value is best known.

It isn’t a big business (the estates in hand are
valued at only £1,756,306) ; but as the paid capital
is only £lO,OOO, and as all of it is out earning good
money, apart from the business, the co. manages to
pay a very solid div'dend. That is the advantage of
a concern of this sort; all the capital that it is
necessary to sink in unproductive assets is the trifle
it costs to buy a brass plate, an office mat and a
few sticks of furniture. In this case practically all
the capital and reserves is represented by “mortgages
and debentures,” wh ch bring in more than enough
to pay a 10 p.c. div. on the capital. As for the
risks, they fall under two heads: (1) Those arising
from faulty investment of the co.’s own funds, though
if the co. doesn t pass all but extremely gilt-edged
securities, the Board is a much bigger ass than it
looks on paper; (2) those arising from the mis-
reading of instructions in wills, etc., and with expert
management there isn’t much in that. Besides the
paid capital and the reserve's, beneficiaries are pro-
tected by £40,000 of uncalled capital; and that’s
only one of the advantages of turning to a profes-
sional trustee instead of depending on the usual
“friend of the family” who doesn’t guarantee any-
thing—riot even that he will be alive when he’s
wanted.

Profits have shrunk, and two things are mainly re-
sponsible—higher costs (the co. paid one-third more
per bale for cartage, for instance), and the drought
which cut into N. S. Wales flocks in the early part
of last year, and thus reduced the clip. For the
year to the end of June, 1910, the co. handled 72.52 G
bales; in the next 12 months, 66,464 bales. For
the past year the total was only 44,234 bales. It
seems very doubtful whether drought has been wholly
responsible for this serious shrinkage; it looks as
though the co-operative idea hasn't as firm a grip on
the wool-grower as it ought to have. In that respect
the man who grows cows and cream can give him
points. The co.’s figures, of course, are not colossal;
but they arc sound. Liabilities are comparatively
small ( £33,002), and they are more than balanced
by sundry debtors, £4o,4o4—presumably advances
against consignments. The capital and the bulk of
the reserves are in the wool and grain stores at Pyr-
mont, £70,779. The co. isn’t in a bed of violets by
any nmans; it is up against keen and not over-friendlv
competition. But the very fact that it gets the
cold eye and the hard shove ought to satisfy the
grower that there is a lot to be said in its favor.
When the people who collect the commission say
that one of their number is a jolly good fellow
every way, the man who pays the commission can
take it for "ranted that the jolly good fellow is no
particular friend of his.

The Country Producers Selling Co.,
Ltd. (Sydney), a more or less co-operative concern,
dealing in farm and station—-chiefly station—pro-
duce :—-
Bonus to
March, Loss. Dividend. Consignors. Deficit.
1911 .. £960 £660
Profit. Reserves.
K'l2 ~ 3:90 5 p.c. = £984 £869 1077
1913 .. 6975 7} „ „ 1731 2872 3449
Besides handling wool (its consignments in the past
year reached a highly respectable 33,508 bales), this
co. operates at Homebush, where it sold 340,201
sheep and 20,867 cattle. In this respect it differs
from the other co-operative wool-selling concern in
Sydney. The size of the business shows that the in-
fant has some enthusiasts godfathers, for this is
practically only the second year of the little one’s
life-"—in the actual first year it did no more than a
trifle of live-stock business. As it goes along it will
certainly need more canital. Its assets, including
freehold and plant (£38,949), amount to £53,371,
and as its paid capital is only £23.078, it has had
to borrow £21,000 on mortgage. Later on it will
be expected to make more extensive advances against
consignments, some of which will he hung up in the
stores waiting for a better market; and at present
it can only do that business by going to its own
uncle for the money. However, matters seem to be
in good, safe hands ; and if the infant goes on as it
has started, it promises to be presently a very lusty
youth indeed.

The net profit is shown “after providing for depre-
ciation, etc.” How much is provided for deprecia-
tion and how much for “etc.” this paper doesn’t
know. And not knowing that, it doesn’t .know
much about this co. A dissection of the published
accounts doesn’t throw much light on the darkness:
Mar., ’O5. Sept., ’ll. Mar., ’l2. Mar., ’l3.
Liabilities £1,165 £19,057 £21.785 £5,506
Ship property 40,393 84,708 75,376 70,375
Liquid assets 45.863 6,684 18,923 10,531
Other assets 2,667 5,522 5,788 5,815
In the last 18 months the book value of ship property
has shrunk from £84,708 to £70,375, and that
looks like severe writng down; but, then, it may
be nothing of the sort, because, though one or two
vessels have been added to the fleet, one or two
others have been lost. On the published accounts
one would say that the co. is paying too much in
dividends, and isn’t making a proper lav-by ; on the
other hand David Fell is the chairman of directors,
and David Fell is a cautious accountant who
wouldn’t be at all likely to go on recommending
dividends that he didn’t believe were fully earned
and could be safely paid. As for the future, things
ought to look up. The South Coast is far less pro-
gressive than it ought to be, considering its natural
advantages; but it is waking up—to some extent
because this once dreadfully slumberous co. has put
a little more ginger into its management of late
years.
Australasian Wireless Co., Ltd. (Syd-
ney).—This co. was formed to buy the Australasian
rights of the Telefunken Co. The capital consisted
of 50,000 ordinary and 15,000 6 p.c. pref. shares of
£1 each. Patent rights and goodwill represent
£43,406 of this amount. The Telefunken Co. ac-
cepted payment as to £6500 in debentures, which
are still outstanding. The first year’s operations
showed a loss of £ 2860, due mainly to litigation
and consequent delays; in the subsequent eight
months to November 30, 1912, for which accounts
have only just been issued, a further loss of £937
is disclosed. Litigation continued during this
period ; but the differences over patents with the
Marconi people, who were also operating in Aus-
tralia, have been settled by an amalgamation. A
new co. is to be formed in which the Australasian
Wireless will have 70.000 £1 shares—4s,ooo it will
get for its goodwill, 20.000 (approximate! v) for
other assets in excess of JjabiUties, and 5000 will
have to be paid for in cash.'. The new co. is to take
over all the shipping and other business of both cos.,
and starts with a revenue said by the directors to
be about £lO,OOO a year. What proportion of the
profits belong;, to the shareholders in the old co. is
not. however, apparent, as the total capitalisation
of the new cov is not disclosed in this co.’s report.
The Federal Pastoral Shearing Co.—
This was formed in Sydney something over a year
ago th buy the business and assets of the Federal
Sheep Shearing Co. The nominal capital is £50.000
in 100.000 shares of 10s. each ; but only 31,312 have

co. to pay for goodwill ( £9500) and other trifles.

wet weather. Unfortunately, the directors don’t seem

disastrous dampness is liable to repeat itself. Apart
from goodwill the co. has assets alleged to be worth
£19.369, and it owes no less than £13,812; so the

The last half-year has been abnormal. Twelve ,h!!
after the term started the disastrous fire broke
in the North Lyell, the source of the go's It
pyritous (fluxing) supplies; and no ore ha Fi
been broken there. The flooding of the mine 9
take long ; but it has taken seven months to get the
water out, clean up the workings, re-timber where
necessary, and prepare for attacking the mine afresh
Ihe outlay directly chargeable to the fire has
thusfar £21,283, which amount has been shown f
the table as written off. The circumstances being
unusual it seemed better to do it that way than i n g
eiude the amount with ordinary working exnens«
and exhibit a consequent loss. The indirect has’
of course, been much greater than the direct loss’
In order to hold the staff together the furnaces were
as far as possible kept running, but not being able
to draw on the rich North Lyell pyritous ore, the no
was driven back on to low-grade Tharsis material'
The half-year’s figures are therefore nothing to to
by. Normal conditions, or thereabouts, ought to
be restored by the end of next month.
Still it hasn’t been all bad luck. At the end of
the previous half-year there was 996 tons of cotmer
on hand and taken into account at £6O per ton
This copper sold at an average of £Bl 7s 39
that £21,000 was added to the past half-year’s

has been mopped up in the struggle to keep the
other works going during the six months.
Future profits depend mainly on the price of
copper; and the copper market is an erratic beast.
In the past 20 years it has been as low as £4O
is. 6d. for a twelve-months’ average, and as high as
£B7 Bs. 6d. In the past three years the variations
have been as much as £35, and the market doesn't
seem very robust at present. As for ore supply, there
is no need to worrv about that just vet. It 'isn’t as

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, halt-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Ado!
Blei chert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution ot
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempnere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

Best Yorkshire Farnley Iron is preferred by engi-
neers, where toughness, great strength and close
grain are needed. Whatever the specification, Iron
of the Farnley type does not vary, but is simply the

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. 5d.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow i6in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [2

Messrs. Mephan Ferguson Proprietary, Limited, of
Footscray, Victoria, have secured the contract for
the supply of steel pipes required in connection with
the duplication of the Bombay Water Supply. The
contract amounts to over £226,000, and the pipes
to be used are of the locking bar type, of which Mr.
Mephan Ferguson is the inventor and patentee.
Engineers will be interested to note that this Aus-
tralian invention has been adopted in preference to
other classes of piping, after critical and searching
enquiries as to the merits of the various types of
water pipes made in the world. It is worthy of
note that all machinery required to carry out this
work has been designed and manufactured at the
firm’s Footscray Engineering Works, and will be
shipped to Bombay, where the pipes will be manu-
factured.*
Messrs. Babcock k Wilcox, Ltd., have received
the order for the supply of all the steam generating
plant and accessories for the electrification of the
Victorian Government Railways, The acceptance of
this firm’s tender was strongly recommended by Mr.
Merz, the English expert. The contract amounts to
£258,000.*

down was so much the policy of the management that
just over five years ago the East Greta stood in the
books at only £10,500 and the Stanford Merthyr at
£9OOO. Since then various items have been lumped

taste in the mouth ; and the steady growth of the coal
business in that district, as the Newcastle coastal
mines gradually peter out, adds to the value of the
important railway asset.

Though an extra i p.e. is now offered,
Maoriland Savings Bank deposits are £57,000 less
than they were at March 31, 1912. It shows that
the people are compelled to draw on their reserves,
despite Massey’s recent assertion that Maoriland was
never more prosperous. The assertion, as usual, was
made on the eve of Maoriland’s customary pilgrim-
age to Cohen. #
Reference was made here a few weeks
ago to the action of the S. Aus. Taxation Depart-
ment in claiming income tax on money received by
way of premium on a new share issue. Ihe claim
was allowed by the lower court; but in a case de-
fended bv the Bank of Adelaide it was upset on
appeal to the Full Court; and the Commissioner,
though he gave notice of appeal to the High Court,
has now abandoned the suit and refunded the bank
its £2500. S. Australia has thus come sensibly into
line with Queensland and other States.

Because, for one thing, “it is detrimental
to a co. to show on the face of the balance-sheet that
it is not able to pay dividends,” the directors of the
unhappy Emu Bay Railway Co. are asking pref.
shareholders to forego half the arrears of dividend
payable to them, and (except where the amount owing
is'less than £10) to take a 6 p.c. funded certificate
payable in 1923 or earlier at the co.’s option.
Amounts under £lO and fractions of a pound it is
proposed to pay in cash. The pref. shares are entitled
to a first 8 p.c. out of profits, half of it being cumu-
lative, and the accumulations amount to £29,rfcsz.
The scheme is good enough. For their arrears the
pref. holders get a negotiable security, and, the old
arrears being nominally wiped out, they have at
least a chance of getting a div. up to 8 p.c. forth-
with. That div. could have been paid out of last
year’s profit, which was over £lO,OOO. And, though
thev are still miles awav from a dividend, the wiping
out of about £15,000 of debt will of course put ordi-
naries a few yards nearer their goal. The Payment
of 6 p.c. on a new interest-bearing debt of ilo.uuu
will certainly wipe out some of that advantage, but
it looks as though they should still gain something.
Broken Hill Block 14, the first pup of the
B H. Prop.'s litter, was also the first to come to
grief The Broken Hill ore-body is like the legs of
an upside-down V. At the apex, near the Block 14
boundary of the B. H. Prop., the rich ore was r ght
at the surface. Unfortunately also the leg was rather
thin there, and the co. didn’t take long to get
through the payable stuff. For several years past it
has been poking around to find overlooked patches of
carbonate ore—the class that occurs above the old
water level. Last half-year working account showed
a profit of £6578, and interest and trifles brought
the total to £8719. Out of that and the money
in hand £l5OO was paid on the 10 p.c. pref. shares
and £15,000 on the whole 200,000 shares (Is. 6d.
per share). The prefs. come in twice; they get
their own exclusive 10 p.c. and then share equally
with the ordinaries. Nobody knows how much car-
bonate ore is left. There are pract callv no re-
serves • the co. just lives from hand to mouth, and
the further it goes the more expensive it is to get
the stuff. Of sulphides there are, according to the
latest estimate, 220,000 tons. In 1911 the Board
thought hard over its problem. It had a mill once,
but the works were idle for so long that they had
become useless (they never were muoh good),
and it would take all the profit the co. could make
out of its 220,000 tons to pay fot a new plant, bo
it decided to sell its ore, and made a contract with
the Junct on North. The latter co. also took its
slimes and naid for them. Unfortunately the Junc-
tion North has troubles of its own, and hasn t been
able to chew up ore very fast. The Bulletin will
not make a guess as to the cost of getting the Block
14 ore out; a good deal of it is in old stopes that
are in a bad wav. Some of it they will perhaps
never get out at all. From the two sources a few
uncertain thousands may be expected every six months
for several years to come; but nobody should trust
to that. The only sure thing is the £75,000 cash
reserves; this and a few trifles bring the surplus
of liquid assets to £81,502. #

Mount Bulga (N.S.W.), with its one
blast furnace, is embarking on a copper-smelting
campaign which may last somewhere about a
month. No attention has been paid to mine develop-
ment, and most, if not all, of the ore to be treated
has been at grass for some time. With the disposal
of this- lot the co.’s end seems near.
* * * *
The Y Water Extended, which attacked
the Graveyard Deep Lead at Emmaville (N.S.W.)
with a few hundreds of working capital, has gone the
way of many similar too-optimistic cos., and appears
to be down and out. Beyond proving that without
doubt a lead does extend under their property, which
was well enough known before, little has been done.
The water beat the pump at 130 ft., so the shaft
stopped at that. #
At Lionsville, on the Upper Clarence
goldfield (N.S.W.), some rich yields were obtained in
the early days, and recent crushings show that all the
good stone was not removed. The first workings
were very primitive, and the manner in which the
present holders are dealing with the problem is very
little better. The district is worth the attention of
people with some money to spend.
* * * *
The Democrat, Cootamundra (N.S.W.), is
getting 12dwt. gold per ton from a big open cut, and
if this represents an average for the full width of
No. 1 (4Oft. wide) and No. 2 (16ft.), the show
should pay dividends. For the fortnight 190 tons
returned 119 oz. smelted gold. As usual in the Coota-
mundra-Temora-Wyalong district, there is plenty of
mineral about, and the manager intends to save con-
centrates, and is adding a Wilfley table.
* * * *
In its past half-year Cope’s Creek
(N.S.W.) paid £2700 (Is. 6d. per share) in divs.,
though profits were only £2140. The output was 39
tons tin concentrates, so the profit averaged £54 per
ton—nothing to give three cheers about, considering
the boom price of the metal. The outlook would be
much healthier if new dredging areas were available.
Cope’s Creek, among the rest, has tried its best to
acquire new ground, but it is difficult to get, and
what is obtainable is poor. #
Divs. amounting to £142,986 were paid
by Westralian gold-mining cos. in April, making a
total of £305,679 since Jan. 1, and £23,198,194
since the industry started. Of this sum Great
Boulder is credited with £4,022,423, Golden Horse-
shoe £3,075,000, Ivanhoe £3,063,750, Orova-Brown
Hill £2,157,241, Great Fingall £1,734,375, Lake
View £1,493,000, Perseverance £1,426,130, Kal-
gurli £1,252,500, and Sons of Gwalia £916,000. To
date no less than £25,500.000 has been dug out of
the narrow strip worked by the Great
Boulder, Horseshoe and Ivanhoe, and the value of ore
still in sight runs* into millions. Boulder’s days,
however, are apparently numbered.
* * * *
Kalgoorlie managers are greatly annoyed
at the administration of the Act which prohibits the
employment of the unintelligible. foreigner under-
ground. The principal complaint is that foreigners,
mostly Dagoes, who pass one inspector are turned
down when another inspector comes along, and prose-
cutions follow. The issue of certificates after exam-
ination is accordingly suggested. Unless something
is done in this direction the managers threaten to
get rid of every alien employed on the mines; but
as that would knock some of the outputs end-ways,
it may be regarded for the present as merely hot
air. „ *
Last year Boulder Perseverance (W.A.)
treated 234,636 tons for £270,434. The net pro-
fit, after writing off cost of development and depre-
ciation, amounted to £25,500, which is £23,000
short of the 1911 figures. Costs are a shade higher
at 18s. 3d. The co. returns to the dividend list after
an absence of three years with a distribution of
£34 986 (6d. per share) ; but as the balance-sheet
only discloses a cash surplus of £23,500, or £11,500
less than required to meet this extravagance, it looks
as if there is a tendency on the part of those in
control to make a splash. Underground work has
been chiefly confined to the development of two new
ore bodies and a general prospecting of the old work-
ings. The latter operation, as already stated, has led
to a number of payable discoveries. One of the new
ore bodies has been proved continuous from the
200 ft. to the 1100 ft. level, exposing an estimated
reserve of 52,300 tons worth 5.27dwt , while another
is said to have so far added 26,000 tons of 6.Bdwt.
ore to the reserves exposed between the 200 ft. and
500 ft levels. Compared with 1911, however, the
total ore in sight—49B,ooo tons, worth 245. 9d
shows a decrease of 16,400 tons in quantity and 6d.
in value. The “probable” reserves are estimated at
857,000 tons, or 49,000 tons more than for the pre-
vious vear, but the average value (235.) is Bd. less.
And with these low averages to work on, a drop of
6d or Bd. is serious. As for the lodes which have
hitherto been the backbone of the property, the sad
news is gently broken to shareholders that all efforts
to find payable values in depth have failed.

The position is that the ’Shoe is far in debt and

* * * *
Queensland’s score for minerals other than
gold was last year 28 p.c. better than the previous
best. But gold is on the down grade. The best year
was 1900, and 1903 was nearly as good. Since then
the output has dropped from 668,5460 z. to 347,9460 z.
last year, when it reached the lowest for a quarter of
a century. And this year is shaping no better. The
output for the four months is only 88,1530 z., as
against 109,7890 z. in the corresponding third of
1912.
* * *
“Gouger”: Helped by the rainy season,
the Annan River (N.Q.) has had three nozzles going.
Results of latest runs: —
Face. Hours. Dirt sluiced. Oxide.
Daly’s . . 586 7500 cub. yd. 140 cwt.
Collingwood .. 918 9000 ~ 120 „
Glass’s . . 792 4000 „ 60 „
Costs appear to run to about per yard; so
there is good money in it at present. Three tons
from 4000yds. means 18d. per yd., taking oxide at
the very low figure of £lOO per ton. The New
Queensland Tin Sluicing Co. has taken over the Home
Rule. First return, 28cwt. for the quarter from
6000yds. ; this means, say, 7id. per yard, which
isn’t anything to jubilate over. Northey, represent-
ing southern people, is boring Lode Hill. Last two
months’ work at this claim: February, 30cwt. ;
March, 70cwt. Elliott and Skirving own the prop-
erty: the former says £5OOO for his share; Skirving
hasn’t mentioned a price. The Roaring Meg has been
looking up a bit. Other sluicers are doing from
fair to tucker; most of them, however, would like
to meet some nice person from the South with a
thousand or two in his pockets. And if the nice
person had money enough to work some of the
ground, say, in the Bloomfield scrub, on a big scale,
he might do very well indeed. Meantime one
Pocock has taken up a dredging area on the Annan,
and is alleged to have got nice persons in London
to back him to the extent of £40,000.
Kelly’s Queen Block, Charters Towers,
not having done much good with the St. George reef,
intends to ooen a drive on the St. Patrick reef,
intersected in driving the crosscut at the 657 ft.
level. Kelly’s has had a brilliant past, for since the
reef of that name was struck £195,000 has been paid
in dividends.
* * *
Blair Athol colliery, back of Rockhamp-
ton (Q.), has just finished its best half-year to date.
It sold 44.641 tons of coal, made a profit of £5774
after providing for depreciation, and paid £5OOO in
d'vs. (Is. per share). And the future looks good:
“Not only have all contracts been renewed on ex-
piry, but a greater volume of business has been
secured with smaller consumers.” The co. has an
idea that the time is ripe for it to go into the coke
business, and small narcels of coal have been sent
around to find out the right sort of ovens. There
ought to be a good market for the product among
the copper-producers of the North.
* * * *
Grcbin. the exnert who went to Papua to
bore for the British New Guinea Development Co.,
has reported that he expects to strike oil shortly.
In Grebin’s memo, to Judge Murray he said there was
a great escape of gas, and boring might have to be
abandoned while a reservoir w’as constructed. This
looks like business, as the making of an oil basin to
receive the outflow of a “gusher” is recognised as the
penultimate stage on the way to striking oil. The
born°‘ has been conducted in grey slate, impregnated
with oil, and this gives hone that the grey shales
of North Queensland and the Northern Territory,
which occur amongst the sedimentary rocks, will also
supply “rock-oil” at a depth. C. H. Locke is also
boring at Valaila for the Federal Government; he
has a modern drilling rig. built in Melbourne, and
capable of going down to 3000 ft. Geological indica-
tions being the same in the Northern Territory as m
the western division of Papua, there is much hope
for oil prospectors in the former place. Already
four Melbourne cos. have been formed to bore at the
Victoria River, and a fifth co., partly subscribed in
Svdnev, is on the stocks readv for launching. Last
vear over £1.000.000 was paid for mineral oil im-
ported into Australia, so there is a good market
waiting to receive the local product. #
Gippsland Copper and Platinum is in
trouble again over its finances, and with none of
the Mount Lvell crowd to help, only a crawl can be
done towards putting the co. into a decent position.
After its second advent the co. smelted 1291 tons,
and got matte carrying 36 tons copper, 41oz. gold.
1270 z. platinum, and 5190 z. silver, the value on
rails beins? £3775. These metals should have left
profits, but without plant to turn out high-grade
matte, the result was poor. Cadwallader ami San-
towski. who now command mining and smelting
respectively, are both confident. Their programme to
get things going involves practically the raising ot
another £5OOO, against which call-monev owing and
Government grant pronr'sed represent £2BOO. Poor
shareholders have to make up the difference, and are
promised, in return, that with plant remodeled,
profits of £4O a day can be earned. For how
long’ They mention that 12.000 tons of ore are
available. Surely there is more than 12.000 tons
in sight. If not, there isn’t enough to justify much
expenditure on plant. *
The new Gitsham gold-extraction process,
referred to last week, consists mainly in the use of
hvdrocvanic acid as a solvent. This solvent, used
up to a 0.1 p.c. strength, has a selective action tor
gold and silver only, and is said to have been suc-
cessfully applied to ores containing copper, arsenic,
bismuth and antimony—all minerals detrimental to
the ordinary cyanide process. Gitsham, the in
ventor, and T. H. Davies, the chemist—the latter a
Ballarat School of Mines graduate and a son of the
long-time manager of the Tasmanian Gold Mine—-
left Sydney last week for London via America to
present the invention to a few millionaires and
others who want to see more of it. #

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, May 19, 1913.
My Dear Arini, —
The late Rev. Father Keating, S.J., rector
of the Riverview College for boys, was noted
for bis charming manners in an only lesser
degree than for his learning. He was a
bright and vivid personality. The casual
visitor on a college sports day generally
came across Father Keating surrounded by
a group of small boys eager to introduce
their adult friends to the Chesterfieldian
chief. Cerebral trouble caused the Light to
flicker out suddenly.
The rain rained heavily on two very wet
foundation stones of the new Common-
wealth Bank the other morning. Prime
Minister Fisher, under the drip of a
streaming umbrella which shed its superflu-
ous water down his back hair, declared one
stone to be well and truly laid. Denison
Miller, as over-lord of the new structure,
seized the second gold trowel and gave stone
number two a hearty slap. The crowd on
the. pavement then squeezed the rainfall out
of its ears, so that the figures might trickle
through without being waterlogged while
the financial Miller trotted out a healthy
family of statistics.
Governor Strickland had the wet end of
the deluge for his first visit to Newcastle
last week. The snail-like ways of the War
Office are moss-grown with the legend,
“Look before you creep.” His Ex. appears
to have made a subtle reference to the fact
that the W .O. turns like an elephant and
falls asleep when it is half-way round. “That
the president of this association [the New-
castle Naval and Military] had been able
to get this recognition [a medal for J. O.
Christianson] from the War Office is a
matter for congratulation.” What will hap-
pen to Bull’s kingdom if the Enemy in
Europe chances to invade the West End out
of the season, while the W.O. authorities
are shooting grouse in Scotland, no man
knoweth.
Major-General Godley (M.L.), who is off
to the Aldershot manoeuvres via ’Frisco, is a
is a smart, soldierly-looking man. He is,
groomed to show the sunny side of 40 to
an. inquisitive world which loves to pry into
weights and ages. According to an M.L.
authority now in this township, Godley has
been one of the rare importations which
the Dominion hasn’t wanted to revoke. He
has placed a firm right-hand on the de-
faulting cadet, and means to drag Khaki
junior up to a state of efficiency in spite of
the. occasional wails of that youngster’s
timid Papa and Mamma. Papa, by the
way, is just as much in revolt in MX. as
he is occasionally in this State. Long let-
ters from “Indignant Father” float through
the M.L. press when Sonny has damped
the soles of his boots on a showery day.
Sydney’s long and rather dull list of
women’s clubs has recently had a thirteenth
club added to its social round. Founded on
the 13th of the month by 13 damsels in the
year T 3, it meets in Eirene Mort’s studio.
Whether the 13 club maidens walk under a
ladder, spill salt, cross their dinner knives,
and smash a mirror as a regular habit, my
correspondent forgets to mention.
A recent English mail brought details of
the passing of the witty, kindly Lady Doro-
thy .Nevill at the age of 87. Bronchitis
carried her off this mortal scene, much
against her will, for she loved life, and was
cheerful and full of hope to the last. A
veritable Mark Tapley, she said of her
family that there had only been one serious
Walpole, her cousin Spencer. This unusually
solid member of an erratic, light-hearted
and rather frivolous race traced his more
even mental balance to the lucky circum-
stance of his great-grandfather having
married a Dutchwoman.
Lady Dolly was so free from the snob-
bery of the Yellowplush school that she
cared nothing for who people were, but
a great deal for what they were. Her house
in Charles-street, Mayfair, was the meet-
mg-ground of all sorts and sizes of person-
ages, who might be anything so long as they
were clever and amusing. A tireless corres-
pondent, she was “Your affectionate cousin,
Dolly,’ to an interminable list of relatives.
A letter written to her kinsman, Chamber-
lain Nevill. about three weeks before her
death, contained the same sprightly com-
ments on men and affairs as characterise
those portions of her books which have not
been too much “edited” by the more ponder-
ous pen of her son Ralph. If Lord Dick’s
grandfather hadn’t thoughtlessly married
dnd left two sons, Lady Dolly’s husband,
Reginald Nevill, for many years heir-pre-
sumptive to the Abergavenny estates, would
have snatched the glittering label “Mar-
quis.”
Of Orrock, the noted English collector,
whose death of old age was cabled the other
day, Lady Dolly tells many quaint stories.
His taste for “wormy” chairs brought down
the ridicule of many of his less aesthetic
acquaintances. So long as thev were Shera-
ton and Chippendale, the enthusiastic Or-
rock cared nothing for their unhygienic legs.
You may laugh,” lie used to say, “but I
shall laugh' louder when my wormy chairs
shall be appreciated at their proper worth
The worm holes can he stopped—everyone
to his taste! Some people like high game. I
like high chairs.” Two Elizabethan chairs
which Orroek had pounced on at 30s.
apiece he soon sold for several pounds.
f 1 ° m jL/ ea V s late . r he re-purchased the best
tor ±.50 almost immediately selling it again
tor iloO. Orroek, before he began his
successful attack on the English horsehair-
chair epoch, had christened himself “The
Voice Crying in the Wilderness of British
Art.
The late Caspar Bruer Wirth. who made
a pile in U.S., has left £4.000,000 or there-
abouts, to his relatives. Philip Wirth, who
owns the circus, gets a mere £200,000 out
of this heap of jrold dust. His cousin,
another Philip Wirth, is likely to bank
about £1,000,000 as his share of this golden
blizzard. At least, that is how the tale ran
last week. But so many wondrous fortunes
that look well in the will resolve themselves,
when the property is overhauled, into a
moth-eaten tenement and * trunkful of dead
securities. A man on the staff of this paper
has half-promised to leave me 1000 Mount
Cannindah copper shares and a dead mine
(it died, he says, 30 years ago) in the centre
of Otago. But I don’t suppose I’ll be able to
buy myself an extra dress out of the pro-
ceeds.
The useful Victor Hood lias departed
hence for more than a few weeks, as at first
suggested. As Private Secretary to Gov-
ernor Strickland, he would have been
mighty helpful to a perfectly new Governor
with an otherwise raw staff. The sudden
Something that has fallen like a thousand
bricks _on the Hon. Victor’s desire to re-
main in Sydney, is a matter of surmise.
He loves Australia, and has such a firm de-
termination to dodge the English climate
that, presumably, he only wants pressing to
become a Commonwealth citizen. Mr.
Hood has seen more of this continent than
many of its native born. He had some
earlier interests in North Queensland
(1894-3900). Wandered off to South
Australia to join the staff of Gover-
nor Ee Hunte (1903-1906). Left the
chain for a trip to Colombo, and hurried
back to Victoria to shadow the steps of
Governor Talbot. Later, he tracked the in-
vited guest to the Carmichael doorstep, and
spent a brief interlude on the staff of Gov-
ernor Fuller. A motor smash terminated
his look-in on the G.-G. Denman regime.
Victor Hood is, therefore, better up in the
geogranhy of this sunlit-patch than the
absentee native-born who love to pose as
Londoners.
Miss Mabel Kingsmill, a North Sydney
girl who went off for a trip with Mr. and
Mrs. St. Vincent Welsh, was last heard of
in Jerusalem. . She is liable to be nearing
these shores, like a homing pigeon, any fine
winter morning, unless Asia clutches her
on the journey back.
Jack London, like Rider Haggard, seems
to have gone on the soil with the most ex-
pensive intentions. The Breeder and
Sportsman gives a long list of pedigree
stock which London has recently bought for
his ranch at Glen Ellen, California. The
U.S. writer has been buying up all the
American horse peeraee, or most of it, so
his pen must be shod with gold these days.
According to the gospel of the cable, an
Australian named Bartram has snatched
second prize for a milking machine. This
exhibit was on view at the English Agri-
cultural Society’s show of these labor-
savers. The weakness of the average milk-
ing machine, as one sees it on N.S.W. dairy
farms, is the mildly hostile attitude of the
Australian cow. She yields to the auto-
matic pressure of machinery up to a cer-
tain point. Then she maintains a sullen
front to the Enemy which is removed to
make way for the human stripper. Not
always, but very often, he is a small boy
with such appallingly dirty hands that a
washing machine is urgently required to
add to the uses of the machine milker. The
inventor who can place on the market a
Dairy Devil that will seize the boy, wash
his hands, and bump him down on the stool
before he has time to resist, will certainly
lessen the infantile death-rate in our cities.
Beatrice Grimshaw, who has anchored
her graphic pen in Papua for some years
was mentioned in the Van Waerwyck’s Syd-
ney passenger list.
From the same rubber-shod island comes
Lieut.-Governor Murray and his private
sec., H. L. Murray.
For once, the usually inscrutable work-
ings of Providence which seems to sit up
all night devising sudden death for the most
useful persons in the communitv, have been
conducted on business lines. According to
the Sydney Telegraph, Mayor Bonner, of
Manly, and Providence are firm allies. Mr
Bonner wants to spend £7OOO of municipal'
cash on new dressing-sheds. In the morn-
ing, tenders for the removal of the old
shanties on Ocean Beach were called for in
the Government Gazette. Providence
presumably reads this dull document. In
the evening it sent along a million horse-
power storm, which removed everything re-
quired, except a few slabs of timber that
were stronger than the storm.
The Royal Alexandra Hospital for Sick
Ohildren didn t have its linen spree as ex-
pected. The weather promised faithfully to
do its wettest to spoil an outdoor fete, so
Lily Bray ton and the tea cakes were put
off till Wednesday in this week. The notion
of getting three years’ supply of sheets,
blankets, etc., in one fell swoop is obviously
sound. Secretary Clarence Moss pointed
proudly to a mountain of towels, a pyramid
of blankets, and a snowy bank of sheets the
other afternoon. Three years’ donations
for the youngsters filled a fair-sized room
and nearly climbed out of a fanlight,.
Clara Butt and her elegant Ivennerley
Rumford left this town in the middle of the
late Flood. That was the night when Noah
drove the express, with Christopher Colum-
bus as stoker. The Junoesque Clara, as
placid and smooth of skin as she was five
years ago and is likely to be 10 years hence
(nothing but an earthquake would dis-
arrange that equable temper), owns to dis-
covering little that is very startling in the
way of. new music. She is a warm and
comforting personality on a wet night. Five
minutes before the Melbourne express
screamed its way out of Sydney two femi-
nine adorers rubbed themselves against the
vast Butt, and purred over her furs like
damp kittens drying themselves beside the
generous warmth of an amiable lady bull-
dog. The Voice, one imagines from the per-
fect physique of the owner, is undamaged
by five years’ wear and tear.
H. N. Southwell, more or less better after
his recent illness, was standing on a little
island of spare flooring on a wet afternoon
last week. In front of him raged a meeting
for the Tom Moore concert, which occurs
on May 28. A serious small boy was pour-
ing forth a^. sweet treble voice over the top
notes of “Killarney,” while detached groups
of casuals discussed the rainfall, the pre-
sent season’s crop of singers, and the last
thing in millinery. Behind H.N.S. swirled
a hot tea urn, and a meeting to promote a
concert which had nothing whatever to do
with Poet Moore. On eitherside, the far-
off echo of prolonged caterwauling came
from the secret recesses of Paling’s Build-
ings. Down a corridor ambled the benign
Kretschmann, with all his hair on end, the
wet weather having set it out in a stiff
frizz. How to convalesce in such a scene
of mixed music it’s up to “Southie” to find
out.

A wild man who doesn’t know the great
things that fill our suburban lives blisters
me with this :

VANDORIAN.
The late M. C. Davies began in Vic-
toria, and ended in Westralia, but be
left a big mark on S. Aus. during
bis most active days, before Timber took
possession of his tremendous faculties. He,
or his firm, built the Torrens bridge, now
the main thoroughfare between Adelaide city
and North Adelaide, the bridge over the rail-
way at Morphett-street, and the swing-bridge
at the Port; the railways to Largs and the
Orange, and the difficult section of the Mel-
bourne line eastward through the hills from
Mount Lofty; and, as a side-line, the long
pier at Largs Bay. It was while doir" these
things that he got the experience of karri
and jarrah which finally took him westward
to end his days as a timber expert.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, May 19, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie,
An intimate friend of Dr Mercer s writes
from Hobart that there is no truth in the
“silly rumors” about the Bishop’s re-
marrying. That being so, I’m sorry for the
lady, whoever she was: she misses a better
husband than most women have the luck
to get. Also, I am sorry for the Bishop. If
that kind of Bishop really thought of re-
marrying, the kind of lady that kind of
Bishop would naturally select would be a
credit and an honor to the household of any
kind of bishop.
Melbourne lias just acquired Dr. Sinclair
as City Health Officer in place of Dr.
Jamieson. Sinclair was assistant in Syd-
ney, and is voted hereabout an all round
good fellow.
The air is full of farewells these days,
and the intending voyager’s last cup of tea
has to be passed so fast that it spills into
the saucer and ruins the cake. Gaud
Mayoress Hennessy had only just tidied up
the Town Hall, after the big good-bye of
Tuesday to the Denman lady, when she had
to put fresh flowers in the vases and brew
some more tea for Councillor Brenan’s wife.
A purely masculine party, headed by the
Gaud Mayor, had handed over compliments
and valuable presents to the Councillor, on
Ihursday. Next day, Mrs. Brenan was
f@ted by women only, at the invitation of
Mrs. Hennessy. Gaud Mayor Hennessy, a
lone man amidst a sea of hats, loyally
helped his hospitable spouse, who beamed
on her guests from the depths of a lettuce-
green gown and chinchilla furs. Mrs.
Brenan, in black, clasped an armful of
roses. A cosy little musical treat was
snugly packed in the blue boudoir rooms
upstairs, where the company disposed them-
selves on blue and gold silk lounges, and
gazed soulfully at the few selected pictures.
Nellie Billings played Chopin to them, and
Millie Tree Chapman sang. Then every-
body admired the chrysanthemums, and pro-
ceeded quietly towards the refreshments.
Several big tea drinkings during the week
must _ have dulled even society’s immense
appetite for free food, for the big brown-
and-red Council Room held a smiling crowd
of elegant tea drinkers, all of whom were
ready to gossip politely while the cup
hovered in mid-air or reposed on the saucer.
The Charley McEvoys have shut the door
of their Cliveden flat and gone into deep
mourning for their only son. killed lately in
a gun accident in Westralia. Some years
back he married and went West with the
girl of his choice. His people did not ap-
prove, and diplomatic relations were broken
off. Lately, a kinder feeling was beginning
to assert itself, and a reunion loomed ahead.
A wedding which had been placarded for
.Saturday, May 17, was sprung as a great
surprise on the numerous friends of the
principals last week. The bride. Miss Faull,
is an artist of note and heiress to iron-
merchant Pauli, of Bendigo. The bride-
groom is Mr. Lambie, brother to the war
correspondent who was killed in South
Africa. The pair are to leave for an ex-
tended honeymoon tour by the Demosthenes
sailing on June 13.
Gaud Mayoress Hennessy invited every-
body who isn t right over the social horizon
to help say farewell to the Denman lady
ere she started for England. Her Ex., all in
brown, and with schoolgirl tails to her
sailor , hat. beamed and hand-shook her way
through the assemblage. The Town Hall,
usually so bare and comfortless, was snuglv
carpeted : the only reminder of chill was a
busy fountain which flung up spray and
gushed like the most persistent flatterer
present. The Gaudess was attired in terra
cotta silk with stamped plush trimmings—
the latter quite up to date, of course, but
very remindful of drawing-room upholster-
ings. The Barttelot lady, in purple, re-
sembied a large pansy blossom. The Frank
Madden dame, dressed severely in black
brought her two fluffy daughters, who
always favor a theatrical style of dressing.
Mrs. Geoff Syme inhabited an elegant ar-
rangement of black velvet and fox fur. Mrs.
Clarke, of Blount Gambier (S.A.), stormed
the dais and proudly presented her daughter
—a damsel wrapped expensively in ermine
from nose to heels.. Mrs. Garnett, wife of
th@ British Commissioner, in cloud-grey,
much befurred, supported an autumnal hat.
Mrs. Captain Waters, a Sydneysider, looked
quite well and blooming after her recent
illness. Mrs. Andrew Fisher was gowned
in black and white. A carefully selected
concert hanpened in the far corner. Carmen
Pinschof Harris warbled richly when the
orchestra was not sawing off lengths of
comic-opera melody. Seemingly Mrs.
Hennessy has abandoned her courageous
attempt to popularise grand-organ thunder,
hood was provided both unstairs and down;
but the rush or charge was as fierce as if
the Best Females had been housekeeping on
reduced strike pay for months. There were
almost ambulance accidents on the crowded
stairs, and the way to the ground floor
buffet was strewn with bits of ostrich plu-
mage. Still, in spite of such performances,
the novelists go on describing the way the
heroine “nibbled.” Asa hard matter of
fact, most women nearly trample one
another’s hats to death when there is free
food to “nibble”; also, they “nibble” so fast
that the plain eater with a full set of teeth
and a few manners is left at the first sand-
wich.
The Melbourne dental students and their
friends will rotate, bound and sit out, on
Wednesday, June 11, in the St. Ivilda Town
llall. the imposing building with the nice
floor and the raw brick forehead.
One of our smartest society women has
an Idea for the fancy dress frivol which is
to happen next week in a Toorak ballroom.
She is going to represent the Paris fashion
ot the day after to-morrow. The masque-
rader refuses to divulge details, but she ad-
mits this much—that the dress will
only need “a chiffon petticoat” to turn it
into a useful ball outfit.
Mrs. Arthur Guise (wife of the G.-G.’s
new private secretary) is giving ice skating
a fillip. She glides about like a swallow on
the treacherous floor, and defies the laws of
gravity in a delightful manner. An exclu-
sively private session, on Wednesday of last
week, served to introduce her to our own rink,
which only opened its doors to the public
on the following day. Lesly Madden, the
lone spinster of the Madden clan, cut her
foot rather badly at the special whirl, and
was out of action for the grand opening.
Lesly of the silvered hair skates very well,
but maintains a prunes and prism pose
from her pursed lips to her small, discreetly-
moving feet. There are three new imported
demonstrators available for learners. Two
are German and one American. One of the
Germans is a lady, and she skims about in
a dazzling manner attired in black satin
and a skirt that leaves off some time before
it reaches her boots. If she manages to
teach our skating girls some of the new
movements, the rink will become as exciting
as a ballet. There is one particular trick
which is rather like a high kick frozen in
mid-air; and it is sustained long enough to
show a generous length of silk stocking
The Best People rushed the Glaeiarium
on opening day. The Austin Wallaces, the
Arthur Robbs, and the smart McKinnon
daughters were in the van; the Frank
Madden girls arrived in a quaint sort of
jockey costume —very. tight-fitting clothes
and jockey caps. Linda Neave skimmed
about like a full-fledged angel in white satin
and ospreys topping a hat of black velvet.
Bessie Tobin wore the neatest skirt on the
ice. It was a narrow, beautifully pleated
garment wdiich seemed to stretch obligingly
at every gliding stride. The color keynote
of the dressing was black. (Last winter it
was white.) What looks like a risky inno-
vation is the fasionable V-cut in the necks
of all the smartest skating frocks. It will
take a robust girl to dodge pneumonia this
year.
Society is “taking up" the Glaciarium’s
American skating instructor—Mr. John
Schener. Said that he is a Columbia Uni-
versity man with a tremendous hockey repu-
tation ; also that he captains the New York
Wanderers—whatever they may be. On top
of all this, he has a full hand of good intro-
ductions.
Mrs. Geoff Syme gave a partv last week,
during a lucky burst of sunshine, to say
good-bye to her mother, Mrs. Garnett. Dur-
ing the function, British Commissioner Gar-
nett and sculptor Cadorin stood shoulder to
shoulder, as men should do in the presence
of overwhelming female odds. (It is only
just to add that Cadorin does social escort
duty as if he really enjoyed it.) An orches-
tra, perched in the gallery, played softly to
the hats below in the hall, and big Maggie
Stirling dropped a few contralto notes
amongst the chatter. Hostess Mrs. Geoff
wore an arrangement of flat tulle and
big velvet choux (as the French call
their fascinating little cabbages). Her
dress was of black, filmy lace that
foamed up waist-high on a white gown.
(I forget for the moment whether the white
foundation was of calico or silk.) Mrs. Gar-
nett supported an artistic blending of neutral
tints in fluffy materials. Mrs Hennessv
wore a large dead fowl on her head, and its
wonderful butter-colored tail feathers swept
the surrounding scenery like cheese knives.
(Memo. Better move on to the next course.)
On Wednesday, last week. Archibald
Strong gave the Victorian Artists’ Society
a dose he called “Elizabethan Poetrah,” and
the cultured souls present mopped up every
drop of it The dead weight of Archibald’s
leckchaw was lightened somewhat by old
English ballads sung by the best Wieder-
mann pupils. Max Pirani, a budding Pader-
ewski of 13 summers, set two Fletcher poems
to music specially for the occasion, and
worked the piano himself while two selected
young nightingales trilled their best. He was
well petted afterwards for his achievement
and compliments flew in all directions. It
all ended in coffee and buns.
„/'] ara u tt arrived in Melbourne, on
W ednesday of last week, and was received
at the Auditorium by about 2000 of the
ery Best People. (Where are New York’s
lour Hundred now?) She had just driven
up from the train with husband Kennerlev
Kumford ; but of course a star doesn’t wear
the bashed expression of the ordinary train
traveller. Clara looked an abridged 'edition
of her abundant self in a black tb P
severity of which was intensified, more or
less, by a Peter Pan collar. While the black
dress made her look slighter, a flat hat had
a shortening effect. Kennerley Rumford’s
clothes were of the latest London cut. (Per-
haps you expected him to arrive in a few
odd duds picked up at one of the sailors’
outfitting shops in Flinders-street V If you
did, you were woefully mistaken). A steady
stream of adoring women filed past the dais,
and the large Clara found it necessary to
come down from her perch and stand foot to
foot, when it came to grips with the throng.
Great sheaves of autumn leaves were heaped
about the hall. The visitors wandered
round the double horseshoe galleries and
invaded the domed orchestra seats, where
they discovered that a low conversational
tone on the platform carried right to the
door. Afternoon tea was laid upstairs in the
lounge, in order to draw the great crowd
from the floor. It drew with such power
that the staircase was the scene of several
scrambles. Women will not observe the rule
of the stairway, and they came up and de-
scended on whichever side they happened to
be. Concert halls and theatres should
display placards, “CLIMBERS KEEP TO
THE RIGHT.”
At the Clara Butt reception, last week,
the worshippers were moving briskly past
the smiling singer when there was a "sudden
stoppage, and a small apoplectic official was
seen to be holding back the puzzled proces-
sion of handshakers. The traffic had been
held up by a Most Expensive matron who,
having secured Clara’s fist, moored herself
firmly, and stood chattering, just as though
she were yarning over the backyard fence
and had the whole day to spare. The man
who was holding the crowd in leash yelled
the next name three times, and was prepar-
ing to shriek for assistance, when the chatty
dame finished her three-volume conversation
and moved on to Kennerley Rumford to tell
the story of her life all over again. The
people who followed her had to be satisfied
with a mere touch of fingertips. Even Clara
showed symptoms of panic until the garru-
lous one moved on.
Artist Penleigh Boyd, just back from
Europe, is now hanging out at the Guild
Hall. is to say, he has hung out
about 70 landscapes on a buff background.
On invitation day, Pen’s pretty wife, in
wedgwood blue silk and Titian hair, looked
very proud, as she shook hands with her
husband’s old friends. Pen himself looks
absurdly young, in spite of the years he has
spent abroad and all his matrimonial cares.
His very fine brush work startled even his
closest friends into adjectives and pur-
chases, while envious artist brethren seemed
to be wondering how he did it. The Roval
Academy picture, “Spring at St. Ives,” is
mostly blue and sunlight, with several
tubesful of Chinese white in the foreground.
Somebody asserted that it was a bush of
blossom. The Continenfal water colors are
quite easy to translate, in fact they have
the same blue in their shadows as the local
gum-tree efforts. Pen uses cobalt where
others slap on shaded mud ; it is like having
good silk for a costume foundation instead
of shoddy sateen. The Pen Boyds are lo-
cated at the Yarra Glen homestead. It was
handed over to them by artist father Arthur
Boyd, who has transplanted the family to
the smoky city.
The business side of Pen Boyd’s picture
show is managed by Alma Cockburn, a
winsome young person with chestnut curls
and the figure of a nymph. Alma first ap-
peared on the horizon of Melbourne’s art-
istic world when the Cadorin couple ex-
hibited their hor tuny mantles and scarves
in the Centreway studio, the other day
Lovie Mueller found her (her people are
the Cockburns), and gave her the glad hand.
Now all the painting tribe are falling over
themselves and begging her to manage their
shows. I atrons are apt to find enthusiasm
and hard cash for canvas and frames, when
the sales are effected through an attractive
little agent with dimples.
, Lovie Cadorin is expecting another swag
of the fiortuny visions by an early mail
1 hese are to be exhibited to a chosen few
of Sydney’s Best People. Meantime, the
tew dreams left over from the last exhibi-
tion are being snapped by casual visitors
to the Cadorin refuge at Whitehall. The
man in the street can look out for a Bvzan-
tine-Greek winter helped out with gossamer
scarves.
The Australian Art Association has covered
the interior walls of the Athenaeum Hall with
the exhibits of its first show. The Denman
lady did the opening trick, on Wednesday of
last week, when enough people crowded in to
effectually lnde the pictures. Help Barttelot
followed her Ex who was garbed (not
garbled, please !) in tobacco brown velvet,
and supported a brown cartwheel hat with
long ribbon ends falling over the back rim.
Edward Officer can orate well only with his
brush, so he mumbled some introductory re-
marks from a crumpled scrap of paper,’ and
looked wretched in a most respectful wav.

lound the walls as if in search of the art

On both Goodwood and Cup days it was cold and
grey—ideal weather for racing, if a trifle cool fov
cup finery The Bosanquets proceeding
from a bedecked box,; and changed the sma, f
built frocks they wore On Goodwood Dai—Ladv h\
was black, and her daugter’s a bluish-violet—f or
coats and skirts on C'up Day. Lady Bosanquet wore
creme, with touches of biaek, and Miss Bosanauet
w,'iu ,Ue ' Mrs ‘ J)lek Hawker surrounded herself
with vieux rose on Saturday, and on Monday she

sartorial eccentricities of the meeting were a hat
closely set with small wings of every hue, from
orange to emerald green, and a “peg-top” skirt-
much pouched on the hips and very scant about the
snides.
The Alan McFarlanes, who have taken a house at
Kensington for the winter, gave a cheerv dance on
Friday evening. Our young people—or at any
i ate the nuttier” section of them—are hard at
work trying to learn the Gaby Glide, the Turkey Trot
and so on. They make rather heavy weather of it
so tar. But, mayhap, an air of assured and graceful
ease will come with earnest practice.
The Orsova put in here on Friday with Lady Den-
man and Major Quilter on board. They were at
once taken to Government House and carefully fed.
Captain Nutting came by train from Melbourne, and
joined the boat here.
Miss Dorothea Proud, winner of the C. II. Spence
Scholarship, left for England on Friday. The
Blackwood Club farewelled her a few days before-
hand.
The Ernest Toms have gone to live in London for
good. He has been connected with Good, Toms and
Co. for about 25 years.
The engagement is announced of Dr. H. M. Jay
(known better as “Budge”) to Miss von Doussa, of
Hahndorf—daughter of the sporting M.L.C.
Race week filled the town with Bowmans, McFar-
lanes, Hawkers, Barkers, and so on. Really,
my dear, we feel at these times not so
much a centre of culture as of agriculture.
Renmark fruitgrowers, who are sick of trying
to dry their fruit when there’s no drying
weather, were down in force. Also, the F.
S. Wvllies, Mrs. Jim Evans, Mrs. Woodham and her
children, and her sister-in-law, Mrs. Woodward. She
is staying till the end of the month, to meet her
mother and sister, who are on their wav out from
England.
A burglar burst into M'11swood, the stately abode
of Clem Hill, the cricketer, on Sunday morning and
stole P 500 worth of iewellery. I forget the exact
particulars, but I believe the loot included a gold
presentation watch with “160” worked on it in
diamonds (160 being one of our champion's cricket
scores) ; a pin, with a d'amond bat displayed upon
it : and other valuables, among them a gold bangle
with all Clem’s “not out” scores inscribed on it in
diamonds and rubies, and a platinum chest protector
with portraits of both the English and Australian
XT’s of the black year in which the Ashes were
last wrested from us, done on it in seed pearls—a
particularly chaste design. I am enabled to state
definitely that the burglar was a man of considerable
daring and strength. I haven’t seen him, but I know
that this is so. You see, Clem Hill is six feet high,
about 14 stone in weight, and a fairly speedy
runner ; and the malefactor, whoever he was, dragged
a safe weighing 3cwt. for ahout lOOvds. before he
blew it up. Do you follow the deductive processes
of my penetrating intellect?
Sincerely yours,
Johanna.

“Micko”: Just read the argument re sewing sacks
behind a chaff-cutting mach ne in an old Bully.
Don’t know who is the champion, but knew a man
on the Warwick Downs who could do his 1000 a day
easy—but, then, he was sewing Smith’s once-used
Bags. This man won about a dozen contests, but he
always stipulated that Smith's sacks were to be used
in the contest. I wondered, until I found that
Smith’s sacks were so scientifically selected that they
were equal to new (Brand XX) for sewing purposes,
and held a greater amount of chaff. Now that I
am a “boss” I always buy from the sack specialists,
T. Smith and Co., Ltd., Sussex Street, Sydney.*
