[James Ed*qnd, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
As showing the keen, almost consuming,
interest taken in forthcoming House-of-
Rich-Landlord elections in the vicinity of
Melbourne there is the case of one candidate
who practically carries his enthusiastic
audience with him. He travels with three
motor-cars, all loaded, so that when keeping
advertised appointments with the enlightened
and discriminating electors in the suburbs he
may not be called upon to address utter
blankness. On three occasions in May this
spirited politician had an audience of 17
people—just the number carried in his
motor-cars.
C. G. Wade, at Manly (N.S.W.), advised
all those who hadn’t time or opportunity to
study the Referendum proposals to “play
for safety and vote No.” Apart altogether
from the degradation of appealing for the
support of Ignorance and Apathy, Wade’s
plea will at any rate provide the reason for
another Referendum pretty soon if the an-
swer this time is No. When the State
Fright leaders appeal for the votes of those
who haven’t had time or opportunity for
study, all that will be necessary to justify
another Referendum is to show 7 that the
people have been given the time and oppor-
tunity which they didn’t have before.
[S. H. Prior, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
One grieves to record that Vic. Speaker
Frank Madden has forgotten the traditions
of his office. Recently, at Camberwell (Vic.),
he described the six Referenda questions as
six baits, and hazarded the opinion that if
the Australian public accepted one or more
of those baits, they (the public) would be
‘‘yanked into the Labor basket and solemnly
dished.” Continuing his conversation, the
artist in mixed metaphor later replied to an
interjector of “Yah!” w 7 ith the classic in-
quiry, “Did that dog bark?” Such lan-
guage might be expected from an unregener-
ate Labah person, but, coming from a monu-
ment of Culchaw, in such a bell-toppered
neighborhood as Camberwell, it calls for
boiling tears.
[Charles Richard Brarish, The Bulletin
Office, 369 Collins-street, Melbourne.]
Bourke (Vic.) can usually be looked to
for some entertainment at election-time;
and the candidature of ex-parson F. M.
Robb, opposing Anstey,
the retiring M.H.R.,
makes for positive joy.
Lately Robb alleged that
Anstey had “made a de-
liberate attempt to induce
the belief that there was
some discreditable reason
at the back of his leav-
ing the Church.” Anstey
called on Robb to sub-
stantiate the allegation;
whereupon Robb, admit-
ting hastily that there
was nothing objectionable
in Anstey’s “spoken
words.” solemnly declared
that his adversary had
made his attack by “nods
and winks, blinks and
pantomime.” In support
of this extraordinary
statement, affidavits were
collected from 16 persons
present at Anstey’s meet-
ing, 14 of them being
women. Grabbing his op-
portunity by its stream-
in g whiskers, Anstey
landed his opponent with
a little work of literary
art, entitled “Nods and
Win k s a' n d Blinks,”
which had an immediate
and tremendous vogue
among the unregenerate
of Bourke. Nowadays,
Robb spends most of his
spare time painfully
thinking up some new re-
buke, dignified and ex-
parsonical, with which to
floor the unladylike Anstey.
[Frank Henty, Glen Iris, Victoria.]
The “go-slow” doctrinaires have no sense
of humor. Letter-sorters at Sydney G.P.O.
see nothing to laugh at in their complaint
that “narks” are secreted above the sorting
room to watch them at work and report
them if they “loaf.” Here is a good oppor-
tunity for a Fusion orator to step in and
harangue the letter-sorters re the tyranny
of Labor Government.
[Edmund Fisher, The Bulletin Office, 369 Col-
lins-street, Melbourne. ]
It seems that Cook’s policy speech at
Parramatta (N.S.AV.) was not fully re-
ported, and the details that would have
laid him open to scathing criticism were
carefully dropped by the kindly reporters.
Both the Sydney morning papers merely
mentioned that Mr. Cook would, if re-
turned to power, evolve a scheme of
National Insurance on the lines that had
proved successful in the Old World, or
words to that effect. Joe Cook now rises in all
his glory to state that his scheme, as out-
lined at Parramatta, would provide for sick-
ness, accidents, maternity, widowhood and
unemployment on a contributory basis.
Which means, of course, that it would be
national and compulsory. Unfortunately,
unless this Referendum which Joe so fiercely
denounces is carried, he cannot do more than
mature his scheme on paper. Under the
existing Constitution he can collect only
from firms and individuals who hover be-
tween two or more States. He cannot col-
lect one tattered sprat from either an em-
ployer or an employee who is a fixture in
any one State.
[C. A. Jeffries, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
HOW WE THRIVE.
“Do one of me like that old chap
and I'll tell you what I’ll do, I —I— I'll
get it framed.”
Says Willy Kelly:—
The Government proposals are not for the grant-
ing of new powers to the Australian people, but for
the transfer of powers which the Australian people
already possess from the State Parliaments to the
Federal Parliament.
And therein Willy speaks the truth, or some
of the truth. He speaks just as much of the
truth as he would, if, for instance, he said
that, having an 18-horse-power motor-car, he
can do as much as a man who has 18 horses,
and no more. William knows (probably)
that, though his 18-h.p. car may be no more
powerful than 18 horses, yet the 18 horses
would most likely not put out all their
power together, whereas it is a dead-sure
thing that the power of 18 horses, put into
one machine, will be all exercised at once.
Incidentally, if Willy thinks it the right
thing for the Australian people, to keep six
engines going to do one job, will he pledge
himself to apply the same principle to him-
self, and, instead of an 18-h.p. engine, when
he next buys a. car, will he see that it is
equipped with six separate engines of 3 h.o.
each? It will be a weird and ungainly
circus; still, what’s good enough for Willy
to recommend to Australia ought to be good
enough for him to recommend to himself.
[S. H. Prior, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
“Orient”: Cabled that the Bagdad railroad
is to go ahead. This is of profound interest
to Australia in more than one aspect. The
first section is from Scutari, opposite Con-
stantinople, to Ivonia, 480 miles. Thence
the line will go to Adana, Mosul, Bagdad,
Busra. The route is over the Euphrates,
along by the Anti-Taurus range to Ilelii and
Koweit, on the Persian Gulf. It is palpable
that, in a future time, with the Westralian
and Bagdad railways built, Australians will
get from Sydney to London in a week less,
slow sea-voyaging being reduced to a mini-
mum.
A good and timely advertisement for the
Fisher Government is its new ten-shilling
note. It is elegantly designed and well-
printed, and, unlike the old half-sovereign,
even the most beered citizen can’t give it
away for sixpence in the dark. The only
objection is that it is a shade too large.
The ideal note should go into an ordinary
pocket book without folding. When the five-
shilling bit of paper monev comes out the
Government might remember this.
[Written on the home verandah early one Sabbath
morn, when the crass was green, and the sky was
blue, and the harbor looked like a sea of glass full
of the upside-down shadows of ships, and the Lord
had forgotten to take in the faded circle of the
moon, and to stow it away in the cash-box among the
other sixpences of Heaven— by James Edmond, of
The Bulletin Office, Sydney. Excuse the length of
the signature.]

The Bulletin has received
three letters this week re the
advisability of cutting down the
working day to six hours. And
dunng the last ten months the
single State of N.S.W. has bor-
rowed from foreign capitalists
some £0,000,000 or £7,0()OOW
because it didn’t do enough
work —and consequently didn’t
earn enough money—to’ pay its
way. It has left the bill to
Posterity in other words it
has pawned the infant so that
the old man may have time to
go to the football match. There
is no reason why the working
day shouldn’t be cut down to
four hours —provided man will
first hurry his brains into the
front line of battle and invent
such machinery as will enable
him to cover current expenses
and keep civilisation advancing
with the proceeds of four hours’
work. At present, the evidence
supplied by the growth of the
Australian public debts sug-
gests that this country wants
to work an hour extra!
C. G. Wade’s proposal to de-
feat the nefarious practices of
the Australian burglar would
apparently be the abolition of
police protection, and the unre-
stricted admission of the foreign
felon, so that competition might
render the predatory profession
unprofitable to the local Sikes
At any rate he proposes to
get rid of the trust and monopoly curse in
Australia by refusing the Commonwealth
power to control local trusts and monopolies,
and removing the Protective duties so as to
allow any other old trust to come in and
feece us.
[J. Mahony, Bancroft-road, Roseville, N.S.W.]

[E. COLLES, Kogarah, Sydney.]
The final Melbourne sitting of the Domin-
ions Commission discovered two interesting
witnesses. The first, from the Chamber of
Commerce, spoke concerning the desirability
of uniform patent laws throughout the Em-
pire. He rather approved of the patent
laws of England, Australia and Maoriland,
but considered those of Canada “particularly
harsh.” There, he said, the importation of
any locally-patented article within one year
was prohibited, and if it was not manufac-
tured within the Dominion within two years,
the local patent became void. After this
witness came a manufacturer of boots, who
complained deeply of a notorious American
company which had a monopoly of certain
patent boot-making machines, and stuck to
it by the simple method of merely leasing its
machines, and stipulating that no other
machines should be used in the factory, im®
agreement, he said, had been declared valid
by the English Courts ; but Canada had de-
clared it invalid and a breach of the Anti-
Trust Act. Exactly! Canada protected it-
self and its manufacturers; the other c °h
tries didn’t. The two witnesses could harm
have put the Freetrade and the Protecti
policy more succinctly.
[F. C. Jones, Elgar-road, Burwood, Victoria.]

MY LAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I saw my land, my maiden.
The dripping sap of flower stems had made her good
sword rust.
Its virgin point was buried in the flowers.
I said, “Who guards this dreaming girl of ours?
Her girdle gold with sapphire gems, her feet with
pollen dust?”
A blundering moth the scattered petals stirred.
There was no answer. Only one mad bird
Voicing the ecstasy of that wild wood
Carolled as though the world was always spring,
While Vision came to me....a horrid thing....
When those white feet went reeling deep in blood.
I saw my land.... my maiden
Whose honey mouth of beauty mould, wine and honey
quaffed.
Even the wise bees for her left the flowers.
I wept: “Who guards this plenteous queen of ours?”
Far off a war drum r011ed.... and those who heard it,
laughed!
QUEENSLAND.

A COLE DALE MINER.
FOUND DR. MORSE’S INDIAN ROOT
PILLS A SPLENDID REMEDY
FOR MUSCULAR RHEUMATISM.
“Rheumatism is a very common ailment
amongst miners, owing to their calling often
requiring them to work in a stooping atti-
tude in dark, damp places. Mr. Herbert
Jenkins, of Coledalc, a coal miner at South
Clifton Colliery, NTS AY., relates in the fol-
lowing statement how he was attacked with
this painful complaint, which doubled him
up for about 18 months, and in suite of
medical treatment was unable to obtain re-
lief. Finally a friend induced him to take
Dr. Morse’s 'lndian Root Pills, with the re-
sult that within two weeks he was com-
pletely relieved and able to resume work.
“Being a sufferer,” writes Mr. Herbert
Jenkins, “from muscular rheumatism for a
period of 18 months, and frequently doubled
up with a bad leg and stooping form, I sub-
mitted my malady to a local doctor who
considered my case a bad one. I commenced
to despair of ever recovering my old form
of robust health asmin, being a young mar-
ried man with wife and family, a"d the
necessities of work as a miner compelled me
to get well as soon as possible. I was in-
duced by a friend to try Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills, and after a course of your medi-
cine for a period of two weeks, I was en-
abled to return to the colliery at Smith
Clifton. I now recommend Indian Root
Pills to my own friends and acquaintances,
feeling sure they effected a speedy cure in
my case.”*

CHICAGO
SPORTING
NOTIONS
The latest challenger of all comers in the
pugilistic line is that well-known athlete Colonel de
Laune Ryrie, Liberal candidate for the federal
constituency of North Sydney. Hardly a meeting
passes without this polished speaker threatening
somebody to “wipe him out.” The bluster grows weari-
some. The writer rises to affirm it as his solemn
opinion that the gallant Colonel is what the Yankee
sporting papers call “a false alarm” ; meaning
thereby that he fights better with his mouth than
his hands, and his language is infinitely more
terrible than his fist. Writer has fortunately escaped
so far the horror of seeing the Colonel stripped, but
judging from outward appearances, he couldn’t fight
for nuts.
[C. A. Jeffries, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.l
The latest news of Johnson was that he
had been committed for trial on somewhere between
seven and a dozen charges of abducting, procuring
and other things under the White Slave Acts. It
seems that the craving- of our colored brother for
White Mary is overwhelming; and many penalties
cannot quench it. And White Mary, finding a gilded
path open to her, slides into it easily with a smug
smile and a plan of future blackmail in her heart.
Unfortunately all the girl® who fall into the clutches
of these lascivious blacks are not “White. Maries”
within the meaning of the colloquial sm, and decent
girls have been brought to ruin and degradation
through the huge nigger organisation that is said to
have branches and agencies all over ‘ the United
States. There is quite a large circle of wealthy
colored men in the Un ted States, most of whom
have made their money by running saloons with
white managers, and by pandering to the secret vices
of the wealthy ruffians of New York, Chicago and
other Get-Rich-Quickly cities. The organisation
alluded to is said to have as subscribers about 85
p e. of the wealthy negroes of the Union. The
object of the co is to allure by gilded promises, or
to acquire by straight-out kidnapping, or any other
means that present themselves, white girls to whom
subscribers have taken a fancy. The great
recruiting ground of the organisation is the vaude-
ville stage, and in a less degree the ord nary theatre.
A girl named Belle Schroder was making much
gilt and a mighty name in Pittsburg on the variety
stage by the daring of her impersonations and the
chanciness of her songs The rugged steel and iron
workers of that smoky town like their wit blue and
their jokes high. Belle supplied the demand. Belle
was notorious, according to the general account.
Terrific stories were told of auction sales of Belle’s
company for a few hours; sales at which Belle,
draped mostly in wreaths of beauty roses (a special
production of Yankee horticulture) was handed over,
blushing, to the h'ghest bidder. Belle could blush
at a moment’s notice, it seemed ; and found it a
mighty asset. The fame of her beauty, her spright-
liness, her accomplishments and so forth reached, it
is said, a certain nigger. Belle had refused to have
dealings with negroes. So she was kidnapped and sent
by a spec'al train to Chicago, and taken in a closed
car from the tsaip to a quiet house in the suburbs.
But Belle’s following swore to discover what had
become of her The negro organisation was sus-
pected. The following was wealthy. It poured out
money like water to find its Belle, and did so.
The following, in the course of its investigations,
accumulated a vast mass of startling evidence, which
ft placed in the hands of the Federal law authori-
ties, and the net result is that Johnson is committed
to take his trial on a large number of charges under
the White Slave Act.
* * * *
That McCoy had recovered his old punch
and his former vim became very apparent when he
got into the ring of the Baker Stadium, Sydney,
with A If Spencelev, of England, on Saturday night.
All the time he worried in, bored in, and bulldogged
generally. But had the writer been in the referee’s
boots Herbert McCov would have been outed for
hitting after being told to break, and doing some-
thing very like holding with one hand and bashing
with the other. McCoy Is a splendid fighter, quite
able to win the majority of his battles cleanly and
fairly, but he takes chances which may eventually
make him intensely unpopular. The time is ripe
for Herbert to take a look at himself and consider
his blemishes. Spenceley had been promised a clean
break, and he didn’t get it. He was bashed and
banged at close quarters in what was nothing less
than in-fighting; and at last the police took excep-
tion to it. After that, the clean break was enforced,
and the Englishman began to pile up ooints very
rapidly, till in the 12th. with a fine left and a
heaw right-cross, he shook McCoy where he lived.
In the 15th McCoy came back, once more got in
close, and put in some violent body rips. Spencelev
came up for the 16th, but he had not recovered,
and something that looked like a white eagle flew
in the air. It proved on insnection by competent
botanists to be the towel, of all things in the world.

Johnny Summers struck trouble at Bris-
bane at the Baker Stadium, in Cremorne Gardens,
on Saturday night when he met Stone, of America,
in a 20-round assault and battery. It is said to
have been a delightful combat, fast, clean, pretty
and mighty clever throughout. Johnny’s guardian
angels were off duty that night, and though the
Englishman touched the scapular in his sock un-
failingly every round, he was outed on points bv
the referee, who happened to be the great “Snowy”
himself.
Midnight Sun’s brilliant victory in the
Adelaide Cup was in accordance with recent whisper-
ings. The Maoriland horse was said to be a good
thing for the. first big race he started in, and the
Adelaide Cup was quite big enough. As a 4-year-
old with Bst. 91b. up, the winner looks likely to cut
an important figure in handicaps and w.f.a races at
Sydney and Melbourne next spring, but he is not
likely to get another chance of meeting Jolly Beggar
at an advantage of 211 b. in the weights. Mountain
Princess did well to carry 9st. into second place.
The consistent black mare is smaller than most of
them, and in a big field of horses is always in danger
of being bumped into obscurity.
* * * *
“Nemo”: Elliot Grant, just dead in
France, spent most of his life as secretary to George
Adams, of “Tattersall’s.” When “Tatt.” found no
resting place for his boots in the mainland of Aus-
tralia his aide-de-camp went to Tasmania with him.
I first knew h : m over a quarter of a century ago at
Redfern, Sydney. I was then the whole reporting
staff of the Redfern Times. Grant was clerk in a
Sydney beer factory. Some years after I joined the
staff of the morning paper at Hobart, and I reported
Premier Braddon when he moved in the House that
“Tattersall” be allowed to pitch his sportive tent in
Tasmania. Parliament gave leave, and Adams and
his confidential man came. . Like h : s chief. Grant
had a wide and well-deserved popularity, and every-
body still alive who knew him will be sorry that he
is gone. Also, everybody dead who knew him will
be glad that he is come.

Totalisator “fractions” make a nice pick-
ing in S. Aus. for charities, 'ihe machine, as run
in that State, pays only to the nearest shilling, and
the surplus bulks up to surprising sums, if favorites
get home. Obviously, if the winner had only 20
backers, the fractions would not be quite
205., while they might be anything up to
the verge of £5O where 1000 tickets were
taken on him. Hence, the good men who con-
trol charitable institutions, though not as a rule a
race-going crowd themselves, are understood to pray
fervently that favorites may get home. The one-day
Oakbank meeting has just found itself with £ll5
to distribute in this way. By the way, it is pretty
certain that the Totalisator Act does not authorise
the withholding of any of the moneys invested, beyond
the race-club’s 7$ per cent., and (latterly) the
00-.-r'rnpipnt’s 24 per cent. So the whole “fraction”
business must be illegal; but no successful backer
has yet been known to bring an action against the
Club for his missing 4|d.

The third day of the South Australian
Jockey' Club’s Autumn Meeting was remarkable for
the consistency of the dividends paid by the machine.
The Second Hurdle paid investors £8 12s. for each
quid on the winner. The S.A.J.C. Handicap saw
winners draw £8 3s. ; the Juvenile Handicap yielded
up £8 13s. on the winner and £4 18s on the
second brute; and the Tote Handicap panned out
£5 18s. on the first steed and £2 on the one that
drove him home. The lowest dividend was £2 135.,
paid by the red-hot favorite Quickshot in the Fisher
Stakes.

The star match of the Sydney League
Rugby competition on Saturday was that between
Balmain and South Sydney at the Cricket Ground.
Over 20,000 people waded out through the slush and
dirt, and faced the biting wind and advised the
players “to get into it, like chickens into mud!’’
The cause of the excitement was that, till that after-
noon, neither of the high conflicting parties had been
licked, and both sides reckoned it was the other
fellow’s funeral. A scrap of the sort is always in-
teresting. This one opened with a loose sort of
scramble, in which both parties lost their feet im-
partially for about a quarter-hour or so, and then
they both got properly going, and the slush began
to fly. It was a wonderfully even game, and at half-
time Balmain was leading by six points to five.
After that, the men from the Tattered Suburb
walked over South Sydney for a while, and with
only 25 minutes to go, were leading by 10 to five.
Then South opened up the game, increased the speed,
and with a series of splendid long-pass rushes over-
whelmed Balmain, and made the score: Balmain 10 ;
South 16. It was an amazing recovery, or masterly
restraint, or sudden flying off the handle, or some-
thing on the part of South Sydney. Some say it
was the finger of Providence. Others regard it as
the cloven hoof of the Devil.
**•*■*
The most surprised team in the Sydney
League div sion was Eastern Suburbs when it found
itself called upon to put up the feet of its life to
keep Western Suburbs from dancing on its chest.
At half-time the Western team led by five to four,
and the Eastern barracker was dumb with terror and
blue with rage, for Western Sydney is a weak team
owing to the ravages of the Soccer game among the
young material. But this particular day that weak
side had the champion team tied in a knot, and
Eastern charges could not break down its defence
nor cross its line. It was the long-range deadly
kicking that changed history on this occasion, and
Eastern Suburbs defeated the West per medium of
four penalty kicks, which left the figures East nine,
West five. North Sydney and Newtown raged out
at’ Erskineville Park, and at half-time North was
dancing on Newtown’s honor and dignity to the tune
of five to two. But at the resumption Newtown got its
system to work, and burst through twice. Adding the
extras on each occasion, it made the score 10 to
five. North lay low, apparently accepting its fate,
till just before time a perfect dribble took the ball
to within striking distance, a two-man passing
charge took it over, the goal was kicked, and as the
whistle blew the scores were equal—lo all. Some-
thing Went wrong with Annandale on Saturday, and
that usually doughty, hard-battling, though unlucky
team crumpled right up before the big, beefy
warriors from Glebe, who ran over it by 29 to nil.
That was the only one-sided game of the afternoon.
This leaves Glebe, Eastern Suburbs and South Syd-
ney in the front rank with six competition points
each. Balmain has fallen back, or, rather, stood
still at four. North Sydney and Newtown have one
each, and Annandale and Western Suburbs hang out
the 15th letter of the alphabet.
* * * *
In the Sydney Rugby Union Competition
only Eastern Suburbs and North Sydney remain un-
beaten. All the others have had to grovel. East
got loose on St. George on Saturday, and the Saints
had an awful time of it, eventually going out by 22
points to three; which is a blazing disaster at the
Union game. Western Suburbs fared little better
than St. George at the boots of Newtown, who
kicked it hard by 22 points to five. University and
Glebe put up a very rapid mill at the University
Oval. Mill's “Essay on Liberty” was nothing to
it. The combatants got all over one another, and
each rolled the other mob cheerfully in the slush.
When it was all over, and the parties wiped the
slush out of its rema ning eye to gaze at the board,
it was seen University had beaten Glebe by 16 to
14. It was a great and glorious mill. Another
surprised mob was that from South Sydney, which
took the sea voyage to Manly, and got comfortably
belted by the Villagers, who scored eight points to
South’s three, Balmain called on North Sydney,
climbed the mountains, and reached the playing
ground apparently in an exhausted state, as North
Sydney crowd immediately fell upon it, got it down,
and walloped it by eight points to three. So
Eastern Suburbs and North Svdney are each six
points, Newtown and University four each; Bal-
main, Glebe, South Sydney, St. George and Manly
are two each. Western Suburbs hoists the sign that
is nothing with a hole in the middle of it.
* * * *
Saturday in Soccer circles in and around
Sydney was the day of the dog, and the underneath
dog at that. The angels of victory hovered around
the teams that were regarded as weaklings, and n-
tended specially by Providence to be chopping blocks
and score-makers for the m ght.v ones. Even the
terrible Sydney Club, the holders of the coveted
Rawson Cup, could not prevail against one of these
babe ’bailers —Canterbury—which made a draw of
it with one goal. At Epping, Glebe went up against
Annandale team with the fixed determination of
throwing its bones into a tree. Instead, Glebe
crawled home dead with its tombstone under its arm,
and the record was three goals to nil. On the same
ground Balmain rushed at Granville, got it down and
walked over it, also by three to nil. The supreme
curse of the afternoon in th's section, however,
took place at Sydenham, where Newtown gave the
Northern Suburbs team indecent burial by nine goals
to two.
There’s a whisper of discontent among
the N.S.W. League footballers. The League compe-
tition is supposed to be run on a district basis, but
some of the districts which have lately acquired
crack players find that these desirable young men
are st'll playing with their old clubs under a system
of permits. The League executive, it seems, is in-
clined to let a man play out the season with the
team in which he started the season ; and there is a
lot to be said for the idea. Of course, the teams
which, under a cast-iron district system, would have
acquired these tall fellows of their hands (to use
the Engl'sh of the Middle Ages) believe in a cast-
iron district system for the time. But should any
of their own crack players be forced by the vagaries
of the landlord, or the incidence of employment, or
pressure of creditors, or any other old reason, to
shift, it’s a million to a trouser button they would
object strenuously to a cast-iron district system.
The Ass, games (Melbourne) furnished a
few surprises and some annoyance to the various
barrackers. P’ran encountered Brunswick and
outed it, by means of reasonably stra ght kicking,
by three points. ’Wick’s habit of piling up a
Kosciusko of behinds -to a mere handful of goals,
will bring it serious disaster; only the perfect
’bailer can alwavs kick goals, but a team with a
record of 87 behinds out of 122 scor ng shots on
four successive Saturdays is not fully seized of the
elements of the game. ’Seray, keeping level with
North t'll the last quarter, held out better and finished
11 points ahead —62 to 51. Essendon squelched Port
by 72 to 56. Brighton jumped on Northcote with
81 to 69 ; also, ’Town dispersed Melbourne Citv by
14 goals nine behinds to seven goals e'ght behinds.
As) ’Town is itself a very trifling sort of circum-
stance, near the tail of the procession, this writer
would seriously urge on the citizens the advisability
of committing hara-kari or some other form of anni-
hilation. Their persistent habit of losing by a
million points, more or less, is a menace to society,
and threatens to drive capital out of the country.
Also, it is dragging down the pillars of the Consti-
tution, and causing conscription, trades unionism,
strikes, un'fication and the speeches of Joseph Cook.
[Signed in humble, not to say abject and grovel-
ling, conformity with the Act, by F. C. Jones, of
Elgar-road, Burwood, Victoria, who hereby smites
the dust with his forehead and accepts all responsi-
bility and remuneration.]

Previous to the week-end games the Ass.
independent tribunal considered the case of a Port
player whom Brighton’s committee had complained
against for resorting to “stoush.” Not one of the
five umpires had seen the incident, though a knock-
out blow on a football field usually attracts atten-
tion, but there was, apparently, no doubt about the
fact, and the three arbiters handed out a sentence of
12 months’ compulsory retirement from the game.

seven when the last term began. Then Carlton had
the wind, and started business, but found itself up
aga nst a wall of stiff defence. Its marking and

’Wood simultaneously added seven, which left it a
goal to the good—6 goals 8 beh : nds to

“Magpie”: Is a Steamer a boat? The
problem is disturbing the various State rowing asso-
ciations, for thereon hangs the result of the inter-
State eight-oared race, rowed (and swum) recently in
Adelaide. Under rowing rules an amateur cannot
work “in or about boats.” The stroke of the
Murray Bridge crew, which represented S.A. and
reached the shore first, is capta n qf a Murray River
steamer. He thought so little of his job as a train-
ing f° r the race that he took three weeks leave with-
out pay to prepare, nevertheless the autocrats of
rowing are likely to decide that he is a tainted
profess onal Possibly his craft has at times to he
rowed, pulled or tugged off mudbanks and he has
acquired much improper not to sav illegal, brawn
anil smew thereby Anvhow, each State Association
will be asked its learned opinion.

THE BEST-
Thus Joe Cook, when leaving Victoria to
spread the darkness in Tasmania: —
It all boils down to this, that what is needed is
a sympathetic Minister, and not a new Anti-Trust
Act.
But a chunk of sympathy as big as Mount
Macedon is merely useless lumber while the
High Court forbids its use. And, anyhow,
would a Cook Government, if such a political
calamity eventuated, supply the “sympa-
thetic Minister” to put the 1910 Anti-Trust
Act to any use?
[M. Benson, Ultima, Victoria.]
The big dailies and the little politicians
are kicking against what they call “politi-
cal unionism.” The other sort of unionism—
the industrial sort—is all right, they say;
in fact, it is a boshter idea, which ought to
be encouraged. But when the unions go
into politics, it is tyranny and the subver-
sion of the sacred rights of the citizen and
a lot of other things. These big dailies and
little politicians have very short memories.
They forget that, in the early ’90’s, when
the workers were relying on industrial
unionism, and were in consequence striking
wholesale and making a mess of things in
general, they were told to drop that ruinous
nonsense and look instead to the ballot-box
and cons’tootional means. In short, the
right-thinking person told the worker to do
exactly what he is doing to-day; and if the
worker now and then shows some impati-
ence with the men and the papers that gave
him advice and now curse him for taking it,
who can blame him?
[J. Grills, Wallan, Vic.]


Sore Throat and Influenza
THE COMMONEST WINTER COMPLAINTS:
THEIR PREVENTION AND CURE.
When the days are cold and wet in winter, disease
germs have a much greater power over man than
they have in summer. The reason is that chill and
damp lower the vitality and render the body less
liable to withstand the attack of the germs and to
destroy them.
Although Nature has made a wonderful provision
for meeting these germ attacks by delegating to
certain particles of the blood the mission to eat up
these intruders, the warmth and moisture in the
back of the mouth and throat enable the germs to
develop with extraordinary rapidity. Each germ
divides into two in twenty minutes, so that, in the
course of a few hours, a single germ will have pro-
duced hundreds of thousands.
If the body is in a good state of health, the germs
are destroyed very soon after being inhaled, so that
they produce no ill-effects.
When, however, the system is below par or
weakened by any cause, the germs easily overcome
the defending forces in the blood, and we get the
ten minutes they were all killed. In spite of
this, Formamint is not offered as a cure for
diphtheria, although it will undoubtedly prevent
that dread complaint and other serious germ-
diseases.
The Prevention of Diphtheria.
This fact is vouched for by many sanitary
inspectors. The sanitary inspector of High
Wycombe, England, writes : “ I know of no other
preparation so effectual in preventing infectious
disease as Wulfing’s Formamint. During an otit-
break of diphtheria I have frequently given away
Formamint to those who have been in contact with
the disease, and no other cases have been removed
to hospital from the same household. Whenever
our ambulance is ordered out for the removal of an
infectious case, Formamint always accompanies
us.”
Many articles have appeared in the medical
papers pointing out Formamint’s supreme excel-
lence in the cure of sore throat. In The Prac-
titioner the Chief Medical Officer of one of the
largest Infectious Diseases’ Hospitals in the world
writes: “ I have never had sore throat myself since
I began to use Wulfing’s Formamint, although I
suffered periodically before.”
A writer in the Lancet states : “ I have had the
best results from the use of Wulfing’s Formamint
Tablets.”
It should be noted, however, that only Wulfing’s
Formamint produces such results. None of the
many imitations now on the market will do so.
How valueless they are is shown by a physician,
who writes that he “ has tried six substitutes, and
has not found one of them efficacious.”
Remarkable Concensus of Opinion.
The social testimony to Formamint’s value is as
remarkable as the medical. No preparation, with
the single exception of Sanatogen, which is made
by the same firm, has received such distinguished
testimonials.
The Right Hon. Viscount MaSsereene and Ferrard
writes: “ I have found Wulfing’s Formamint most
effective for the cure of sore throat, which quickly
disappears after using a few tablets.”
The Right Hon. Lord Glantawe writes: “ I have
been using Wulfing’s Formamint for sore throa
with most satisfactory results.”
The Right Hon. Lord Kingsale writes: “land
my family use quite a of Wulhng
Formamint Tablets, and find them all you say t ey
are. I always carry a goodly number in my poc ,
and have recommended them to many friends.
As to their value in influenza, Sir George Alex
ander writes: “Formamint Tablets have been y
constant companions during the winter, and a
found them a dangerous enemy to the intiue
fiend.”

It is in this way that sore throat,
influenza, and bad colds arise.
Obviously the only means of cure
and prevention is to destroy the
germs in the mouth and throat.
Of all the methods hitherto de-
vised by Science for producing this
result, nothing can compare with
Wulfing’s Formamint. This is
unanimously admitted by the medical
profession.
Many thousands of doctors, in-
cluding several to crowned heads of
Europe, have written to the pro-
prietors stating that they invariably
use Wulfing’s Formamint for treat-
ing sore throat. The reason is that
nothing destroys the germs so rapidly
and completely.
This was first proved by a famous
scientist, who removed some virulent
germs of diphtheria from the throat
of a patient seriously ill with that
disease, and mixed them with a
solution of Formamint. Within

Fig. 1 shows the germs allowed to grow freely on
a culture medium comparable to the favourable
breeding ground of our throats. Fig. 2 shows the
remarkable decrease in the number of germs when
the breeding ground had been treated with the
saliva of a person who had sucked three Formamint
Tablets. Fig. 3 shows the entire destruction of the
germs when the breeding ground had been treated
with the saliva of a person who had sucked five
Formamint Tablets five minutes previously. Thus
the sucking of Wulfing’s Formamint prevents germ*
diseases by destroying the germs which cause them.

The Dago has been taking his limelit turn.
Coming from out of his litter of oyst and
lob in a Sydney suburb, he plastered his
window with wild print-illustrations of the
Greek’s victorious arguments with the Turk,
and supplemented them by written descrip-
tions. For instance: —
This picture represent the vigorous battle of Ger-
vantoporo, to which as we see is a difficult and very
strong fortifications. But the victorious Greacian
army didn’t take them very long to destroy the Tur-
kish batteries. On account the deadly fire of the
Greecian Artilleries they soon seize the Turkish forts
and then the Crown Prinse of Greece order the
general attack from the infantary and horseman.
Then with the enthusiastic cheer the whole Greacian
army rushed like lions. Then the town had to sur-
rend.
“Jeames” writes: —
According to the London papers the Marquis of
Headfort has become a Catholic. This would,
doubtless, be the doing of the Marchioness who was
Kosie Boote, one of the several Gaiety Girls (Zena
Dare and her sister were others) who got their
schooling in a convent. A dozen years ago London
forecasted a rapid and alcoholic career for young
Headfort. He had just entered the “Blues,” and
was going the pace at a brisk canter which promised
shortly to work up into a gallop. Then he met and
fell in love with Rosie, who, if not the loveliest, was
quite the straightest and strongest-minded of her
kind. They married, and the event brought him up
with a round turn. From that day to this he
has been a model nobleman. The young Marchioness
sailed into the society of her husband’s friends and
relations without a struggle. For one thing, she did
not embarrass them by calling for small ports in the
morning hours; assaulting the servants; painting
her face the unfashionable color; and in general be-
having like the stage hussy of a Hall Caine vision.
Like nearly all the musical comedy maidens who
have burst into the peerage of late years, she offended
rather on the side of ultra-gentility. (The genteel
person, male or female, is unpopular among the
best people in Britain to-day.) Her uncompromising
Catholicism, of course, exasperated the Headfort
family, which has always been strongly Protestant.
The baptising of the children in the Pope’s faith ex-
asperated them still more. A good reason for this
is to be found in the fact that suitable Catholic
partis are almost as rare in England to-day as they
were when Dizzy deplored their dearth half a cen-
tury ago.
It is astonishing how well these Burke-and-Stage
unions turn out. I can’t recall half a dozen un-
happy ones in the past decade. I ascribe it to the
fact that the musical comedy wife enters on the con-
tract determined to play the game—knowing well
that however dull the game may turn out, it is
bound to be pleasanter than that which she has been
at on the boards. A big proportion of society girls
marry with a different point of view. They are
gaining nothing in the way of comfort or a secured
future. So they claim —openly or otherwise —a free
leg as one of the terms of the matrimonial contract.
And that leads, almost always, to trouble. The suc-
cessful wife is she who first attaches her husband’s
affections to her with the most powerful chains in
her ironmongery store; and then watches him like
an eagle or vulture.
Manuscript found in a bottle: —
Providence is always overdoing it. Perhaps it is
because it is such a large concern that it ladles out
such absurdly large helpings of rain and wind. Man
seems to spend half his time with his collar off
sprinkling the garden with a hose; during the other
half, his hat has to be tied on with string while he
digs a channel for the stormwaters to rush off his
sodden premises. The Ma State had wanted, rain
before May (or was it April?) arrived, garbed in
slate-colored clouds and with a double rainbow in her
hair. Then something started prodding Providence,
and Providence woke up with a start and pulled
over the whole celestial sideboard. After that, all
days were the same slate color, so that you could
hardlv tell 12 noon from 6 p.m., and the heavens
were filled with wind and rain, and the man who
got separated from his umbrella was a lost and
sodden soul. The morning paper that arrived on the
doorstep like the remnant of a burst pudding called
the misery “a disturbance,” and pretended to keep
some account of the falling wet in hundreds of
“points.” Sometimes it mentioned casually that the
humidity on the previous day had been “100 p.c.” ;
and the baffled reader with the cold in his head
wondered dully if that meant he was breathing
water, and, if so, whether he was entitled to regard
himself as a fresh-water fish. Presently the official
caster of horoscopes, or someone of the kind, re-
marked in a grave official voice that the wretched-
ness was “passing away” ; and immediately the voice
of the prophet was drowned in a white roar of rain,
and people had to bail out their goloshes before they
could put them on again. About that time, the
slamming of household artillery reverberated in the
morning pudding on the doorstep. The lowlying
parts of the State were flooded, and farming folk
were sitting on ice-cold iron roofs burning gun-
powder as a means of hailing the rescue boats that
were going to and fro amongst the trees. One family
put to sea, so to speak, on tables, while the house
cow looked on from an island that had once been
a hill. The mail trains were pulled up on the edge
of things, until such time as the rails should re-
appear, and one hamlet reported briefly over a wet
wire tenanted by drenched birds, and strung on poles
covered w th refugee ants and minor jim-jams, that
it had had two floods within seven days. Then there
was a long silence, and, because there was nothing
else doing, another black storm blew along and wept
superfluously over the remains, like the forty-third
widow of a dead bigamist. P.S. —No time to write
any more. I have provis'oned my raft —made out of
two barrels and the counter —and intend to try and
row myself ashore with the cribbage board. This
letter goes in the last empty bottle. —Yours, Bill
Spreeover.
“H.A.L.”: Looking at things generally, the
time seems to me ripe for the appointment
of a Public Curser. There are a lot of
people, animals and politicians, as well as
wet days, “dead marines,” Freetraders, bald
poets, and cold soup that require ground and
lofty execration, but the art of execrating
has been neglected? Where, for instance, do
we hear anything so comprehensive as befell
one Spinoza, bad- in 1656: —
By the sentence of the Angels, by the decree of
the Saints, we anathemalise, cut off, curse and exe-
crate Baruch Spinoza in the presence of these sacred
books with the six hundred and thirteen precepts
which are written therein, with the Anathema where-
with Joshua anathematised Jericho; with the curs-
ing wherewith Elisha cursed the children, and with
all the cursings which are written in the Book of
the Law: cursed when he lieth down, and cursed
when he riseth up, cursed when he goeth out and
cursed when he cometh in, the Lord pardon him
never; the wrath and fury of the Lord burn upon
this man and bring upon him all the curses which
are written in the Book of the Law. The Lord blot
out his name under Heaven. The Lord set him apart
for destruction from all the tribes of Israel with all
the curses of the firmament which are written in the
Book of the Law.... Then shall no. man speak to
h : m, no man write to him, no man show him any
kindness, no man stay under the same roof with him,
no man come nigh him.
That is mild to some of the great masters’
efforts ; still, it is very fair and worthy of en-
couragement in these dull days. I move that
a Chair for Cursing be established at the
University.
Law-suit squashing is becoming quite the
thing in the S. Aus. Supreme Court. Lately
it was Judge Murray who cut in at the start
of a case between the Agricultural Society
and one of its best supporters, and told them
to settle it in a give-and-take wav, like
good fellows. Now it is Judge Gordon on
that bitterest of all things, a church dispute.
It was a story straight out of Crockett, only
Lutheran, instead of “Wee Free. The
parties were all Germans from up-country,
llage claimed to have a resolution expelling
him from Church membership set aside.
Pastor Hessfeldt said llage had been thrown
out for saying evil things about Reimann;
and llage replied that he hadn t said half
the things alleged, and it was a long while
ago, anyway. Vv hen they had got so far,
the Judge told them they would probably
all go broke before the matter was threshed
out, and he practically insisted on a confer-
ence, to which he himself lent an ear. Re-
sult, Reimann and llage to shake hands,
llage to go back into the Church, nobody to
refer to the matter in future, and each side
to pay its own costs. Next day it was a
promising breach-of-promise that was
squashed. No love-letters read, no (“Roars
of laughter”), only a verdict for a reason-
able sum by agreement. What the lawyers
think of all this is not known. What they
say is that they are delighted, and that his
Honor has but anticipated their dearest wish.
“Subbubs”: Without a doubt Private En-
terprise does things better —for itself. Take
the Manly Ferry Service. During the gale,
on Thursday last, it cut down its day ser-
vice to one boat per hour, and after 7
p.m. it cut off the dashed thing altogether.
Why? To ensure the safety of the public?
Not a bit of it. If a ferry boat can cross
the Heads at 1 p.m. and 2 p.m. it can
likewise perform the feat at 1.30 p.m. And if
a co. can safely run boats when the gale is
at its height at 8.45 and 9.15 a.m. it can
obviously do the same at 7 p.m. and
thereafter when the wind has started to die
down. The explanation of the hourly ser-
vice, and of the total stoppage after 7
p.m. is purely a financial one. There is no
money to speak of in a day service when the
weather is as wet and cold as a drowned
whale; and none whatever in a night service
in such conditions. But the ferry co.
has no more equitable right to take such
matters into consideration than, say. an hotel
has to close its doors on such days as it
reckons that there may be little doiug in the
drinking line. For years past, whenever
the weather has been so unpleasant that no
one would be likely to use the Manly
steamers save residents —which is to say.
season ticket-holders —the company has seized
on the first pretext to shut down on its ser-
vice. What is wrong, in the first place, with
the co. being forced to provide boats which
shall be safe between the Heads in any
weather? Failing that, it seems to me, anti-
Socialist though I am, in a political sense,
that it is up to the Government to take oyer
this service and so run it that a big section
of law-abiding citizens will not be trans-
formed from time to time into users of ob-
scene and furious language, and searchers
after comfortless pub bedrooms, and hurried
buyers of tooth-brushes and chill new night-
wear.

some barmaids and a motor-ear the
would be even madder than he is already. 01 ’

CHARLADY WINTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For close on a quarter of a mile along the main
promenade on Manly beach the sea had dashed
against the wall with such violence that the great
stretch of sand was gutted out to a depth of 10ft
....The wind carried several people off their feet
and flung them against the rails of the bridge
The penetrating force of the showers so water-logged
some of the roofs of the tramcars that the electric-
lighting installation of the cars was affected
Reports of recent gale.
With her broom of wind and her bucket of rain
Comes old Mrs. Winter to clean up again.
She tumbles the waters and sluices the sands,
And sweeps Summer’s rubbish with busy, wet hands.
The chocolate wrapper thrown down by the flapper,
The weather-stained paper, the cigarette butt,
The waste of the hamper—she makes it all scamper
Along with the rubbish of Wowser and “nut.”
The cliffs and the beaches —the long harbor reaches,
Wherever a littering human has been,
She splashes and dashes. For next Summer’s
mashes
The bays will put on a. new mantle of green.
For she scrubs and she rubs in a lunatic way,
And gushes, and rushes, and flushes all day;
While you hear her at night
Working on without light,
Without gossip or tea,
Or a word re her fee.
Then she never begs clothes,
Or looks in with a nose
That is red to report that she’ll “soon ’ave ter
stop—
That unless someone ’ere kin run out fer a drop
O’ dry gin, ’cause ’er ’art is that bad! —yes, thank
you
As it’s ’ere in the ’ouse, bottled lager will do!”
Yes, she’s rather untidy—she sighs and she sobs;
But we wish that Ma Winter —hardworking Ma
Winter
Would take on our everyday, cleaning-up jobs!
N.S.W.

What the grey, languishing, dropping hairs

ROWLANDS MACASSAR OIL gives an

taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

A BRAVE SPY.
Captured was a spy during
The scene of War,
And sentenced to death
By court-martial Law.
Don’t veil my eyes, he
Did fiercely cry,
Pull off the rag, I’m
Not afraid to die.
With folded arms he stood
To meet his Doom.
The drums rolled and a
Rifle volley did boom.
The execution was over,
The fatal wretch lay dead.
And was roughly consigned
To his burial bed.

A COUNTRY SCENE.
The whitening stumps are standing around
The green brown grass is long and lush
The stock contented upon the ground
A peaceful scene is o’er the bush
Gum trees in plots are here and there
To give the shade the stock's require
With a foal at feet a creamy mare
Is feeding about down on a mire
The bush is alive with caroling birds
That shine like silk in the sunshine rays
Their songs are sweeter by far than chords
Sent from a harp that a minstrel plays
A range of hills with deep, shaded guil es
Close to a homestead surrounded with vine
Where ferns are waving in winds that sullies (?)
This is a picture that most divine.

There is an odd “improvement scheme
on foot in Adelaide. Hindley-street used to
be the principal thoroughfare—shops at one
end and residences at the other. Fifty years
ago, however, trade moved across King Wil-
liam-street into Rundle-street, and the Hind-
ley-street houses went to the Afghan and the
Chow and the kind of white woman who is
likely to marry her discolored relative Shem.
Never was there a slump more complete.
The top 50yds. or so, nearest King William-
street, were saved by the Theatre Royal and
a fashionable milliner’s, but beyond that
point a rigid invisible line was drawn. Lat-
terly, the Coffee Palace and West’s Pictures
have enticed people another 100yds., and now
the big local shopkeepers are clamoring for
brighter lighting, and, in general, the re-
moval of all reproach from the name. But
it will be hard work. Fashion the tone
in these matters. Also, the nanle has too
often been heard in the police court. As for
Light Square, the handsome enclosure half-
way along the street, to say of any woman
that she frequented it would probably lead
to a libel action. Colonel Lifeht, the de-
signer of the city, was splendidly interred
in the centre of the square when he died,
but half a century later, whe,n ,the time
came for a statue, it had to be put far away
in Victoria Square to give it a fair chance.
Many people think that the uoper-half of
the street —the whole thoroughfare is two-
thirds of a mile long—should be given an-
other start under a new name.
The efficiency of the Postal Department in
Melbourne was flatteringly acknowledged,
the other week, by an amazing citizen named
Samuel Slade. He wrote to the P.M.G.
stating that a letter had come to him from
England addressed merely, “Mr. Samuel
Slade, Melbourne, Victoria, Australia.”
There had been very little delay in the de-
livery of the letter, and the recipient reck-
oned that great care and sagacity had been
shown by the post-office employees who
handled his correspondence. As a matter
of fact, there are only two Sam Slades in
the alphabetical section of the Melbourne
Directory, and this particular Sam is one
of them. He stood an even money chance
of getting his letter on the day of its arrival.
And if he hadn’t got it by the next day, at
latest, he might have reasonably proclaimed
the inefficiency of the Postal Department
from his own house top.
[lnsinuations that this is political are repudiated
by Enwryp Fisiifr. The Bulletin Office, 369 Col-
lins-stvpet, Melbourne.]

Cobra, Boot PolisK
Chunder Loo,
Of Akim Foo,
Shines upon
The ocean blue.
When his flag streams
In the breeze
Foes are polished
Off the seas.
From his course
Fie never swerves,
Peace and leather
He preserves,
And the people
Crowd the quay
When the COBRA
Puts to sea.

PT ACPSannr.iTit.ri the Referendum (Constitution Alteration) Act 1906-12 provides that the POLLING
merit arv “ the , aws of th e Commonwealth for the time being in force relating to Parlia-
mentary Elections shall be polling places for the purposes of the Referendum.
THE PROPOSED LAWS ARE AS FOLLOW:
1. TRADE AND COMMERCE.
A PROPOSED LAW.
To Alter Paragraph (1) of Section Fifty-one of the Constitution,
of rA, 1 ! ' e . n^? ted by the King’s Most Excellent Majesty, the Senate, and the House
of Representatives of the Commonwealth of Australia, with the approval of the elec-
tors, as required by the Constitution, as follows:
COMMERCE) A 19lT 7 CONSTITUTION ALTERATION (TRADE AND
2. Section fifty-one of the Constitution is altered:—
(a,) by omitting from paragraph (i.) the words “with other countries and
among the States” ; and
(b) by adding at the end of paragraph (i.) the words “but not including
trade and commerce upon railways the property of a State, except so
far as it is trade and commerce with other countries or among the
States.’
Preamble.
Short title.
Trade and
commerce.
2. CORPORATIONS.
A PROPOSED LAW.
To alter Paragraph (xx.) of Section Fifty-one of the Constitution,
enacted by the Kings Most Excellent Majesty, the Senate, and the House
of Representatives of the Commonwealth of Australia, with the approval of the elec-
tors, as required by the Constitution, as follows:
™™Jw This Act may be cited as CONSTITUTION ALTERATION (CORPORA-
TIONS) 1912.
2. Section fifty-one,of the Constitution is altered by omitting from paragraph
(xx.) the wo.rds “Foreign corporations, and trading or financial corporations formed
within the limits of the Commonwealth,” and inserting in their stead the words: —
“Corporations including:—
(а) the creation, dissolution, regulation and control of corporations;
(б) corporations formed under the law of a State, includ ng their dissolu-
tion, regulation and control; but not including municipal or govern-
mental corporations, or any corporation formed solely for religious,
charitable, soientific, or artistic purposes, and not for the acquisition
of gain by the corporation or its members ; and
(c) foreign corporations, including their regulation and control.”
Preamble.
Short title.
Corporations.
3. INDUSTRIAL MATTERS.
A PROPOSED LAW.
To Alter Paragraph (xxxv.) of Section Fifty-one of the Constitution,
Be it enacted by the King’s Most Excellent Majesty, the Senate, and the House
of Representatives of the. Commonwealth of Australia, with the approval of the elec-
tors, as required by the Constitution, as follows: —
1. This Act may be cited as CONSTITUTION ALTERATION (INDUSTRIAL
MATTERS) 1912.
2. Seetion fifty-one of the Constitution is altered by omitting from paragraph
(xxxv.) the words “Conciliation and arbitration for the prevention and settlement
of industrial disputes extending beyond the limits of any one State,” and inserting
in their stead the words:—
“Labor, and employment, and unemployment, including:—
(a) the terms and conditions of labor and employment in any trade, in-
dustry, occupation, or calling ;
(b) the rights and obligations of employers and employees;
(cl strikes and lockouts;
(a) the maintenance of industrial peace; and
(e) the settlement of industrial disputes.”
Preamble.
Short title.
Industrial
Matters.
4. RAILWAY DISPUTES.
A PROPOSED LAW.
To Alter the Constitution bv empowering the Parliament to make Laws
with respect to Industrial Disputes in relation to Employment in State Rail-
way Services.
Be it enacted by the King’s Most Excellent Majesty, the Senate, and the House
of Representatives of the Commonwealth of Australia, with the approval of the elec-
tors, as required bv the Constitution, as follows:
1. This Act may be cited as CONSTITUTION ALTERATION (RAILWAY DIS-
PUTES) 1912.
2. Section fifty-one of the Constitution is altered by inserting after paragraph
(xxxv.) the following paragraph:—
“(xxxv,A.) Conciliation and arbitration for the prevention and settlement
of industrial disputes in relation to employment in the railway ser-
vice of a State.”
Preamble.
Short title.
Railway
DispuUs.
5. TRUSTS.
A PROPOSED LAW.
To Alter the Constitution by empowering the Parliament to make Laws
with respect to Trusts, Combinations and Monopolies
Be it enacted by the King’s Most Excellent Majesty, the Senate, and the House
of Representatives of the Commonwealth of Australia, with the approval of the elec-
tors, as required by the Constitution, as follows: —
1. This Act may be cited as CONSTITUTION ALTERATION (TRUSTS) 1912.
2; Section fifty-one of the Constitution is altered by adding at the end thereof
the following paragraph:—
“(xl.) Trusts, combinations, and monopolies in relation to the production,
manufacture, or supply of goods, or the supply of services.”
Preamble.
Short title.
Trusts, combinations,
and monopolies.
6. NATIONALISATION OF MONOPOLIES.
A PROPOSED LAW.
To Alter the Constitution by empowering the Parliament to make Laws
with respect to Industries and Businesses declared to be subject of a monopoly.
Be it enacted by the King’s Most Excellent Majesty, the Senate, and the House
of Representatives of the Commonwealth of Australia, with the approval of the elec-
tors. as required by the Constitution, as follows: —
1. This Act may be cited as CONSTITUTION ALTERATION (NATIONALISA- Short title.
TION OF MONOPOLIES) 1912. . „ , iU
2. The Constitution is altered by inserting, after section fifty-one thereof, the
following section: — , ,
“51A. —(1) When each House of the Parliament, in the same session, has by
resolution, passed by an absolute majority of its members, declared that
the industry or business of producing, mkimfactur.ng or supplying any
specified services, is the subject of a monopoly, the Parliament shall have
power to make laws for carrying on the industry or business by or
under the control of the Commonwealth, and acquiring for that pur-
pose on just terms any property used in connection with the industry
or business. ,
2. This section shall not apply to any industry or business conducted or
carried on by the Government of a State or any public authority con-
stituted under a State.”
Preamble.
Nationalisation
of monopolies.
Dated the 25th day of April, 1913.
JOHN GILBERT McLAREN,
Commonwealth Electoral Officer for the State of New South Wales.
Commonwealth Electoral Office for the State of New South Wales, Sydney.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
.just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain oft' them —the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot l |,ir t the ear.
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the h e a r i ng-
nerve corm
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
of you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Brums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been ,
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may wear
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
Even a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they are only
sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover, and also an
inserter. After the first pair is bought you may pur-
chase a single drum at any time for Bs., but the
pair you get at first will last quite two years. Do
not ask for a trial pair. We do not send them out
on trial, and so they are never secondhand. Use the
Order form below, and the Outfit will be sent you
at once, with the fullest instructions. Don’t delay,
but have the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.

The Smothered Press.
Consider the attitude of some of the great
dailies on the “Press Gag” Act since the
ominous day when the Federal electoral
writs were issued. Grandma Herald, after
announcing petulantly that she couldn’t
abear it and it was like somebody’s brazen
impudence, ignored all Federal issues in her
leading columns, but turned on the leaders
of the Ma State Opposition to give Sheol to
the Federation. Ever since then (as the song
says) the kind old lady has published long,
medium and short reports of all kinds and
descriptions of speeches—Liberal, Labor, In-
dependent, Freetection and nondescript. But
Granny has boldly, badly and indeed coura-
geously declined, refused and purposely
omitted to sign one of them. The
dear old lady now owes the Law
about £1,492,731 11s. 2d. in fines for high-
spirited capers. She has made amends by
publishing articles on the Governor-General,
the Vacant North, the Cricket Season, Com-
missions, Drink, the Third, Fourth and other
Parties, the Suffragettes and the Cadets.
Also, the Balkans, the Deficit and the D
(this last one under another title). At
latest date she had tackled Reciprocity, Re-
ligion and Strikes and the Law ; also, some
more Balkans. Aunt Matildagraph has
kept on her “dig” since the deadly 23rd
April. She never said nuffin to no one, but
opened a new packet of hatpins on April 24,
and started “Campaign Points,” by L.
Buchanan, of King-street, Sydney—and
very gentlemanly, not to say gentle, digs,
they are! Aunt ’Tilda, without warning,
then entered upon a long course of articles
on Abolishing Government by K.C., the
Balkans, Printed Poison, Man or Beast,
Captain Cook, the Cost of Living, Problems
of British Suffragettes, the Balkans, Ameri-
can Views of Treaty Obligations, Pacific
Problems, Apprentices, White Australia, the
Balkans. Scaddan’s pastoral leases, Tram
Fares, the Subbubs, Tin, Ferries, Farmers,
Hospitals, the Pres§ and the Public, the Bal-
kans, the World’s Youth, China, Lord Den-
man and (of course) the Balkans. At time of
writing Auntie is hitting up a circulation on
little things like Britain, Defences and the
Racial Qualificatiops for Citizenship, while
three of her best (R. L. Nash, Thomas
Courtney and H. J. Taperell) are getting
in “side” articles on the subjects they should
be allowed to handle in the leading columns.
Also, ’Ector Carruthers and several others
are being allowed to speak a niece at inter-
vals on matters Federal. In Melbourne the
Argus, with impressive dignity, is signing
its leaders thus :
Written, after consultation, to express the views
of the Argus, by David Hewitt Maling, 197 Collins-
street.
The Age just dabs on three or four names
to each article, such as :
G. F. H. Schuler, 233 Collins-street, Melbourne.
Benj. Hoare, 233 Collins-street. Melbourne.
Ambrose Pratt, 233 Collins-street, Melbourne.
Now, why can’t Sydney’s daily mentors go
and do likewise? As an earnest Liberal
with some good old Tory views, and as a con-
sistent Baptist Unitarian, this writer main-
tains that the S. M. Herald and Tele-
graph are not sincere in their alleged oppo-
sition to Labor, or to the “Press Gag” Act.
If they were they wouldn’t publish a line of
Fisher, Hughes and Co.’s speeches and
manifestoes. They would shut down abso-
lutely on Fisher and all the members who
helped pass that Act. They would be con-
sistent, and by utterly suppressing all Labor
reports and documents, show N. S. Wales
that there is only one cause —their cause—
and only one side to the present political
scrap. Then Labor, at vast expense and
effort, would have to start a large and im-
posing journal of its own —and it couldn’t
do it in time to win this election.
[Written, after profound consultation with him-
self, by Edward MacCulloch, Megalong-street, Ka-
toomba (N.S.W.), masquerading more or less effect-
ively for literary purposes as O. C. Cabot, which is
the word “tobacco” reversed, the “0.C.” being sup-
posed to stand for “Oliver Cromwell/’ who has
the misfortune to be dead.]
: «.
During his sojourn in Melbourne with the
Dominions Trade Commission, Rider Hag-
gard, repenting of his lugubrious speech to
the Sydney Institute of Journalists, waxed
gay, and talked Zulu in the street. He
escaped arrest for “langwidge.” When the
Commission adjourned on May 2, Haggard
was asked by a fellow Commissioner on the
way lunchwards, “Are you coming?” In
reply whereto the biographer of Umslopogaas
delivered himself: “Yebo, Inkose, ni siham-
ba.” The only Melbourne paper able to
make anything of that speech was the Her-
ald, which apparently possesses a Zulu re-
porter. Dipping his assegai in the inkwell,
he translated the remark, “Yes, sir, I am
coming.” The phrase is from Bantu, the
language spoken by most of the Kaffir tribes.
But “Yebo” is tantamount to “My oath!”
instead of the mere “Yes” of affirmation. A
debate, between Haggard in Bantu and Sin-
clair in Maori would be worth reporting,
though it is doubtful whether the Pitman
system was devised to cope with the Zulu
click, which makes that language sound like
a hurricane of kisses.
Horrified at the idea that there should
breathe a man who refuses to bow down
and worship the Party Government fetich,
the Argus devotes columns to assuring the
electors of Gippsland that they (the said
electors) are dead, dog-tired of that good
democrat and brainy citizen, G. H. Wise.
Wise, a genuine Liberal, who, when the
Fusion was perpetrated, refused to be seen
alive or dead with the Cook crowd, sat in
the last Parliament as an independent demo-
crat, and is seeking re-election as such. The
Argus (6/5/T3) alleges:—
The straightforward, honest farmer of the Gipps-
land type wishes to have as his Federal member one
who feels called upon to take one side or other in
the party welfare of politics.
According to the absurd old Tory sheet, the
political wisdom and foolishness of all the
ages, from Noah Arkbottom to Bruce Smith,
have been absorbed by two parties, and all
people who stand outside the two are cranks
and nonentities. Which is equivalent to
saying that a man may be either a giant or
a midget, and retain a certain amount of
public respect, but should he be a person of
sft. Bin. or thereabouts he is a monstrosity,
a freak of nature and a grave error on the
part of the Almighty.
[Albert John Owen, 129 Grey-street, East Mel-
bourne.]
