BRISBANE.
BANANA LAND
The country is going to the dogs, capital
is clearing out, industrial unrest is a blight,
and the Pastoral Employers’ Association met
at Longreach (Q.) the other day to offer it-
self its own condolences. In fact, things
are specially bad so far as these squatters
are concerned, because the administration
of the State’s Water Conservation (mostly
artesian bore) Department is an additional
“grave menace” to the cow and jumbuck in-
dustry. _ And just to prove how much they
and their own crowd believed the gloomy
yarns, _ the secretary reported that “Glad-
stone is duplicating its [freezing and meat
packing] works. Lake’s Creek is being
duplicated as far as freezing and storage
capacity are concerned. Ross River is
duplicating, and I understand also that the
Burdekin and Bowen meat works are put-
ting in additional freezing machinery.”
None of these people expect to make a penny
out of the works. They know that the coun-
try is being ruined and that there soon
won’t be any cows to can. But they do
it because- —well, apparently because they
know they will be ruined, and they might
just as well be ruined that way as any
other.
A HEALTH FADDIST.
DOCTOR : “How about baths? Do you bathe
regularly? ”
PATIENT: “Ho , no. [?] too bloomin' cold this
weather. But I AVE been go in' in a lot
for whisky lately!"
Maryborough gave itself some champagne
and cheers the other day when that good
Australian concern, Walkers, Ltd., steamed
out of its shops the first locomotive made in
Queensland for another State. It is the
firstborn of 20 which Victoria has ordered,
and the rest of the family are to be handed
over at the rate of one per week. After
being allowed to gaze at the landscape of
the Mary River and wonder why the town
crept so far up the river before it camped,
the 95-totj'giant was duly pulled to pieces
and stowed .away in readiness for the boat
Which, Will 'take it aAvay south. Victoria
requires the animal to steam 2000 miles by
way of trial, and most of it could have been
done on the way if the bungle of different
gauges had not interfered.
On the Road
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The dust is on my hurrying feet, the dust is
in my eyes,
And far behind me in the vale that o’er the
rough hill lies
There is a garden soft with dew and bright
with butterflies.
There is a sweet, white maiden bed. a little
crucifix,
Beyond a. patch of weeded ground where
phlox and daisies mix,
And in the spring bean blossoms curl about
their rigid sticks.
The window swings wide through the day;
it looks toward the hills,
The mignonette all big with bees, the room
with incense fills.
Sometimes < a blundering moth lights on the
pillow’s virgin frills.
Oh ! If a man can judge of Hell or Heaven
with mortal eyes,
I sometimes think that each for me in an
old memory lies,
A homely garden, soft with dew and bright
with butterflies !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, May 37, 1913.
My Dear Tiiargomidah,—
Beatrice Grimshaw, novelist, passed
through this week on her way south. Bea-
trice writes well, but those who have lived
in the Islands regard her tales of those parts
as not irretrievably blighted with the curse
of accuracy. One thing the lady possesses
in abundance, and that is courage, She
even went down in a diver’s dress While in
Tonga—a shuddersome idea to most women,
who could not abide the creepy things that
D. Jones keeps on the ground floor of his
damp, dark emporium.
Mrs. Arthur Feez, daughter May and Miss
Green have gone to Townsville, which at
present is very lively. Rockhampton, too,
is drawing a big crowd for Carnival week,
and his Ex. has promised to open the Show.
Some engagements : CissieO’Hagan, sister
of Mrs. Tom McCawley, is to be married in
September to one Ramsay, of Melbourne.
The lady has a mass of friends, and she will
be entertained at numberless teas, etc.,
before then. Frank Ahern, of football
fame, is to wed Miss Millingen early in
June—lda Chambers is giving a party in her
honor next Wednesday. And Clive Overell,
youngest son of W. J. Overell. and Alice
Logan, are two others who have conspired
matrimonially.
The wonderful “Gaby glide” season has
begun. It was said that the assemblies were
not to be held this year, and several sighs of
relief had been breathed, but w T hen the good
people of the Prevention of Cruelty to Ani-
mals Soc. announced that they would hold a
big ball in August, and hoped that the
country would roll up to that in place of the
usual assembly, that nearly-dead institution
suddenly revived and announced that it
would be alive about that date.
This will be another year of private
hops, dignified with such names as “Othello,”
“Kismet,” “Blue Bird,” or some such incon-
spicuous decoration. The “Birds” took theif
exercise last night in St. Andrew’s Hall, and
to-night the “Buttercup” Club is modestly
revolving at the Cafe Majestic. The past
pupils of the Grammar Schools are to enjoy
a rhythmic leaping next week, and several
other whirls or sarabands are about to palpi-
tate in the middle distance.
Lvndhurst, the John Reid home at Clay-
field, was filled on Thursday night with a
cheery bunch of boys and girls. Daughter
Eileen had a birthday and a guest from
Sydney simultaneously, so a celebration of
some sort was to be expected. Mrs. Reid
and Miss Munro (the guest), and Ruby-
Earl (who is almost another daughter)
helped to distribute the fruit salad and
unemployed partners. Peach blossom de-
corations were a gay side line.
The new tennis courts at Emmanuel Col-
lege were opened yesterday. The rain
rather spoilt the proceedings, but no cause
of complaint could be found against Miss
Waraker, the matron, who had lent a careful
eye to the food department. By unanimous
vote she was made an hon, mem. of the
Ancient Order of the Floury Hand for her
success.
Mrs. George Webster and her popular, hus-
band gave a party on Tuesday night, in
honor of Miss Macdonald, who is to be mar-
ried shortly, Mrs, Duckett White and Mrs.
Charlie Mant, sisters of the bride-in-contem-
plation, are giving a big At Home for her
next week.
Josephine Lewis, who leaves for England
in a month or two, gave a concert in His
Majesty’s on Wednesday night. The house
was full—the most-attended concert within
the memory of the oldest concertina —and a
waggon-load of flowers rolled steadily up to
the stage. Lena Hammond, W. Crisp, and
a baritone named ; England worthily sup-
ported the programme, with violinist Herr
Diebels a dead heat at the post, or what-
ever the technical term is that means the
same thing. Miss Lewis promises remark-
ably well, and should her performance on
Wednesday night be anything to go by, this
little Queensland girl will before long make
another recruit in Australia’s musical front
ranks. If she lacks anything it is strength,
but a long voyage and a complete change
of conditions may mend that.
Mrs. A. B. Carvosso, one of our most popu-
lar young hostesses, entertained a jolly party-
in Cafe Rowe, on Thursday afternoon. A
musical competition was won by Betty Cock-
burn, of the golden voice. The hostess wore
black, and some exquisite white fox furs.
Our very own songstress, Mrs. Gilbert
Wilson, being ill and unable to sing at the
Liedertafel concert on Tuesdav night,
Leonard Francis filled her place with two
of his pupils, both of whom sang prettily
and frocked acceptably. Their names were
Miss Hilda Reid and Miss Cylla Roughsedge.
The Women Students’ Club of the Univer-
sity contemplates a spree of some kind, but
an aristocratic vagueness broods over the
whole affair. Which reminds me that one
A. St. Ledger is still writing about the
powers of the Australian Constitution to
that untameable supporter of the Democracy 7,
the Courier.
Mrs. Rumor is talking of an engagement
between a squatter’s widow and a Southern
man, but so far the most persistent society
sleuth has not been able to discover if things
are quite settled. In between whiles, she
wonders why another wedding has been post-
poned.
AUSTRALIA JANE.
[*No responsibility attaches to Australia Jane
for the reference to A. Ledger. That was interpo-
lated by John Barr, The Bulletin Office, Sydney,
lie being addicted to depressing subjects.]
One Oliver Cromwell Roberts, a Califor-
nian chemist, dropped in the other day &nd
told the Government he had an idea or two
about this nrickly pear business. Banana-
land was all ears immediately, and gave him
100,000 acres in the Dulacca district to
play with. If he can shave the prickly
whiskers off that land in ten and a half
years, he can have it. Roberts’s idea is to
cut tracks into, the thorns mass and then
send in a patent cart to fondly dispense
heavy gases which will duly hang around
the pear and strangle it to death, after
which it can be burned off. Easy, isn’t it?
AN OLD
GRIEVANCE.
THE BETTER HALF:
“ To-morrow is
the fourteenth
anniversary of our wedding."
THE VULGAR TRACTION : “Will, you needn’t
taunt me with it.”
What grim experiences had many of the
early arrivals in old Moreton Bay ! A few
days agQ, a Mr. and Mrs. Shears, of Mount
Cotton, celebrated their “golden wedding.”
The husband came out in 1849, in the ship
Emigrant. Black fever broke out on the
vessel, and out of 360 passengers only 60
were left alive when quarantine was lifted
in Moreton Bay.
Yalleroi, a township between Blackall and
Jericho (which, by the way, is in no way
related to Jerusalem in Maoriland), has just
had itself surveyed and its streets named.
As a change from the common variety of
name usually inspired by the mayor, his
missus and the pet goat, the streets (on the
plan) are called after the grasses of
Bananaland —Flinders and Mitchell and the
like. Western Bananaland has a streak of
originality in this respect. Longreach has
named its thoroughfares after native birds;
Barcaldine’s wheel-tracks are called after
trees; Blackall’s after flowers.
The old wrangle about Queensland’s
artesian water has been renewed. J. B.
Henderson, the State’s hydraulic engineer,
says the flow is easing off, and for the 73rd
time or so he has cried aloud against the
waste-of the valuable liquor. A .remark of
his to the Empire Trade Commission, that
the squatter wouldn’t control the flow be-
cause he didn’t want to suggest doubts and
suspicions about the value of his property,
has set the Pastoral Employers’ Associa-
tion grinding its teeth in anger; and the
yarn is told again that, if the flow from a
bore is stopped or regulated, the chances
are that the maddened water will make a
new channel for itself underground and
escape. As a matter of fact, this doesn’t
improve the outlook at all. It is dead cer-
tain that the pressure is easing; and if
the only way to stop that serious movement
is to do something which may be more dan-
gerous still, things are only middling.

“Kobbi : A deputation from
somewhere in Southern Banana
land took its hat the other £
into a Government office anil
begged somebody to drop into if
enough borrowed dollars to resume
certain lands for a park. The
money was forthcoming, along with
some praise for the council which
was doing its duty to the public
But the obliging Minister forgot
to mention that bis
Government was too
tired to see that towns
retained the parks
they had, and did not
give them away at
half-price sales. Rock-
hampton is a shining
example of what a
council ought not to
be. It happened this
way. The Railway
Department suddenly
decided that it was
unwise to draw a
crippled engine all the
way to Brisbane for
repairs,, and suggested
workshops at Rock-
hampton. Now, Com-
missioner Evans had
his rather cute eye on Fitzrov
Park, and forthwith he asked for
eight or ten acres of it. Finally he
asked for the lot. The local coun-
cil. with immense dignity, said
“No!” and turned its back. But
then some publicans, drapers and
what not presented a petition
whining that if the Commissioner
did not get his fingers into that particular
hit of earth, the shop would be built else-
where. They had land of their own, but it
didn’t occur to them to pass that over—it
was so much easier to hand over the public
park. The scared council thereupon slew
its dignity and went out and offered the sur-
face to the Government with commendable
humility. _ Yet, if anyone told Rockhamp-
ton that it was not progressing, and that it
would never need any more parks than it
has now, the thud of the reoly would dis-
turb the penguins at Mawson’s Polar camp.
If a Brisbane Opposition journal is to be
believed, Premier Denham was received at
Atherton, on the fertile tableland behind
Cairns, by an enthusiastic deputation of
10 Hindoos and one pressman!
Courteous remark of a disappointed M.L.A.
deputationist to Minister for Railways
Paget: “There’s something rotten in the
state of Denmark if that is the way the rail-
ways are administered.” The fury of a she-
bear robbed of her whelps is nothing to the
rage of a politician when the loan fund
peters out.
The water hyacinth makes ruinous pro-
gress northward. At a meeting of the
Waterford Shire Council one of the muni-
cipal dads remarked that a ferry would be
an impossibility at that place, and he added
that a few miles away, at Loganholme, it
was now out of the question to pull a boat
across the river, so choked was it with
hyacinth. This pest will yet cost Queens-
land almost as dearly as the prickly pear.

Argus :
Touching the Bulletin's reference to us (B.
l/5/’l3) and the recent Fassifern election. You

that any individual outside the recognised sacred
bounds of Liberalism’ or Laborism has a eommete
right to offer himself to the people as a candidate.
We growled because in Queensland there is no la"
to prevent a candidate —any political jackanapes
securing elect : on on a minority vote. The Govern-
ment, in effect, permits this sort of thing because
it has devised no effective electoral machinery to
thwart it. “

For the Country Horseman
The saddle here illustrated is expressly designed
for country use, for stock riding, etc., and is built
of the best materials throughout. If any of our
friends are requiring such a saddle they will find
this a genuine bargain.
It has a hog-skin seat, a plated bar-tree, extra
long flaps, with points to buckle below. An economi-
cal feature is the skirt, which extends all round
and thus adds years of wear to the saddle by pro-
tecting its interior from the weather. It has a
deep, “sweepy” seat, 4in. kneepads, nickel-plated
iron stirrups, and double buckle leather girth. The
price complete is £3/15/- Mention of Tub
Bulletin when ordering will ensure your having a
leather surcingle given in free, and the saddle will
be forwarded freight paid. It is to be obtained
from ,T. P. Talty, 388 Sussex Street, Sydney.

THE AUDITORIUM,
Now nearing completion, has been erected at a cost

proper in Australia.

The Auditorium is equipped with heating and cooling
apparatus, which cost over £3OOO, by which the
atmosphere in winter and summer will be kept to an,
average temperature of 65 degrees.
Ample provision has been made for spacious en-
trances, exits, crush rooms, etc., all of which
will be heated in winter.

GROW HAIR
A Simple, Safe and Reliable Way that Produces Surprising Results.
NEW preparation causes big stir among hair specialists.
The above dearly shows Results from a Short Course of my True Hair=Growing Compound.
10,000 FREE trial boxes.
Those who are troubled with falling hair, dandruff,
baldness, itching scalp, etc., know full well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-
ever, of such conditions. These troubles can be
quickly overcome by my True Hair Grower.
I am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
1 reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
1 had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here let me
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time —yet the s hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, I fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This lead him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later. I
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair.
Since then the hair on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world—l am
not interested in decrying others—but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest.

* * * *
An inventor of some note, E. H. W. Crossley, has
returned to Sydney, after an absence of 15 years.
During that brief period he has meandered over
500,000 miles of land and liquid with the “Nautilus”
wreck locater in one portmanteu, and a submersible
submarine for deep-sea salvage, research work and
pearling in the other. The wreck locater is, briefly,
a captive buoy which floats above the place where
D. Jones has hidden the evidence of his guilt. The
submarine vessel can submerge itself, with two opera-
tors inside its waistcoat, to a depth of 150 fathoms,
which is nearly as deep as some of the machinations
of the anti-Australian parishioners to defeat the
Referenda. The Australian Government has a reflec-
tive and baleful eye on this vessel just now, and this
fact has, no doubt, given birth to the rumor at the
Wimmen’s Political Clique that the low Laborahs
intended to put the pearl—ah—works under that
stupid Socialism.
“Toltec”: Are we Australians troglodytes? If not,
why do we so dearly love to go underground to eat
our food ? One can readily understand the Celts or
the Eskimo preferring, from mere instinct and by
heredity, to descend into the earth to tear their
tucker. But by what strange diversion of atavism
do Australians, children of the sun, living in the finest
average climate on the planet, patronise tea-shops
and caf6s underneath the footpath ? Six new dining
places have been opened in Melbourne’s Collins-street
during the past 12 months, and five of them are below
the surface. To me, an old boulevardier, the custom
is incomprehensible. Every one of those eating-
houses should have been put on the roof, up near the
sun and the fresh air, and not down near the sewer
and the gaspipe, amidst mephitic vapor and in the
unwholesome earth. The Australian has much to
learn about refinement in cooking and eating.
“Scared to Death”: It’s an ill wind that doesn’t
turn some commercial windmill. Went into the neigh-
boring ironmonger’s, the other evening, to get some
household artillery for the reception of the masked
burglar. The obliging young man in charge opened
the showcase and took out a pretty automatic pistol.
“This,” he said oroudly, “this is the sort of gun
used by” “Royalty ?” I suggested. “No, sir!
This gun” “Roosevelt, then?” “No, sir!
This gun was used” “Balkan war?” I per-
sisted. He angrily disembowelled the thing, and
strewed the curious parts on the counter for a loading
demonstration. “This pistol,” he remarked, in a cold,
level voice, “was used by the Houndsditch anarchists
and kept 1000 troops at bay. The price is 50 shil-
lings.” Then I took him fully into my confidence.
I said, “Look here, I am not as rich as a Houndsditch
anarchist. Have you got something else-—something
not quite so good—about twelve-and-six, say?” He
had ; and he sold it to me with the contempt I de-
served. I haven’t the slightest intention of using it,
#*ecause I’m certain it would explode in my face.
But 'I don’t grudge the twelve-and-six. It was worth
the money to hear the beautiful way he spoke about
those anarchists .who died to make a gunmaker’s ad-
vertisement.
* * * #
Wagga (N.S.W.) is sitting quiet and reminding it-
self that, anyhow, there are other fools. A while
back a philanthropist made a promise of £2OOO to
the local hospital—it had nursed him, he said, and he
was grateful. The committee soon got busy, and the
doctors were invited to discuss the plans of the new
wards which were to be built with the gift. But
just before the meeting, the sec. of the hospital got a
note from the Chief Sec.’s office in Sydney. It ap-
pears that the gentleman who was to supply the cash
had just come out of gaol after serving two years or
so for false pretences ; also he is known to the police
under 10 different names ; finally, his whereabouts at
the moment were a mystery. When the doctors
heard the letter read, they just looked at each other
and laughed, and then went home. Meanwhile the
local paper is putting the inevitable cadge nicely by
inviting Wagga to lessen the disappointment.
“Straight Wire”: Agents engaged in selling busi-
nesses on commission are not always the
sharps they are painted. Numbers of men
who are ostensibly established in a concern
are really trading in selling businesses. They
take up a standstill or declining business, put a little
life into it, and place it for sale in an agent’s hands.
The agent introduces prospective buyers, but the
owner doesn’t come to terms. In the meantime he
notes those prospective buyers, and subsequently he
advertises in the papers that the sale of his business
4s withdrawn from all agents. Then one or other of
the good marks is accidentally encountered, and a
sale is effected. Men who hav6 >sold half a dozen
businesses that way laugh at the simplicity of the
average agent. They even say that he is an ass in an
office.
The imported expert, Brewis, gets the job
oc Director of Australia’s new lighthouse
service at £BOO a year. On July 1 the Com-
monwealth will formally take over all the
State lights, glares, torches and illumina-
tions, and get a move on with long-needed
improvements. .In addition to a central
staff, Brewis will have four district staffs
with salaries aggregating over £lO,OOO per
annum, four steamers are to be supplied,
new lighthouses built, wireless communica-
tions arranged, fog signals started, and
other up-to-date safety schemes installed.
Australia is going to get on to its job at
last.
THE DEATHLESS AD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Each man’s a walking ad. to his good deeds
Until he dies.
And when he kicks the pail ■ someone proceeds
To advertise
Upon a whited stone his place and date
Of death and birth,
And then a little verse or two to state
What he was worth
Unto his sad relations now bereaved,
Who miss him so—
Poor souls, if what they write ’s to be believed
They’re sick with woe!
Although, as on the stone they advertise,
He’s gone above,
They tell a tale of chronic streaming eyes
And deathless love.
And if the corpse, while on the sunny earth,
Made any hay
And leaves some tin behind, his actual worth
’Twere hard to say.^
Unto his legatees, left here below,
Who, though they call,
Find nothing left to answer but his pho-
to on the wall.
Now, if I have a ton of cash or so
When 1 depart,
I’ll leave it to some hospital, although
’Twill break my heart
To let it go at all, for always tin
Did pass me by.
I cannot work, but p’raps some day I’ll win
A Tatt.’s, and I
Will be a corpse of many means one day
With wealth untold,
And when I know I can no longer stay
Among my gold,
I’ll play philanthropist to advertise
My virtues rare,
And won’t 1 swagger round in Paradise
If I get there.
N.S.W.
FREDA OLDMAN.

always use tyres of American size..
Tyres in millimetre dimensions do not accurately fit rims
in inch size. It is a makeshift to which one should resort only
in a case of dire necessity.

The Old, Old Story.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There was a king with a large jaw and a queen
with a plain face on the throne of England ; there was
a king with a large jaw and a queen with a fair face
on the throne of France. In both countries it was
as clear as crystal to the lords of the State preserves
of loaves and fishes that things in general were settled
for ever.— Charles Dickens, in “A Tale of Two
Cities.”
rn'JIS is the old, old story—story of
I Life and Fate :
-1- Of love surpassing a woman’s, and
fiercer than woman’s hate;
Of love for one’s home and country—fighting
with one intent:
Be it as small as a country, or as wide as a
continent.
Be it republic or kingdom—snow-waste or
sand-waste or sod—
Subject to gods or idols, or Christ or Allah
—or God !
Black men, and brown men and white men;
captains of realms brought low—
Slaves of the basest nations, and princes
with youth aglow.
Chiefs of the sword —or the boar-spear-—the
lance or the nullah or axe;
From the Saxon Alfred and Harold, to the
last of Tasmania’s Blacks!
Strong men and brave men, and weaklings
who fought on the Weaker Side,
Till their bones gleamed white to the Hea-
vens, and the country was “pacified.”
Cave or hut in the forest, or tent on the
desert, was home—
Bare breasts of brave “Barbarians” barring
the roads from Rome ;
Shafts of the hairy Norsemen, ringing on
plate and mail;
Spears from the shrouded desert, reddened
to tell the tale,
Till our hammers rang on their statues, as
we camped by temple and dome,
When we’d taught their shattered legions
that all roads lead to Rome !
Red eyes watch from the branches (what
ape-like men are these?)
Matted heads from their burrows in the
roots of the giant trees:
Shapes that are human glide from the depths
of the forest boles — 1
Oh ! the straightened scythes and sickles and
the plough-shares bound to poles !
Oh! the rush to certain slaughter, because
of a monarch’s crime,
Of the bands that bore no banner, and the
feet that kept no time!
Oh! the back-to-back resistance of the stub-
born “boor” and “clown”—
Oh! the wild song growing fainter, as the
volleys mowed them down !
Oh! the yell from Hell to Heaven, of a
peasant fighting yet,
And the gasp of a throttled horseman, and
the thrust of a bayonet!
Oh ! the blood-soaked coat of sheep-skin, and
the hair thrust back to see
The triumph of blind oppression and the
murder of Liberty !
And the folk of the peasant village flee to the
marsh and mire,
M here the wives of charcoal burners are
crouching afar from a fire.
And the old men and children huddle —while
the village flames, for a sign—
Where the daughters of slaughtered swine-
herds hide from the greater swine,
A-tremble at every hoof-beat. Nobles! the
time is near,
When your dainty ladies in terror shall hide
them with less to fear!
This is the old. old story (tear out the
bricks and flags)
Shrilled by the starving children, screeched
from the throats of hags :
Shrieked by the maniac mothers, roared by
the maddened men,
Down from the roofs of hunger, and up from
the sewered den !
Storm in the streets of Paris ! Fire in vil-
lage and town !
The blood of the “Vermin” is - up and the
blood of oppression is down !
Fire to the roofs of Moscow! wider and
fiercer and higher—
(Holland could fight with water, but Mos-
cow must fight with fire.)
We have different uses for fire. —Serfs ! ’tis
your country still!
Nobles of Russia ! (or Poland!) Tyrants—-
or what you will —
We are patriots still, O Invasion ! Where
is your boasted might?
With the Russian Wolf on your left —and
the Russian Bear on your right!
A' horseman waits by the ferry, where no
boat waits for a load —
Three lanterns hang from a belfry, and four
hoofs ring on the road !
A call to the sleeping homesteads —men who-
have made a vow —
“If they want war and must have it, let it
be here and now.”
Lightly they tread on the grasses, grasping
the fljntlock gun,
Shadowy figures of farmers in the woods of
Lexington!
(America boasts no longer; this is the turn-
ing tide —
We have outgrown our Freedom, we have
outgrown our pride ;
We have outgrown our honor, we have out-
grown our truth —
We of the younger nations — We have out-
grown our youth!
Flintlock and truth and honor, “pepper-
box,” pistol and pike,
Gave way to a race of liars, made way for
the insane strike.)
Dawn on the slopes of Eureka ! misty and
cold and grey.
Clay-stained sons of the Nations (and their
leader said “Let us pray.”)
Dawn on the slopes of Eureka —and Day in
a Commonwealth?
Still as the years go over, tyranny creeps
_ by stealth.
It is not day, my brothers, all is not well
and right;
The volley from far Eureka is echoing here
to-night!
Allah be praised ! They’d wake us ! Wake
us if that must be!
Spear from the rock waste and desert and
asres of mystery.
The Lion, the Bear and the Jackal, in the
goat’s blood their thirst would slake.
Tripoli, Turkey and Persia, say, are these
all you’ll wake??
Beware of the East, O Christian, for the
. sak e of your fairest and best;
It is written, and, written, remembered that
the tide of Invasion goes West.
You builded a wall, O China ! to keep your
enemies out;
You cradled the mightiest river and you con-
quered the flood and the drought.
1 atient and peaceful and honest —children
of Industry—
Wise with the wisdom of ages—yet they
could not let you be!
Aor wall nor mountain nor ocean justice or
peace could win.
You builded a wall, O China! Let them see
that it keep you in.
England ! Preserved from invasion through
the stormyYatter years—
Blind to the crimes of nations, and blind to
their victims’ tears!
Leave to the little people the barren and use-
less mile,
Turn to your own and save them, saving
yourself the while!
Starved in the streets of London, children
of misery—
England! do you not hear them? The
Drums of Battersea !
Giving our youth and our old age, staking
our best and our all —
Fighting in jungle and desert, firing from
r window and wall;
Whether we plan in a cellar, whether we
plot m a den,
Or write while we starve in a garret, we are
the leaders of men.
Hunted and murdered wherever the foot of
Oppression has trod.
For the sake of Humanity aid us! We are
thy rebels, Oh God!
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
Art and Snails.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“~V/¥T lIEKE are you going to now?”
f f demanded the stout lady on the
verandah, as her husband ap-
peared with a lighted lantern.
He paused at the top of the steps that led
down to the sleeping garden, and peered at
her patiently over his spectacles. “I was
just going to get some snails, my dear.”
She fanned angrily, and sniffed. ‘‘You're
always looking for the filthy things ! I be-
lieve you would rather go and look for snails
than sit and talk to me for five minutes. As
for asking me to sing to you !” She
broke off, and laughed in a short, unpleasant
way that was worse than a malediction.
. 1 “ e snail-hunter, recognising that he was
centre of a domestic typhoon, hesi-
tated in helpless anger.
The fan went faster than ever. “Go on
t°. your snails!” sneered the shadowy
wielder. It isn’t necessary to be sociable.
1 don t mind being snubbed and slighted !”
“Oh, don’t talk nonsense!” chattered her
husband impulsively. “/ ’m not slighting
you ! Why can’t you sing and let me listen
from the garden?”
‘‘But you never asked me!”
“Well, I’m asking you now!”
“Of course” —with deadly sarcasm—“of
course you don’t want me to sing!”
The snail-hunter drew a deep breath. “Oh,
yes I do !”
“Then why don’t you stay here instead of
rushing away into the garden the minute
you ve swallowed your tea? Ho you know
that I have not been asked to approach the
piano since Mr. Storpor went away to Eng-
land?”
“Why can’t you go to the d—d piano
without being asked?”
“You might at least pay me the compli-
ment of asking me!”
The snail-hunter said “Bah!” under his
breath. Still it was sufficiently distinct.
“Anyone would think that I couldn't play
or sing a note the way you go on! Not once
have you asked”
“But I’m asking you now, aren’t I?”
“Yes, but I have to ask you to ask me!”
The baffled hunter turned away, and,
armed with the lantern, stumbled furiously
into the maze of dark bushes.. “D —n it
all!” he muttered. “I wish she had that
singing bounder back again! Never could
stand him myself, but he was a useful brute,
after all!” Reaching a likely haunt, he
went down on his knees and commenced to
search carefully for slimy destroyers. “Never
ask her to sing!” he mumbled furiously to a
sleeping rose-bush. “Why the blazes should
I ask her to sing? Who told her she could
sing? Wish to Heaven she could find an-
other musical maniac like that Storpor
bounder. I swear I'm not going to encour-
age her to kick up a row!” He went on with
his search, and was feeling happy over
several fat Captures, when loud, defiant
music was heard within, and robust but un-
interesting cries and whoops troubled the
dreaming night.
“She thinks she’s Melba!” soliloquised the
vindictive shadow with the lantern.
* * » *
Three evenings later, the snail-hunter and 1
his bosom friend communed together in a
homeward-bound train. The snail-hunter
was desperate. “She keeps on bothering nue
about her infernal singing—or what she
calls singing. Wants to know why I don’t
ask her to sing and hang over the d —d
piano. That bounder Storpor, who went to
England some months ago, used to talk
about her voice and flatter her until she was
nearly off her head and ready to play his
accompaniments until the piano fell to
pieces under her hands. Now dashed if she
doesn’t want me to do the same thing!”
The bosom friend pawed bis grey mous-
tache and nodded sympathetically. “They’re
like that,” he remarked. Then, suddenly re-
collecting : “You used to sing a bit once,
usen’t you, Dredglin?”
Dredglin started guiltily. “Yes, but hang
it all, Branger, that was 15 years ago. I
never could sing much. I only did it be-
cause I couldn’t stand sitting round at those
confounded evenings we used to go to.”
Branger slapped his knee. “That’s the
whole trouble! Fifteen years ago there
were no gramophones, and we had to amuse
one another as best we could. Every dashed
girl and fellow who had any sort of voice

Give Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy
as soon as the child becomes hoarse,
or even after the croupy cough
appears. It will prevent the attack.

thank Heaven, we can turn on Melba, or
Harry Lauder, or a crack band, and nobody
has to stop talking-or say ‘Thank you tor
rotten entertainment.”
Dredglin’s eye lighted up. “That’s so, he
murmured.
“Now only the people who can perform
really well are allowed to sing or play. Its
all on account of the high standard set by
the machine. People daren t pit themselves
against it.”
“Just so, Branger. But there are some
who can't perform still kicking up a row in
front of pianos. I refer, .of course, to the
women of our time who were taught as girls
that they had to have some sort of accom-
plishments in order to make a show in
drawing-rooms. They can’t get out of the
old habit; nothing short of death will get
them out of it.”
“But they have a rough time in society,
Dredglin! People simply won’t listen to the
sort of noises we put up with.”
“Yes, but the sort of women I refer to let
themselves loose in the home where there is
no one to stop them. Go for a walk any
night and listen to the criminal noises you
hear issuing from every third villa. Come
round and listen outside my house.”
Branger became suddenly confused, and
tried unavailingly to get out with, “Oh, I
wasn’t referring to Mrs. Dredglin, you
know !”
“/ was !” said her husband angrily. “That
woman wants me to take the place of the
Storpor lunatic and help to keep up the
illusion that she is a singer, worth listening
to. But I won’t do it! I’m not going to
encourage her! D—n it all, she can no
more sing than I can! Why the blazes
can’t she realise that, like a sensible woman,
and stop bothering me, eh? Just imagine
how she would perform if I lost my temper
because she didn’t ask me to make an ass of
myself!”
The train rushed into Branger’s station
and he rose to go. “You might try it on
next time, Dredglin. Good-night.” He
carefully gathered up his parcel of new
gramophone records and stumbled out.
The train resumed its journey, bearing in
its inmost recesses a snail-hunter immersed
in thought.
Dinner had just concluded. Dredglin, a
dangerous gleam in his eye, saw that his
cigar was well alight, and remarked in a
carefully careless way to his stout and un-
suspecting wife, “Now, Sybil,, my dear, sup-
pose we have a little music?”
Obviously it shocked her —but in the same
pleasant way that an unexpected legacy
would have done. “Why, of course, dear.’
she cooed, and, Vising almost in haste, swept
with a happy rustle towards the drawing-
room. Her husband followed to light the
gas and perform other small duties of the
kind. There was a pleasant bustle sorting
out the songs, opening the piano, and ar-
ranging the other small preliminaries of the
uproar. The dinner had been good, the cigar
was better, and he was in a softer mood
than usual. Iler pleasant, pillowy shoulders,
viewed from the back, called up memories’
of the dear, dead days of long ago, when he
had wooed her, and. of his own free will,
incited her to sing. His thoughts were
running easily along pleasant lines when,
after her third song, she wheeled round on
the music-stool and commanded him to find
his own autographed copy of “Queen of the
Earth” and come and sing it to her. Like
a man in a daze, he hunted through the port-
folios until he found the tattered relic of
many misspent nights. He gave it into her
hands, and she placed it reverently on the
piano rack, while he cleared his throat and
prepared to shock her.
The showy introduction was played with
great spirit. Then he took a deep breath
and commenced, and the night shrank back
from the wild, raw sounds that came out of
him. The lady at the piano winced a
little, but loyally kept going in a way that
warmed his heart. As he got used to the
strange sounds, it seemed to him that he
really was not doing so badly; a few bars
further on he was singing earnestly and
feeling a rart of reverence for himself; his
voice was a bit rusty, certainly, but it gave
him a lot of pleasure listening to it, and his
accompanist was keeping on as if she was
satisfied and fully expected him to go right
through. He forgot his original base pur-
pose in his enthusiasm, and boomed away
with all his soul. The applause she gave
him at the end set him hunting feverishly
for other tattered relics. He found them
without much difficulty, and together they
kept it up till a late hour. As the stout
lady sentimentally remarked when the piano
was at rest, “it was quite like old times.”
* * * *
Next evening, Dredglin, the re-discovered
singer, and Branger, the gramophone en-
thusiast, travelled homeward together and
got along very pleasantly until Branger said
in an offhand way, “I’ve got the ‘Bedouin
Love Song’ here —you know, the thing
Storpor used to murder up at your place.”
“He was a rotten singer!” chattered
Dredglin viciously.
“He’s worse than rotten now,” grimly
added the gramophonist.
Dredalin pricked up his ears. “How .do
you know? Have you heard him lately?”
“I heard him as I was passing your house
last night.”
Branger didn’t notice his friend’s convul-
sive start as he got up to leave the car-
riage. “Good-night,” he grunted, and stepped
out, never suspecting that he had lost a
friend.
Dredglin fumed home, burst in the open
door, and flung his stick into the hallstand
with a rattle. Then he went in search of
his wife, found her reading, and, without any
tender preliminaries, inquired angrily, “Am
I. or am I not, a worse singer than that
d —d Storpor?”
Flustered Mrs. Dredglin very naturally
tried to temporise. “Well, tvhat a question
to ask! What makes you?”
“Please answer me !”
The commanding tone shocked and an-
gered the stout lady. She ruffled her fea-
thers threateningly, put down her book and
smiled unpleasantly. “I know you never
liked him, but Mr. Storpor was considered
a very good singer.”
“That is to say that I am not a good
simrer?” ... , . ,
The lady shrugged her shoulders and took
up her book again. “I didn t say anything
of the kind !”
Her husband’s tone was ferocious
retorted, “No, but you inferred as much !
Down came the book with a bang, and a
very wild and flushed lady rose to her feet.
“I don’t know why you are bothering me
with such rude and absurd questions, but
surely you do not put yourself on the same
level as Mr. S torpor? Why. he used to sing
beautifully /”
Her husband said something shocking, and
rushed from the room muttering, “That fat
bounder! If i couldn’t sing better than he
does, I’d cut my throat!”
There was no more “Queen of the Barth
after dinner. But the snail lantern danced
again in the garden, and when crashing
chords and wildly defiant female shouts
troubled the evening air, the seeking shadow
whispered vindictively, “She thinks she s
Melba!”
KODAK.
The Backing of the Winner.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Maori pushed his way to the rails
and critically surveyed the horses
doing their “preliminary.” A faint
smell of man and dry shark accompanied
him. lie breathed like a pit lied bullock,
and his stomach rose in heavy surges that
threatened to break its moorings of red
waistcoat.
“What te name that horse; number
seven?” he wheezed, excitedly. “You tin-
kum belong Maori or Pakeha.” The race
card supplied the information, “Mr. Tai
Eruiti’s br. g. Tetika.”
Hori’s stomach took on a new lease of
life, and bumped forty to the minute when
he had swallowed the information.
“Tetika! Tetika! Belongum te Maori,”
he kept muttering to himself. “Py corry,
ehoa! that te good horse. He get te run
dam’ quick. I tink he te feller for te win,
eh ?”
Most assuredly I did not. As a matter of
fact, I had it from a chap, who knew the
cousin of one of the girls a “jock” was
partly engaged to, that the steeplechase was
a cert, for Goldlight. This Tetika animal
was an outsider with about as much jump
in him as a common or field jumbuck—
which is saying a lot. However, it wasn’t
my game to give the Maori the “office,” and
beyond mentioning that the horse might
have a chance, did nothing to reduce the
dividend that Goldlight would ultimately
pay.
“But eh Tama,” commented Ilori, “look
at te way he run ; more bettern te Pakeha
horse, efery time. I back him for te quid—
yes, Taihoa! you see he win, for I te very
lucky feller,” and the surging piece of
humanity bumped its way through the
crowd around the Tote barriers.
The starter had threatened, sworn, cajoled
and at last succeeded in getting the mounts
to face the starting machine. “Clank!”
went the lever, “swish !” hummed the ropes,
and eight horses loosed themselves to the
crowd’s accompaniment of “They’re off.”
Something cannoned into me, something
soft and flabby, and a whisky-laden voice
breathed : “I gettim on. One poun’ Tetika.
What te color te jock?”
Hori had returned. Ilis bleary red eyes
peered at the blue and silver jacket that
flashed in the sunlight a clear length behind
the rest of the field. He puffed and blew
like the exhaust of a motor. His eyes
bulged, and the red waistcoat heaved in
short, sharp jerks. The Tote ticket was
clenched tightly in his teeth, and looked like
the protruding tongue of a “hei tiki.”
“Py corry ! Look to Tetika. He te feller
plenty strong for te jump,” he breathed;
and as the horse leaped at the hurdle Hori
gave imaginary assistance with his right
arm and shoulder.
“Dammitall! ’oo yer ’ittin’?” demanded a
voice in the rear. “Oh ! it’s you, yer Maori
bug, is it? Done er doller on the bloomin’
’orse, an ’think yer own the ’ole show.
Bli’me, fer a match, I’d put a clotch on yer,
yer shark-eater.”
Hori scowled, felt in the recesses of a
hip pocket and produced a much flattened
packet of cigarettes, which he handed to his
verbal assailant.
“You my good frien’, eh? Never mind
te match, Taihoa! You” He broke
off suddenly as the line of horses became
visible at the far side of the course. “What
I tell you!” he shouted, as the silver and
blue went up a place. “I te very lucky tan-
gata. Kapai Tetika. You show ’em ! My
word, I tink you pay te heavy divvy, moren
ten pound.” All unconsciously he took an-
other chew at his ticket. “Na ! See ! What
you talk now for te Maori horse? He sleep
third, eh? Kapai Tetika,” he yelled.
“Oh, shut up!” called someone from the
crowd. “Wot th’ eller ->’er making all th’
noise about?” But Hori heard him not.
His jaws were working hard, and he made
fearful grimaces at the approaching horses;
“Kamate! Kamate!” he breathed, and
generally w’orked up an impromptu “haka.”
The crowd commenced to appreciate Hori.
At the last jump Tetika was lying third,
and from thereon the Maori lost pounds of
flesh in the rivers of perspiration the excit-
ing finish drew from him.
“Easjf, one hand, you win.” Ilis right
arm used an imaginary stick, and his boots
dug phantom spurs into the atmosphere.
“This time! Haeremai! Haeremai! Hu!
Hu te Tetika ! My cri’ he win. No ! he te
second ! Na ! I te feller for to pick ’em —
Tetika te winna,” he shouted. “Goldlight!
in a canter,” boomed the crowd. “Py corry!
I tell you Tetika. Ka kino te Pakeha
horse. Na ! Yes! Tetika w-i-n-s /” and the
crowd confirmed it with, “The outsider, by
a whisker.”
“Ae,” said the Maori. “What you tink
now, eh? My missus be very happy to me.”
His face was greasy, and the white collar
about his neck hung limp as a rag. He
wagged his head, and grinned like a girl
with a new hatpin.
“I gif you te poun for my luck, eli? What
you tink te divvy be, ehoa ?”
I made a calculation as he felt in bis
pockets; then suddenly his jaw dropped, and
he became limp, very limp.
“I’y Cri!” he groaned, “I te Tangita
kino! I smaller te plurry ticket .”
